Received 
      21st November 2020
    First published on 25th August 2021AbstractOxide perovskites have emerged as an important class of materials with important applications in many technological areas, particularly thermocatalysis, electrocatalysis, photocatalysis, and energy storage. However, their implementation faces numerous challenges that are familiar to the chemist and materials scientist. The present work surveys the state-of-the-art by integrating these two viewpoints, focusing on the critical role that defect engineering plays in the design, fabrication, modification, and application of these materials. An extensive review of experimental and simulation studies of the synthesis and performance of oxide perovskites and devices containing these materials is coupled with exposition of the fundamental and applied aspects of defect equilibria. The aim of this approach is to elucidate how these issues can be integrated in order to shed light on the interpretation of the data and what trajectories are suggested by them. This critical examination has revealed a number of areas in which the review can provide a greater understanding. These include considerations of (1) the nature and formation of solid solutions, (2) site filling and stoichiometry, (3) the rationale for the design of defective oxide perovskites, and (4) the complex mechanisms of charge compensation and charge transfer. The review concludes with some proposed strategies to address the challenges in the future development of oxide perovskites and their applications.
Hamidreza Arandiyan
Hamidreza Arandiyan received his PhD from Tsinghua University in 2014. He was awarded a Vice-Chancellor's Research Fellowship in the School of Chemical Engineering from UNSW Sydney in 2015. He was awarded a University of Sydney Senior Fellowship in the School of Chemistry in 2018. Currently, he is a Senior Research Fellow in the Applied Chemistry & Environmental Science at RMIT University, Melbourne. His research interests lie in heterogeneous catalysis for environmental remediation and energy applications.
Sajjad S. Mofarah
Sajjad S. Mofarah obtained his PhD in Materials Science and Engineering, UNSW Sydney, in 2020, with a focus on the defect structures of functional oxide materials for energy and environmental applications. He was appointed as a Research Associate at the same institution in 2019, expanding his work on advanced characterisation of 2D heterojunction materials based on ultrathin metal-based coordination polymers for catalysis and energy storage. Since April 2020, he has been a Research Associate studying the cyclic reprocessing of battery materials. His research interests include layered oxides, defect creation and characterisation, batteries and supercapacitors, and related thermodynamics.

He obtained his BSc degrees in Ceramic Engineering (1977) and Chemistry (1980) from the University of Missouri-Rolla, his MSc degree in Ceramic Science (1980) from the Pennsylvania State University, and his PhD degree in Ceramic Engineering (1987) from the University of New South Wales. His principal research foci are ceramic processing and phase equilibria, which have been applied across many areas. At present, his main work is on catalysis, biomaterials, geopolymers, and waste utilisation.

1. Introduction
Perovskite was initially identified as a mineral of the chemical formula CaTiO3 in 1839 by the Russian mineralogist Gustavus Rose (1798–1873). It was named in honour of the Russian mineralogist Lev Alekseyevich von Perovski (1792–1856).1 Since the first publications on perovskites in 19112 and the upswing in publications in the 1960s, the term perovskite has been used for a large range of complexes that are defined in terms of the molecular formula ABX3, where A and B are cations and X is an anion,3,4 as shown in Fig. 1.
 Fig. 1  Crystal structure of a cubic ABX3 perovskite. 
In the ABX3 structure, A is a larger cation that forms a cubic sublattice and B is a body-centred cubic smaller cation. The ideal cubic perovskite structure has the A cation in 12-fold octahedral coordination and B in 6-fold cubic coordination. X generally is oxygen, but it can also be a halogen (F, Cl, Br, I), B, C, N, P, or S. This variety of potential ionic candidates facilitates the existence of a large number of perovskite structures, the number of which has significantly increased from partial substitution at both A and B sites.3Fig. 2 illustrates this flexibility, incorporating the basic concepts of cation and anion substitutional solid solution formation by acceptor, donor, and neutral dopants plus the resultant charge compensation through redox, ionic, and electronic mechanisms.
 Fig. 2  Conceptual summary of mechanisms to modify physicochemical properties through defect engineering (ICC = ionic charge compensation, ECC = electronic charge compensation, redox = redox charge compensation). 
This range of compositions gives rise to an equally broad range of chemicophysical properties, including ferroelectricity, ferromagnetism, semiconductivity, superconductivity, thermal conductivity, photovoltaic properties, and catalytic properties.5 Perovskites have particularly important applications as heterogeneous catalysts for many catalytic reactions, including gas–solid reactions (e.g., CO oxidation, CO2 reduction, volatile organic compounds (VOCs) conversion) and liquid–solid based reactions (e.g., photodegradation of organic pollutants, photocatalytic water splitting), owing to their versatile advantages including excellent oxygen mobility, thermal stability, oxygen vacancies, electron mobility, redox behaviour, mixed ion and electron conduction, surface/bulk oxygen evolution, and energy band structure.6 Regarding the energy storage field, perovskites are broadly used as anodes in air–metal batteries, fuel cells, and supercapacitors.7 The realisation of specific catalytic functions from perovskites depends on the design of specific surface sites. Kröger and Vink8 first formalised the standard method for the description of lattice defects in defective solids, which are more catalytically active than their defect-free counterparts. However, this approach has the limitation that it treats solids as homogeneous, but it is known that the surfaces of materials have higher activities than the bulk.9
The purpose of the present work is to provide a comprehensive overview of the different types of structural defects in perovskites, as summarised schematically in Fig. 3. The text considers the design and engineering of defects to generate properties applicable to catalysis and energy storage, and it canvasses an extensive range of recent experimental and modelling work on the resultant properties. The present work goes beyond the boundaries of current recent and older reviews in that it provides a state-of-the-art assessment of both fundamental and applied characteristics of perovskites for catalysis (electrocatalysis, thermocatalysis, photocatalysis) and energy conversion and storage applications. The present work aims to integrate these fundamental and applied aspects of perovskites from the perspectives of chemistry, chemical engineering, and materials science. It should be of particular interest to researchers as an introduction to defects and their impacts on the properties and performance of all materials, using perovskites as a model.
 Fig. 3  Schematic illustration of defective perovskites for catalysis and energy storage. 
2. Perovskite catalysts
2.1. Crystalline perovskite structures
The control of the architecture and dimensionality of perovskites, which can be varied from 0D (quantum dots) to 3D (bulk), necessitates a comprehensive understanding of cation sizes and valences, crystal structures, defect chemistry, and stoichiometries. As suggested, the term perovskite is used for any inorganic or hybrid inorganic/organic compound with the stoichiometry ABX3. The cation valence permutations include the range from A1+B5+X32− to A5+B1+X32−, where X is oxygen for oxide perovskites. However, halide perovskites are limited to A1+B2+X3, where X is a halide. Partial substitution at both A and B sites by other cations is pervasive as this allows compositional and property tuning.10 When no replacement is attempted, these are known as single perovskites and, when substitution for A, B, or both A and B is attempted, these are known as double perovskites.11 The most common valence combination for single oxide perovskites is A3+B3+O3, and the most common variant of double perovskites is A(BB′)O3 or A2+(B3+,B5+)O3, although (AA′)BO3 or (A3+,A2+)B3+O3 perovskites are also known.12 More broadly, of the 2346 possible single oxide perovskites, only ∼265 have been synthesised and reported.12 However, when the 1536 possible halide and other feasible types of A1+B2+X3 perovskites are considered, the total number of potential single perovskites rises to 3882.
The idealised perovskite exhibits a cubic Bravais lattice of space group Pm3m.13 As shown in Fig. 4a, the structure consists of a central A ion located within a cubic B sublattice, where each B cation is coordinated by six octahedral X ions.14 As the unit cell is defined by the B cubic sublattice, the B–X distance is one half the unit cell. Fig. 4b and c illustrates the individual layers within the structure, which are layered in a BAB sequence in the (001) direction, where the B layer is BO2 and the A layer is AO. An alternative setting for this structure is suggested by Fig. 4d, which shows a (111) projection that reveals an AO3 layer normal to this orientation. The stacking of these layers, their close interatomic proximity, and the higher-order hexagonal symmetry enable shear of this plane. As a result, it is possible to form polytypes comprised of differently layered sequences of BO6 octahedra in hexagonal (H), rhombohedral (R), and cubic (C) symmetries.15 When the BO6 octahedra share only opposite faces, the stacking sequence is H; when they share only corners, the stacking sequence is C; and when faces and corners are shared, the stacking sequence is R or a mixture of H and C. The most commonly observed polytypes are 6H (e.g., BaCrO3), 2H (e.g., BaMnO3), and 9R (e.g., BaIrO3).16–18
 Fig. 4  (a) Perspective view of the 3D perovskite structure, (b) BO6 layer (purple plane in (a)), (c) AO layer (orange plane in (a)), and (d) (111) projection of the perovskite structure. 
The idealised perovskite has a Goldschmidt tolerance factor (t) of 1.19 However, distorted perovskites with values outside of the range 0.7 ≤ t ≤ 1.0 have been observed.20 In these cases, the mismatched ionic radii result in distortion of the BO6 octahedra, which facilitates phase transformation to another polymorph.20 The origin of these phase transformations is the existence of an A/B ratio smaller than that of the idealised perovskite structure, as in the case of SrTiO3, which causes the BO6 to tilt to accommodate the structural contraction in the orthogonal directions by optimising the nearest-neighbour bonding with A (viz., A–X).21 This ready deformability has resulted in perovskite materials being considered to be soft ionic structures.22 The twenty-three possible variants of the discrete extents of tilt cited by Glazer21 are condensed to sixteen according to symmetry. In subsequent works by Shojaei23 and Yin,12 these sixteen variants, with some differences, were reduced to fifteen variants to characterise the permutations for eighteen alkali halides. However, in reality, 90% of such perovskites exhibit only four types of Glazer tilt, these being of the space groups Pnma (69%), R3c (14%), Pm3m (12%), and I4/mcm (5%).12 Elimination of equivalent space groups reduces the total to fourteen permutations, as given in Table 1.

2.2. Dimensionality of perovskites
Deviation in the dimensionality of the idealised 3D structure of perovskites is achieved by extending the concept of the tilting of the BO6 octahedra to the breakage of the B–O bonds binding the octahedra together. This results in either complete isolation or partial sharing of BO6, with cleavage of the 3D perovskite along one of its crystallographic planes. Consequently, the concept of soft perovskites also applies to the ease of bond manipulation. When this breakage is engineered, lower-dimensional structures of newly spatially arranged BO6 octahedra are formed.31 The resultant new bonding of the BO6 octahedra results in the conversion of a 3D structure (Fig. 5a) to establish three potential dimensionalities: (2D) – edge-shared octahedra, resulting in stratified configurations, i.e., nanosheets (Fig. 5b);32 (1D) – corner-, edge-, or face-shared octahedra, resulting in linear and zigzag configurations, i.e., nanowires (Fig. 5c);33 and (0D) – isolated octahedra, without electron sharing, i.e., quantum dots (Fig. 5d).34 While perovskite quantum dots have been examined for applications involving optoelectronics and photovoltaics,35 the high surface/volume ratios of the other two low-dimensionality perovskite structures underpin their potential applications in catalysis and energy storage.
 Fig. 5  (a) Three-dimensional bulk perovskite oxide, (b) two-dimensional layered perovskite oxide, (c) one-dimensional perovskite oxide, and (d) zero-dimensional perovskite oxide. 

2.2.1. 3D perovskite structures. 
The infinite corner-sharing of the BX6 octahedra establishes an extensive network to form a bulk perovskite in 3D. The presence of cations in which the tolerance factor falls outside of the range 0.8 ≤ t ≤ 1 can result in the formation of lower dimensionalities. This unlimited corner-sharing results in the formation of direct band gaps (Egs) within the semiconducting range of 1–3 eV. As a result, the crystal structure plays a key role in the catalytic and electronic properties.36 The reason for this is that corner-sharing destabilises the unpaired electrons in the B cation orbitals, which allows band engineering of the conduction band (CB) and valence band (VB) through hybridisation of the respective metal d orbitals and oxygen p orbitals.37 The functionality of perovskites can be expanded through alteration of the corner-sharing by tilting of the BX6 octahedra, which can be initiated by, inter alia, substitution to introduce defects and atmosphere control. Low-dimensional (LD) perovskites are of considerable interest because of their tunable optical and electrical properties.38 These materials qualify as LD when at least one of the physical dimensional parameters is ≤100 nm.39 While 1D perovskites can be synthesised by self-assembly, 2D perovskites usually are synthesised by exfoliation of 3D structures.40 As oxide perovskites tend to exfoliate at A sites occupied by small BX6 interlayer cations, organic–inorganic perovskites exfoliate owing to the larger interlayer spacing resulting from the presence of large organic chains.41


2.2.2. 2D perovskite structures. 
State-of-the-art 2D nanosheets generally are synthesised using two main strategies, viz., top-down (exfoliation) and bottom-up (assembly).42 The latter approach involves oriented lateral growth with limited outward growth. This method suffers from low yields and constraints on applicability from procedural complexities.43 In contrast, the former strategy involves exfoliation of stratified/layered bulk structures. Perovskites with 2D layered structures are based on [An−1BnO3n+1]2− layers, where n is the number of layers and charge balance is achieved by the presence of one or two interlayer metal (M, M2, or M2O2) cations. The interlayer cations generally are exchanged with protons in acidic solutions to form H[An−1BnO3n+1] and H2[An−1BnO3n+1].44 This facilitates the cleavage necessary to exfoliate the stratified structures and create 2D structures with tailored thicknesses. These bulk layered perovskites generally are one of three types, including Ruddlesden–Popper, Dion–Jacobson, and Aurivillius phases, as shown in Fig. 6. While the configurations of the layers of the Ruddlesden–Popper and Aurivillius phases are similar,45 Dion–Jacobson phases have half as many interlayer cations. The high surface areas, high densities of active sites, and outstanding stability arising from their low ionic diffusivity and good resistance to water penetration highlight the potential of these 2D perovskites in energy and environmental applications.46 The orthogonal preferred cleavage planes in 3D perovskites lead to the formation of (100)-, (110)-, or (111)-oriented layered perovskites.45
 Fig. 6  Schematics of principal bulk layered perovskite structures. 
2.2.2.1. Ruddlesden–Popper phases. 
The Ruddlesden–Popper phases have the general formula M2[An−1BnO3n+1], where M and A typically are alkali, alkaline earth, or rare earth metals (where the two M ions are interlayer exchangeable cations); and B is a transition or post-transition metal; and n ≥ 1.47 When high-temperature processing is used, phases with n > 3 generally are unstable.48 However, room-temperature precipitation from solution can yield phases with n = 5, low-temperature layer-by-layer methods can produce phases of n = 6, and epitaxial films can result in phases with n as high as 10. The first such phases, Sr2TiO4, Ca2MnO4, and (LaSr)AlO4, were synthesised in 1957.47Fig. 6 shows a representation of the 2D layered architecture of Ruddlesden–Popper phases, which are of the K2NiF4 structure group, with a tetragonal Bravais lattice and space group I4/mmm.49 As indicated above, the interlayer A site, with coordination number 9, is occupied by an alkali, alkaline earth, or rare earth element, while the B-site, at the centre of the octahedra and so of coordination number 6, is occupied by transition or post-transition metals.50 There may also be two M ions that are interlayer exchangeable cations in the form of alkali, alkaline earth, or rare earth. In this case, the formula M2A2B3O10 corresponds to a type of Ruddlesden–Popper phase with n = 3. This is characterised by the original perovskite block inserted into the rock salt layer, where the larger cation (M+) is located, such as the structures Na2Nd2Ti3O10,51 A2Ln2Ti3O10 (A = K, Rb; Ln = La),52 and MLa2Ti2TaO10 (M = Cs, Rb).53 The Ruddlesden–Popper phases can be exfoliated to form 2D structures in a number of ways, the most common of which is ion exchange using a proton in order to lower the energy requirement for cleavage,54,55 as shown in Fig. 7. Fukuda et al.56 reported a multiple-step fabrication process containing ion exchange of Cs in Cs6+xW11O36 with intercalated H+, swelling the resultant H2.10Cs3.90W11O36·6H2O through intercalation of the strong organic base tetra(n-butyl)ammonium hydroxide (TBA+OH−), and applying mechanical stress by intermittent shaking for 10 days. The rationale for using TBA+OH− is its ability to cause a 50-fold increase in interlayer spacing.57 This work was inspired by an earlier effort by Ebina et al.,58 where HCa2Nb3O10·1.5H2O was intercalated with TBA+OH−. While protonated Ruddlesden–Popper phases would be expected to preclude intercalation and exfoliation owing to the small resultant interlayer spacing, the success of these preliminary studies suggests that another factor is relevant to exfoliation, which is the chemistry of the intercalating ion and its ability to reduce the activation energy for intercalation.54,55 The means by which this is achieved is neutralisation of the acidic protons by the strong organic base. This has been confirmed by the work of Schaak et al.,59 who used TBA+OH− to tailor the exfoliation of the Ruddlesden–Popper tantalates and titanotantalates H2[An−1BnO3n+1] (A = Na, Ca, Sr, La; B = Ta, Ti) into atomically thin single and thicker multiple layers. Fig. 7 illustrates the basic principles behind the self-assembly of perovskite nanosheets, and the process can be refined to generate more sophisticated structures. For example, Liu et al.60 reported the layer-by-layer assembly of 2D Ruddlesden–Popper nanosheets consisting of alternating C8H17NH3-capped CsPb2Br7 nanosheets, which were then converted into layered (C8H17NH3)2CsPb2Br7 superlattice nanocrystals. Further, they demonstrated that the exfoliation of these 2D nanostructures was reversible by exfoliating the superlattice nanocrystals back into monolayer building blocks by sonication in toluene. Since most transition-metal-based Ruddlesden–Popper phases are not ion-exchangeable, probably owing to the high electronegativities of the transition metals, these phases remain in the form of 3D bulk layered structures. However, the existing interlayer gaps facilitate charge carrier intercalation, which makes these materials suitable as anodes for Li- and Na-ion batteries.61
 Fig. 7  Schematic of the mechanism of exfoliation through ion exchange and reassembly of bulk layered perovskites. 
2.2.2.2. Dion–Jacobson phases. 
Another family of perovskite compounds is the Dion–Jacobson phases, which have the general formula M[An−1BnO3n+1], where M and A typically are an alkali or alkaline earth ion (where the single M is an interlayer exchangeable cation) and B is a transition or post-transition metal.62,63 For instance, M[Ca2Nb3O10], which is of the K2NiF4 structure type and where M is Li, Na, K, Rb, Cs, NH4, or Ti, was synthesised by Dion et al.62 Subsequently, Jacobson et al.22 used ion exchange to convert K[Ca2Nb3O10] to H[Ca2Nb3O10] in order to vary the layer thickness. The resultant low interlayer charge density allows these acid-exchanged Dion–Jacobson phases to be exfoliated easily to form monolayer colloids.64 The structures consist of triple perovskite sheets interleaved with M sheets. Depending on the nature of the M ion, the relative displacements of adjacent triple perovskite sheets, which are parallel to (001), are O, b/4, or (a + b)/4.22 In common with the Ruddlesden–Popper phases, Dion–Jacobson layered perovskites, such as acid-exchanged HCa2Nb3O10 and HLa2Ti2NbO10, exfoliate into nanoscale colloidal sheets when reacted with a bulky organic base.59 This is a reflection of the fundamental acid–base chemistry of these layered solids. Of the layered transition metal oxides, titanates are less acidic than both niobates and tantalates, which is reflected in the periodic electronegativity trends.65 This suggests that Group 6 cations are more acidic than those of Groups 4 and 5, and so the former would be exfoliated more readily.66
One study of note reported a rapid and simplified strategy for the simultaneous exfoliation of Dion–Jacobson perovskites and surface modification of the resultant nanosheets using a fast microwave-assisted reaction.67 This work involved the simultaneous synthesis of the bulk layered CsLnNb2O7 (Ln = La, Pr) perovskite and surface modification by tetra(n-butyl)ammonium (TBA+), followed by protonation to form HLnNb2O7 nanosheets. The TBA+ surface groups were then replaced with different n-alkyl alkoxy and n-alkylammonium groups by microwave reaction (≤1 h). This work exposed the potential for surface modification of perovskite nanosheets with a wide range of organic ligands containing hydroxyl or amine functional groups. Another study of note reported the synthesis of CsBa2Ta3O10 and its exfoliation to nitride Ba2Ta3O10 using a nitridation–protonation–intercalation–exfoliation process.68 This allowed the engineering of lateral sizes ranging from several hundred nanometres to a few micrometres and heights of ∼2 nm. The band alignment indicated that these materials would be suitable for visible-light-driven photocatalytic water splitting. Interestingly, CsBa2Ta3O10 exhibited two different Ta–O bond lengths, which effectively stretched the TaO6 octahedron. The flexibility of these layered perovskites can be exploited readily using thin-film techniques, such as laser-deposition or molecular beam epitaxy, which allow the synthesis of multiple-layer structures. In contrast with high-vacuum techniques, ultrathin films from suspensions can be controllably restacked by Langmuir–Blodgett (LB) deposition.69 For example, Li et al.70 used LB deposition to synthesise ultrathin films of the Dion–Jacobson phase Ca2Nam−3NbmO3m+1, where the number of layers (m) varied in the range 3–6, as shown in Fig. 8a. The atomic force microscopy (AFM) results, shown in Fig. 8b–e, reveal the 2D morphology with respective average thicknesses of 1.85, 2.30, 2.74, and 3.20 nm. The high dielectric constants (k) of these ultrathin-film ferroelectric perovskites suggest their suitability for efficient energy storage. In another study, a colloidal suspension of the Dion–Jacobson phase KCa2Nb3O10 was initially exfoliated into 2D nanosheets of Ca2Nb3O10 and then self-assembled into ultrathin films of different numbers of layers (m) and corresponding thicknesses, where m = 5 (7.5 nm), m = 10 (15 nm), and m = 15 (22.5 nm).69
 Fig. 8  (a) Schematics of Ca2(NbmNam−3)O3m+1 nanosheets (m = 3–6) and (b–e) AFM images of individual nanosheets of (b) Ca2Nb3O10, (c) Ca2NaNb4O13, (d) Ca2Na2Nb5O16, and (e) Ca2Na3Nb6O19 (reproduced from ref. 70 with permission from the American Chemical Society). 
2.2.2.3. Aurivillius phases. 
The face-centred orthorhombic Aurivillius phases have the general formula [Bi2O2]2+[An−1BnO3n+1)]2−, where A is an alkali or alkaline earth ion or a post-transition metal ion and B is a transition metal.71 In these structures, the A sites are 8–12 coordinated with oxygen and the B sites occupy two octahedral nonequivalent positions.72 These structures have highly anisotropic 2D structures that link Bi2O2 layers with perovskite BO6 layers by weak van der Waals bonding. A promising Aurivillius phase is Bi2MoO6, where the fluorite-type Bi2O22+ block and the MoO42− perovskite block arrangement exhibits high electron and ionic conductivity, which makes this material attractive for catalysis and energy storage applications.73 The layered Aurivillius phase [Bi2O2]2+[W2O7]2− has been protonated by selective acid leaching (3 days) to form H2W2O7.66 An alternative to protonation is Li+ intercalation, which has been applied to the Aurivillius-type layered perovskite Bi4Ti3O12 to form Li2Bi4Ti3O12, which was exfoliated upon Li removal by intercalation of H2O.74 This technology has the advantage of avoiding the use of acidic media for protonation. This exfoliation approach has been applied to the optoelectronic materials WS2 and MoS2.75 The preceding materials represent single-layered perovskites in that the structures contain a single perovskite block. However, there is a group of more complex double-layered perovskites, one of which has the general formula M′[(La, Sr)(Nb2−xMx2+)O9], where interlayer M′ is Na1+ or Cs1+, M2+ is a transition metal ion with two valences (Co, Ni, Cu), and [(La, Sr)(Nb2−xMx2+)O9]1− is the double perovskite block.76 The partial substitution of Nb5+ by the M2+ ion at the B-site is responsible for the formation of oxygen vacancies, which act as active sites for catalysis for metal–air batteries. Another notable feature of these structures is that, when the large ion Cs (0.174 nm) is the interlayer M′, a 2D structure is formed, while in the presence of small Na (0.102 nm) as the interlayer M′, the cubic 3D structure is retained. The thickness of the nanosheets of CsLaSrNb2(M2+)O9 can be tailored through the use of different intercalating ions. The role of the ionic radii of these intercalating ions in the cleavage of the 3D structure into 2D has been investigated by Uma et al.77 where CsCa2Nb2AlO9 formed a layered 2D structure with proton intercalation and ion-exchange between the anionic slabs. In contrast, intercalation of K+ followed by protonation failed to exfoliate owing to suppression of proton diffusion and ion-exchange, thereby resulting in the retention of a 3D bulk structure.


2.2.3. 1D perovskite structures. 
Strategies for the preparation of 1D structures as nanowires generally rely on control of the growth kinetics, often by the use of mineralisers for the enhancement of growth in a single direction78 or by the use of a capping agent to constrain growth in the complementary directions.79 For example, Magrez et al.80 reported the growth of highly uniform single-crystal KNbO3 nanowires of aspect ratio ∼100 and diameter ∼60 nm with the assistance of the KOH mineraliser during hydrothermal synthesis. The structure was orthorhombic, with the (110) nanowire growth direction. Mao et al.81 obtained high yields of single-crystal nanowires of BaTiO3 and SrTiO3 of aspect ratio >25 and ∼65 nm diameter. The fabrication method was described as a solid-state reaction at 820 °C in the presence of NaCl and a nonionic surfactant (nonylphenyl ether), which resulted in the directed growth of the perovskites in the (100) direction. However, other techniques, such as vapour–liquid–solid (VLS) growth82 and soft chemistry,83 have also been reported. Further, synthesis can be done through self-assembly of nanoparticles to yield self-assembled 1D polycrystalline perovskites. A more direct method to fabricate 1D nanofibres is sol–gel electrospinning. George et al.84 synthesised highly crystalline SrMnO3 perovskite nanofibres by this technique, followed by calcination at different temperatures. The nanofibres were polycrystalline and porous, with a randomly oriented grain size of ∼30 nm. The pore and grain sizes could be altered by changing the calcination temperature, which had a significant effect on the electrochemical properties of the nanofibres, which were developed for application as an electrode material for supercapacitors. The electrochemical capacitance reached 321.7 F g−1 at a discharge current density of 0.5 A g−1, retaining 87% after 5000 cycles. The full-cell supercapacitor exhibited energy and power densities reaching 37.3 W h kg−1 and 400 W kg−1, respectively. Ma et al.85 used the same electrospinning technique to prepare (La1−xSrx)(Mn0.9Ni0.1)O3 nanofibres in order to examine the effects of oxygen vacancies on the electrochemical performance. This work revealed an optimal performance window as a function of increasing Sr content in which corresponding oxygen vacancy formation increased the specific capacitance, after which lattice distortion decreased it. The maximal specific capacitance of 113.9 F g−1 corresponded to the composition (La0.85Sr0.15)(Mn0.9Ni0.1)O3.


2.2.4. 0D perovskite structures. 
The size of semiconductor crystallites strongly affects the electronic and optical properties of these 0D materials. This is an effect of the natural length scale of the electron–hole pair, which is known as the exciton Bohr radius (rBohr).86 The spatial confinement of the electron and hole wavefunctions, i.e., quantum confinement, results in quantisation of the electronic band structure into discrete energy levels.87 The key outcomes of this are that the catalytic activity increases significantly despite a significant simultaneous increase in the Eg.88 This equates to performance commensurate with the surface rather than the bulk. Semiconductor materials are classified into different categories, depending on confinement in other spatial directions:89 3D – confinement in no dimensions; 2D – confinement in one dimension; 1D – confinement in two dimensions; 0D – confinement in all three dimensions. The existing shortcomings in bulk layered perovskites, such as their lacklustre stability, can be addressed by lower-dimensional perovskite derivatives. Several methods have been developed to synthesise 0D perovskites, including hot-injection colloidal and solvent-based synthesis methods, which are based on three-dimensional isolation of [BX6]4− octahedra from the inorganic A-cation.34 Although these 0D materials can have a physical dimension of several millimetres or larger, similar to bulk materials, they act as bulk quantum materials owing to their intrinsic confined nature.90 The most extensive work has been done on the 0D inorganic perovskite Cs4PbBr6. These 0D materials exhibit unique properties of incorporated organic molecules and inorganic semiconductor materials. These properties include intrinsic Pb2+ ion emission, large exciton binding energy, and a small amount of polaron formation upon photoexcitation. They also exhibit anomalous green photoluminescence with improved stability and high quantum yield, which arise from the localisation of carriers on the [BX6]4− cluster and the formation of defect-assisted emission.91

2.3. Heterojunction nanostructures
The fabrication of heterostructures can generate new electronic states at the interfaces between phases, thereby offering the potential to create novel materials of unusual properties.92 Consequently, this approach represents an advanced strategy to modify the electronic band structure of oxide and other materials for energy and environmental applications. Heterojunctions can be viewed in terms of two nanostructural classifications: (1) intrinsic heterostructures, which are heterojunctions formed between (i) chemisorbed structural sheets in lattices and superlattices disrupted by planar dislocations, significant lattice mismatch, etc. or (ii) physisorbed immiscible solid solutions; and (2) extrinsic heterostructures, which are heterojunctions formed between two mutually physisorbed nanoparticles. One study of note of an intrinsic heterostructure reported a relatively simple method of forming a series of perovskite superlattices through the layer-by-layer assembly of the Dion–Jacobson phases LaNb2O7 and Ca2Nb3O10 as building blocks.93 It is not possible to create the resultant LaNb2O7/Ca2Nb3O10 as a 3D bulk structure. The resultant superlattice layers established ferromagnetic/antiferromagnetic coupling at the interface, which suggests potential applications in multiferroics.
Another study of note of an intrinsic heterostructure reported the synthesis of monolayers of anionic sheets derived from HCa2Nb3O10, HSr2Nb3O10, HCaLaNb2TiO10, and HLaNb2O7 grown on cationic substrates. Combinations of these phases were used to synthesise multilayer films with an interleaving organic polycation. This layer-by-layer chemical adsorption is a convenient way to fabricate thin films of materials of specific stacking sequences.64 A study of note of an extrinsic heterostructure reported the fabrication of 2D/2D heterojunction photocatalysts consisting of g-C3N4 and exfoliated ultrathin KCa2Nb3O10 nanosheets.94 Effective electron–hole charge separation deriving from the synergistic effects of nanosheet heterojunctions appeared to enhance the effectiveness of photocatalytic degradation of the refractory antibiotic tetracycline hydrochloride. A second study of note of an extrinsic 2D/1D heterostructure reported data for a p–n junction heterostructure combining p-type double perovskite (Ba, Pr)(Mn1.75Co0.25)O5+δ nanosheets with n-type nitrogen-doped multi-walled carbon nanotubes (NCNTs).95 Interestingly, the oxygen nonstoichiometry of nanosheets derived from the partial substitution of Ba2+ by Pr3+ at the A site and the charge-compensating creation of oxygen interstitials. This heterojunction exhibited significant enhancement in bifunctional oxygen electrocatalytic activity. The optimisation of the band alignment and the consequent charge transfer from the perovskite to NCNTs offers a new path to facilitate redox oxygen activation. A proof-of-concept rechargeable Zn–air battery with a cathode of 90 wt% perovskite + 10 wt% NCNT has demonstrated a specific discharge capacity of 789.2 mA h gzn−1 and cyclic stability for 85 h at a current density of 5 mA cm−2. While the preceding are examples of oxide–oxide heterojunctions, there are also many metal–oxide systems that have been investigated.96 Gao et al.97 prepared the perovskite composition La(Mn0.9Pt0.075Ni0.025)O3−δ as a catalyst for the oxygen reduction reaction (ORR) in alkaline solution. Pt3Ni nanoparticles were exsolved in situ on the surfaces upon reduction, thereby yielding the heterostructure. The catalytic activity was found to improve, which was attributed to the synergy between the host perovskite and nanoparticulate precipitates. Beyond these effects across interfaces, there are also other considerations that apply within and across grains, which are relevant to double perovskites and two mutually contacting perovskites. These involve the potential for electron exchange between materials, which can take place by several processes: (1) intrinsic ionisation – in the case of double perovskites, the electron exchange occurs between ions, which are intrinsic to the composition, at the B site, as in, e.g., Fe3+ + Co2+ → Fe2+ + Co3+; (2) intervalence charge transfer (IVCT)98–100 – in the case of two mutually contacting single perovskites, electron exchange occurs between a matrix ion of one perovskite and that of another perovskite, thus representing an extrinsic effect for both structures, as in LaMnO3 and SrNbO3, giving Mn3+ + Nb4+ → Mn2+ + Nb5+; and (3) multivalence charge transfer (MVCT)101,102 – in the case of two mutually contacting perovskites, one (or both) of which is a double perovskite, electron exchange occurs between multiple matrix ions of one perovskite and the ion(s) of another perovskite, again an extrinsic effect, as in SrMoO3 and (La0.6Sr0.4)(Co0.8Mn0.2)O3, giving Mo4+ + Co3+ + Mn3+ → Mo6+ + Co2+ + Mn2+.

Fig. 9a and b show the electronic band structure of a heterojunction between the single perovskites ABO3 and AB′O3.103 The electronic structure is altered at the interface owing to the mismatch in Fermi energies (Efs), thereby resulting in increased charge transfer by establishing a local potential (ΔΦ) across the interfacial region. In another work,104 density functional theory (DFT) simulation indicated that electron exchange occurred owing to differences in electronegativity of the B-site ions in the superlattice of Sr2VO4 and Sr2MnO4, which resulted in hole doping of the VO2 layer and electron doping of the MnO2 layer (Fig. 9c). However, in contrast with the authors’ explanation, an interpretation based on IVCT based on electronic charge compensation could be examined experimentally from X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) data. Table 2 summarises the range of single and double perovskites of different dimensionalities for catalysis and energy applications.

3. Defects
3.1. Classification of point defects
The different types of defects and their equilibria typically are expressed in terms of the Kröger–Vink notation.125,126 This notation indicates the type of solid solution (substitutional solid solubility (SSS) or interstitial solid solubility (ISS)), the lattice ions, the identities of defects, the locations of defects, valences of all species, and whether there is ionic charge compensation (ICC) or electronic charge compensation (ECC). More broadly, these defects are defined in terms of whether they involve only their own lattice species (viz., intrinsic defects) or they involve species that are not part of the base chemical system (viz., extrinsic defects). These features are incorporated in representative reactions that describe the defect equilibria, which must maintain mass and charge balance. A final point about these equilibria is that when ICC is involved, the stoichiometry matches that of the dopant; when ECC is involved, the stoichiometry matches that of the lattice. The common descriptors used in the Kröger–Vink notation are as follows:

: metal ion at a metal sublattice site (M), hence of no net charge (×)

: oxygen at an oxygen sublattice site (O), hence of no net charge (×)

: metal ion (acceptor dopant) substituting in the metal sublattice (M), with one net negative charge (′)

: metal ion interstitial (i), with one net positive charge (˙)

: substituted metal ion, replaced at its lattice site, that has diffused to the surface (S), hence of no net charge (×)

: metal (M) vacancy, with one net negative charge (′)

: oxygen (O) vacancy, with two net positive charges (˙˙)
e′: electron, with one net negative charge (′)
h˙: hole, with one net positive charge (˙)

Table 3 gives some examples of the relevant defect equilibria.

It is implicitly clear that intrinsic ionisation for a single structure is similar to IVCT and MVCT, although the latter two are extrinsic effects as they involve two structures. However, in addition to these sources of defects, there is another source of defects, i.e. F0, F+, or F++ colour centres, which are represented as , , or , respectively.127,128 These Kröger–Vink symbols represent oxygen vacancies with two, one, or no electrons, respectively. Colour centres have been identified in layered rubidium tungstate Rb4−xW11O35, which has a 2D bronze-type tunnel structure.129 This tungstate exfoliated into nanosheets (3 nm thickness and average lateral dimension of 400 nm) and intercalated with TBA cations. The highly efficient photochromic behaviour was attributed to the presence of colour centres, although it is possible that it also resulted from quantum confinement effects.
3.2. Point defects in perovskites
Although single perovskites do not involve substitutional solid solubility, this is the norm for double perovskites, which have isovalent cations at the A site but isovalent and/or heterovalent cations at the B site.130 The structural flexibility of these soft ionic perovskites allows their physicochemical, electronic, and other properties to be modified and tuned according to the nature and extent of substitution. Electron exchange by ionic and electronic charge compensation, IVCT and MVCT, and colour centre formation are sources of active sites that may be beneficial for catalytic and energy storage applications.131Fig. 10 shows the three principal solute ordering patterns that are observed in double perovskites; all of these can exhibit ordering at the A or B sites, which results in reduced symmetry.12 In the very common rock salt pattern, ordered B site substitution is more likely than A site ordering owing to the high strain between neighbouring A cations.132 The resultant ordering pattern exhibits alternating B and B′ patterns that interleave through the structure in 3D. This perovskite is defined as A2BB′X6. The driving force to form the rock salt pattern is governed by the valence difference (ΔV) between B and B′, where a random pattern results when ΔV ≤ 3 and the rock salt pattern results when 3 < ΔV ≤ 6; the layered pattern occurs when ΔV = 2.133 It has been suggested that Jahn–Teller distortion of either the BO6 or B′O6 octahedron favours the layered pattern.134 As indicated in eqn (1)–(17), it is possible to have defects on and within both cation and anion sublattices as vacancies and interstitials, respectively.
 Fig. 10  Schematics of cation ordering schemes in perovskites (reproduced from ref. 135 with permission from John Wiley & Sons). 

3.2.1. A Site deficiency. 
Eqn (1)–(4): A1−xBO3 structures can exhibit a wide range of stoichiometries, which makes them suitable as anode materials in Li-ion and Na-ion batteries.136 In the extreme, when the A site is entirely unoccupied, a new BX3 structure forms, which is a ReO3-type perovskite, where the B site cation alters valence from 3+ or 4+ to 6+.137 There are many BX3 perovskites of this structure, from WO3 to highly complex structures, such as CoPt(CN)6, where the bridging octahedral anion CN is polyatomic.138


3.2.2. B Site deficiency. 
Analogues of eqn (1)–(4): vacancies at the B-site (AB1−xO3) are energetically unfavourable unless there are compensating factors, such as B–B interactions.139 However, another class of perovskites is hexagonal, such as Ba3Re2O9 and Ba5Nb4O15, which enable B site vacancies.140


3.2.3. A or B interstitial. 
Eqn (12)–(15): for A1+xBO3 or AB1+xO3, although it is commonly assumed that substitutional solid solubility follows from stoichiometry compensation and equilibration, the fact that solute ordering at 50 mol% does not always occur demonstrates that some of the solute ions are located interstitially. This can have a significant effect on the defect equilibria.


3.2.4. O Site deficiency. 
Eqn (5): oxygen vacancies (ABO3−δ), which are well known as catalytically active sites,141,142 can be generated by substitutional solid solubility using what is effectively an acceptor dopant. In some cases, the oxygen vacancy concentration can be high enough to result in the ordering of these anion defects, which is the case in brownmillerite, CaFeO2.5. Although this oxygen vacancy concentration is 16.67 mol%, thus suggesting that the ordering is layered since this would involve one-sixth of the oxygen sites, ordered rows in the (110) direction have been reported for other perovskites.143


3.2.5. O Site interstitial. 
Eqn (16) and (17): in other cases (ABO3+δ), such as LaMnO3+δ, oxygen interstitials can form, where charge compensation probably occurs by La or Mn vacancy formation.144 However, it is also possible that this is affected by Mn valence increase. One of the techniques to achieve oxygen injection is by cleaving the perovskite on the (100) planes, which has been done for perovskites in the Sr–Ti–O and La–Ni–O systems.15

3.3. Classification of structural defects
Defects are classified according to several categories. As shown in the representative examples of Fig. 11, there are many types, including intrinsic and extrinsic. They may also be classified according to dimensions or locations. By dimension, they can be 0D, 1D, 2D, or 3D. By location, they may be external (viz., surface) defects145 or internal (viz., bulk) defects.146 While the 0D, 1D, and 2D defects are small and are considered to be atomic-scale defects or microdefects, the 3D defects are larger and so are considered to be macrodefects.
 Fig. 11  Schematic of representative types of intrinsic and extrinsic defects in perovskites. 

3.3.1. 0D point defects. 
Strictly speaking, 0D structural defects are point defects. In addition to the previously mentioned vacancies and interstitials, there are several other common types of defects:147
• Schottky pair: simultaneous formation of a cation site vacancy and charge-compensating anion site vacancies or an anion site vacancy and charge-compensating cation site vacancies.
• Frenkel defect: movement of a cation or an anion from its lattice site to an interstice, with no charge compensation requirement.
• Antisite defect: transposition of two lattice ions, including cation–cation and cation–anion, with no charge compensation requirement.


3.3.2. 1D linear defects. 
1D defects are known as linear defects or dislocations. They are well known as the sources of plastic deformation in metals, although they also occur commonly in ceramics.147 The two types of such defects are edge dislocations and screw dislocations, with both consisting of regions of lattice disregistry. An edge dislocation can be visualised as a partially inserted plane (thus causing compression) or removed plane (thus causing tension) of ions in the lattice. In contrast, a screw dislocation is equivalent to partial shearing of the lattice along a plane. The extent and direction of the disregistry in terms of the number of lattice spacings is known as the Burger's vector.


3.3.3. 2D planar defects. 
2D defects are known as planar defects or interfacial defects. There are many types of these, the principal types of which are as follows:148,149
• Surface: interface between solid/liquid, liquid/gas, or solid/gas.
• Phase boundary: interface between two materials of different chemistries and/or structures.
• Grain boundary: interface between two discrete grains, whether of the same or different materials.
• Low-angle grain boundary: interface between grains in which the angular mismatch is ≤15°, although this varies according to crystallography and lattice spacing; also known as subgrain boundary.
• High-angle grain boundary: interface between grains in which the angular mismatch is >15°, although this varies according to crystallography and lattice spacing.
• Coherent twin boundary: interface of arbitrary orientation that forms a mirror image in which all adjacent ions are matched.
• Incoherent twin boundary: interface of arbitrary orientation in which adjacent ions are partially but regularly matched.
• Antiphase boundary: interface effectively comprised of antisite orientation (i.e., identical adjacent ion pairs rather than adjacent cation–anion pairs).
• Domain boundary: interface between magnetic domains.
• Slip (glide) plane: interface between two regions in a crystal that have sheared along a plane.
• Stacking fault: crystallographic plane present or absent that alters the periodicity of the lattice.
The Ruddlesden–Popper, Dion–Jacobson, and Aurivillius perovskites are examples of materials in which stacking faults play key structural roles.150,151 For instance, the Ruddlesden–Popper defect is expected to become a candidate for electrochemical energy equipment, due to its superconductivity, catalytic activity, photocatalytic activity, structural characteristics, and certain unique characteristics (including oxygen migration anisotropy and controllable stoichiometry, controllable oxygen excess stoichiometry and oxygen deficiency). Besides, the ability to inject molecules into their interlayer spaces, the ion-exchange capacity, and ability to exfoliate into nanosheets can be important features of Ruddlesden–Popper oxides widely used in energy conversion and storage applications, for example, (La2−ySmy)NiO4+δ,152 NaLnTiO4 titanates (Ln = Nd, La)153 and Lan+1NinO3n+1.154 Compared to the perovskite structure, this structure also has higher redox stability, rapid surface oxygen exchange, and oxygen permeability, for instance, (La0.6Sr1.4)MnO4+δ,155 (LaxSr3−x)Fe2O7−δ,156 (La2−xSrx)(Cu1−y(Mg, Al)y)O4,157 and (La0.6Sr0.4)(Co0.7Mn0.3)O3.158


3.3.4. 3D volumetric defects. 
3D defects are known as volumetric defects. The most common of these are as follows:159
Micropore: void of diameter <2 nm
Mesopore: void of diameter 2–50 nm
Macropore: void of diameter >50 nm
Pit: surface void, often formed by etching
Cluster: localised agglomeration of multiple vacancies or ions within a grain
Inclusion: discrete secondary phase present intergranularly or intragranularly as solid, liquid, or gas
Microcrack: intergranular or transgranular fracture surface requiring electron microscopy to detect
Crack: intergranular or transgranular fracture surface requiring optical microscopy or visual inspection to detect
Order–disorder: transformation between more-ordered and less-ordered lattice states

Table 4 summarises the defective perovskite catalysts, highlighting the dominant defects that have been detected and the characterisation techniques used to identify them.

3.4. Strategies for defect generation

3.4.1. Solid solubility. 
The prognosis to achieve substantial substitutional or interstitial solubility usually is considered in terms of Hume-Rothery's rules,196 although these are for close-packed metals rather than ceramics. These rules, which may be used as general guidelines, are as follows:197
Substantial substitutional solid solubility. 
Crystal structure rule: the crystal structures of the dopant and matrix lattices are identical.
Size rule: the crystal radii of the two cations differ by ≤15%.
Valence rule: identical or near-identical cation valences can result in complete solid solubility but, when they differ, a solute cation of valence higher than that of the solvent cation exhibits greater solubility.
Electronegativity rule: the electronegativities of the two cations should be similar.
Substantial interstitial solid solubility. 
Solubility range rule: the solubility range of the solid solution should be relatively large.
Size rule: the crystal radius of the solute cation should be smaller than that of the solvent cation.
Valence rule: the extent of solubility decreases with divergence between the valences of the two cations.
Electronegativity rule: the electronegativities of the two cations should be similar.
The creation of oxygen vacancies in perovskites, which requires acceptor doping (p-type semiconductor), is often assumed through what is assumed to be substitutional solid solubility. However, as stated previously, this may not be the case. A preliminary consideration of these procedures requires assessment of the solubility limits of dopants, which rarely are known and vary according to the processing temperature. Further, exsolution may occur upon cooling. A third preliminary consideration is the uncertainty of the A- and B-site cation valences, which may vary according to precursor valence, oxygen partial pressure (pO2), achievement or nonachievement of equilibrium, IVCT/MVCT, and/or the formation of colour centres. When stoichiometric compensation of the composition is used (Ma1−xMbx) and failure to achieve substitution occurs, the possible alternative outcomes are:
• Interstitial solid solubility: charge compensation typically would be expected to take the form of cation vacancy formation (reduction is also possible) but certainly not oxygen vacancy formation.
• Mixed solid solubility: the charge-compensation outcome is dependent on the dominant vacancy-formation mechanism (cation or oxygen).
• Overcompensated/complete insolubility: upon exceeding the solubility limit, the resultant A- or B-site cation deficiency would be expected to lead to the formation of oxygen vacancies for charge compensation.
When stoichiometric compensation is not used (viz., direct addition of the dopant), then all of the preceding outcomes are possible.
It is important to consider these issues when interpreting data outputs because the prognosis for substitutional solid solubility can be subjected to preliminary consideration in terms of Hume-Rothery's rules and potential defect equilibria. When the actual solid solubility mechanism is unknown, assessment of the data in light of these considerations may reveal the existing or dominant mechanism.


3.4.2. Defects from doping. 
The substitution of La3+ by Ag+ in La(Mn0.9Co0.1)O3 resulted in a decrease in the cation valence of the A site and consequent increases in both Mn2+ and Co2+ valences.198 In addition, charge compensation involved oxygen vacancy formation to maintain the electrical neutrality. Similar B site substituted perovskites, including La(Cr0.9Ni0.1)O3,199 La(Co0.75M0.25)O3 (M = Cu, Mn, Fe),200 and La(Fe0.9Ni0.1)O3,201 exhibited symmetry reductions that generated perfect cubic perovskite structures. In addition to cation doping, anion doping of perovskites is also capable of generating high concentrations of oxygen vacancies, as in the case of replacement of lattice oxygen with nitrogen, as has been done with CaTaO2N,202 NaTaON,203 and LaTiO2N.204 In these cases, the substitution of two O2− by two N3− generates an oxygen vacancy .


3.4.3. Defects from reduction. 
Thermal reduction by heating in a reducing atmosphere, where the pO2 at sea level is <21.16 kPa, is a commonly used strategy to generate surface defects in perovskites. This generally involves the use of overpressures of reducing gases (e.g., H2, NH3 and CO) or inert gases (e.g., He, Ar) or heating under vacuum. Also, chemical reduction through the use of reducing agents is another such strategy for defect generation. This process involves the action of an electron donor, which causes the agent to oxidise and the perovskite to reduce. Good reducing agents are species that readily give up electrons, including ions and elements of large sizes, low electronegativities, low ionisation energies, and/or highly negative reduction potentials. The strongest reducing agents, therefore, are the alkali and alkaline earth elements, the first-row transition metals, the first three or four elements of the second- and third-row transition metals, the lanthanides (rare earths), and the actinides.
High concentrations of oxygen vacancies have also been generated in SrTiO3205 and Co3O4/kaolinite201 nanostructures by solid-state reaction using the reducing agent NaBH4. In addition to such solid-state reducing agents, there are also reducing solvents.206 These solvents generally are aprotic, so they are capable of donating an electron. As they facilitate hydrogen bond formation, they generally lack O–H or N–H bonds that would act as proton (H+) sources rather than electron sources. The most effective aprotic solvents tend to possess intermediate to high dielectric constants and large dipole moments. Such solvents include water, alcohols, and carboxylic acids.
The double-layered perovskite PrBaMn2O6−δ with high oxygen vacancy concentration was fabricated by hydrogen reduction at 800 °C, and it demonstrated high gravimetric and volumetric capacitances.207 Thermal treatment under argon at 950 °C created oxygen vacancies in non-stoichiometric (Ba0.5Sr0.5)(Fe0.8Co0.2)O3−δ.208 This promoted defect formation in the local tetrahedral and square planar sites during which the perovskite deviated quickly from the ideal structure Pm3m to a highly oxygen-deficient limonite phase of space group Pcmn.208 A novel flame reduction strategy has been used to generate a controlled amount of oxygen vacancies in Fe2O3, TiO2, ZnO, and BiVO4 thin films.209 Also, phosphorus-doped cobalt oxide with abundant surface defects has been synthesised using a microwave-induced plasma.210 Similarly, Xu et al.211 designed Co3O4 nanosheets with high oxygen vacancy concentrations by simple plasma treatment. Sn(Co0.9Fe0.1)(OH)6 perovskite hydroxide was used as a precursor to produce a high concentration of Sn vacancies on the surface layer by energetic Ar plasma treatment.212 Wang et al.213 demonstrated the synthesis of Co3O4 mesoporous nanosheets with controllable oxygen vacancy concentrations by annealing Co3O4 with NaH2PO2·H2O in an Ar atmosphere at 250 °C. During the annealing process, the decomposition of NaH2PO2·H2O released the reducing gas PH3, thus generating the oxygen vacancies.


3.4.4. Defects from other methods. 
Kwon et al.214 suggested that the oxygen vacancy concentration could be increased by the application of a low electrical potential (±1.5 V) and low temperatures (≤150 °C). The potential exhibited reversible drift and the oxygen vacancies coalesced into 2D planar defects. Another recently developed technique is proton-assisted oxygen vacancy creation (PAOCV),215,216 where the introduction of protons (H+) or the formation of hydroxyl groups (OH−) significantly lowers the energy of formation of oxygen vacancies. The defect equilibria for these processes are as follows:
Acidic media  
Basic media  

3.5. Stoichiometry and defects

Fig. 2 shows that there are numerous ways in which perovskite stoichiometries can be altered for the purpose of modifying the physicochemical properties through the generation of defects. This usually is done through compensated stoichiometries that are based on the assumption that the solubility mechanism is substitutional, although this is not confirmed regularly. Table 5 demonstrates both the resultant stoichiometries and defect types that are generated by the principal approaches of A site, B site, and A and B site substitutions. This information shows that one of the principal methods by which defects can be generated and carefully controlled is through extrinsic doping by donors and/or acceptors. Success in these are contingent upon the achievement of homogeneous distribution of the solute dopant ions, which may be difficult to achieve in practice owing to limitations in mixing and thermal gradients in the solids. However, the information in Table 3 indicates the second principal method by which both intrinsic and extrinsic approaches can be used to generate defects. That is, the existence of variable cation valences and O2(g) in equilibrium shows that the equilibrium can be altered and carefully controlled through the application of oxidising or reducing atmospheres for both undoped and doped perovskites. Again, success is contingent upon the achievement of homogeneity, which may be handicapped by low diffusivity, localised atmospheric variations (e.g., top vs. bottom of solid), and thermal gradients.

It can be seen that if mixed donor and acceptor ions are substituted at the A and B sites, then the charge-compensation mechanisms, where K+ would generate oxygen vacancies and V5+ would generate Ti vacancies (ignoring the potential for oxygen interstitials), effectively would create Schottky defects plus a residual of one or the other defect, depending on whether x > y or x < y. This information also indicates that when the B site consists of an ion capable of reaching a higher valence state, such as Mn3+, then Mn3+ → Mn4+ oxidation (rather than the shown example of Ti reduction) can occur. Further, if the extrinsic influence of atmospheric control is applied, then the resultant valence alteration from redox reaction, IVCT, MVCT, and/or colour centre formation can generate charge-compensating defects.
Although Table 5 assumes ionic charge compensation, this is not necessarily the case as electronic charge compensation as well as mixed charge compensation217 could also be possible. However, it is relatively easy to interpret these defect equilibria accordingly, where vacancies such as anion vacancies (e.g., ) and cation vacancies (e.g., ) are replaced by 2h˙ and 2e′, respectively.
Although defect equilibria involving valence changes are described in terms of integral changes, there are many XPS reports that are interpreted in terms of fractional valence changes.98–100 This apparent anomaly can be explained by the effects of electronegativity, which result in alteration of the distribution of the electron cloud of adjacent ions. Consequently, the introduction of dopants with electronegativities that are higher or lower than those of the intrinsic lattice cations (or anion) has the potential for further nuanced refinement of the charge-compensation mechanisms and the associated performance. Some introductory examples of the preceding approaches follow.
3.6. Self-charge-compensating Schottky defects and Frenkel defects
The preceding text generally has considered ionic defects in isolation. However, each ionic species in perovskites plays a generic role. That is, A-site substitution usually is used to enhance the catalytic activity, but B-site and anion substitution usually are used to direct the selectivity.3 Consequently, consideration of these ionic defects can be done using CaTiO3 as an example consistent with Fig. 11:
Schottky pair formation
Ionic charge compensation (A site)  
Frenkel defect formation
Cation interstitial (B site)  
Anion interstitial  
These defect equilibria can be extended to Schottky pair and Frenkel defect formation involving both A and B sites simultaneously, as shown in Fig. 11. These defect equilibria demonstrate that the formation of Schottky defects with acceptor dopants can generate oxygen vacancies. In the former case, Table 6 summarises the number of oxygen vacancies per perovskite molecule that can be generated. These equilibria also point out that anion Frenkel defects can also generate oxygen vacancies. In contrast, cation Frenkel defects would generate cation vacancies, not oxygen vacancies. Since Schottky and Frenkel defects require the removal of lattice ions from their equilibrium sites, they are often generated with the use of high-energy beam sources, such as X-rays, neutrons, and gamma irradiation,218 although heating is another source.219

Table 6 makes it clear that maximisation of  derives from doping with monovalent ions. The disadvantages of this approach are that (i) these ions are corrosive and hence tend to corrode the supports on which they are equilibrated and (ii) they have high vapour pressures and so tend to volatilise, thereby making control of the stoichiometry difficult.220
Bak et al.221 reported B-site substitution in LnNiO3 (Ln = La, Pr, Nd) with Fe. A stepwise mechanism appears to have occurred. In this case, the electrochemical technique used involved the dissolution of Ni and the resultant formation of Ni vacancies , which were then charge-compensated by oxygen vacancies . The authors considered these defect pairs to be Schottky defects, with a stoichiometric ratio of 2/3 being required for charge balance.
Liu et al.222 examined the decomposition of NO over the Ag/(La0.6Ce0.4)CoO3 perovskite and speculated that the Ag1+ loading partially substituted for La3+ and that this was charge compensated by  formation, thereby effectively forming a Schottky pair. Co 2p XPS data indicated that the increasing Ag level resulted in an increase in valence. These issues are consistent with a different type of dual charge compensation mechanism for (La2−xRbx)CuO4−δ223 and (La1−xKx)CoO3:167 (i) oxygen vacancy formation (ionic charge compensation) –  and (ii) IVCT – La3+ + Co3+ → La2+ + Co4+. However, the well known ease of Ce4+ ↔ Ce3+ redox switching offers216 another alternative for charge transfer.
Liu et al.224 used the DFT calculations to examine the intrinsic point defects of SrTiO3 under various chemical conditions. In stoichiometric SrTiO3, stable Schottky defects consisted of Sr, Ti, and O vacancies, which would form in the stoichiometric ratio 1/1/3, respectively. The formation energy per defect was relatively low at 1.64 eV, which was significantly less than that required for Frenkel and antisite defect formation. The authors inferred that the compositional homogeneity of this perovskite could be monitored through the detection of the different types of point defects as variations in the SrO and TiO2 contents favoured the formation of different types of defects.
DFT studies of LaMnO3,225 LaFeO3,226 and LaGaO3227 have indicated that Schottky defect formation energies are less than those required for A-site, B-site, or oxygen Frenkel defects. This observation implies that the intrinsic defects in such perovskites are likely to be vacancies rather than interstitials.
3.7. Charge compensation and charge transfer
The assumption of ionic charge compensation is a commonly held but often incorrect assumption as there are alternative mechanisms in the form of electronic charge compensation and redox charge compensation. As has been noted, another phenomenon that is often overlooked is IVCT, which is a form of electronic stabilisation, arising from electronegativity differences, that results in changes in valence. This explains the frequently observed shifts in XPS peak binding energies, which reveal increasing valence with increasing binding energy and decreasing valence with decreasing binding energy. As the defect equilibria formalisms as a function of solubility mechanism, charge compensation, and type of dopant generally are derived, Table 7 provides a comprehensive description of these formalisms as a guide to the derivation of the various forms of charge compensation and charge transfer using Cr doping as a prototype as it exhibits valences in the range 2+ to 6+ and CeO2 as the lattice for the sake of simplicity.
