3.8. Microscopy-based techniques for the characterisation of defects
Although determination of the type, amount, and distribution of defects can be a critical stage in the characterisation of functional materials, each technique that is applied has specific limitations. Nonetheless, several microscopy- and spectroscopy-based techniques have emerged to address these issues. Microscopy is a direct-imaging technique that is a powerful tool for the characterisation of defects and related features of materials, which are critical for catalyst design and to understand the achievement of outstanding catalytic performance. Some advanced microscopy technologies for defects are summarised as follows.

3.8.1. Transmission electron microscopy (TEM). 
TEM is the most commonly used technique for the detection of both bulk and surface defects, including vacancies, dopants, amorphous phases, pores, etc. owing to the penetration depth of the electron beam.228 In addition to the imaging of nanostructural features, its capacity for selected area electron diffraction (SAED) allows the identification of phases and the determination of crystallographic characteristics.
Types of defects. 
0D point defects (e.g., cation interstitials and vacancies), 1D defects (e.g., line defects), 2D defects (e.g., twinning, edge dislocations, screw dislocations, grain boundaries, segregation), and 3D defects (e.g., superlattices, precipitates, pores).
Advantages. 
A TEM is generally equipped with an energy dispersive spectrometer (EDS) and a camera for obtaining SAED. The former provides information regarding the chemistry and distribution of ions even at very low concentrations, while the latter gives data about the crystal structure, crystallinity, and lattice distortion. TEM images can be analysed further using standard microscopy software, the principal variants of which convert images into simulated diffraction patterns (viz., fast Fourier transform (FFT)) and provide particle size distributions. Therefore, the TEM package is very useful for obtaining data on the crystal structure, dominant facets, morphologies, single-crystal size variations, particle size distributions, thicknesses (of films), and phase chemistries (including heteroatoms, segregation, and solid solutions).
Challenges. 
TEM imaging is not suitable for perovskites that are unstable under high-voltage and electron-beam irradiation; such perovskites include halide perovskites. One strategy for TEM characterisation of unstable perovskites is the use of a low-temperature stage (viz., liquid nitrogen) to reduce reactivity. More broadly, while TEM characterisation of powders is relatively straightforward, the imaging of films may be problematic owing to the thickness, which must be thin enough to transmit electrons. Generally, samples of ≤100 nm thickness are required, although this decreases as the atomic weight increases. Traditionally, the achievement of such thicknesses was done by progressive cutting, mechanical grinding, mechanical polishing, dimpling, and ion beam milling. More recently, the availability of focussed ion beam (FIB) milling has obviated the need for ion beam milling, thus accelerating the process significantly.
Examples. 
Dong et al.229 used high-resolution HRTEM, SAED, and FFT to characterise single-crystal double-perovskite (Pr,Ba)Fe2O5+δ, in which the presence of oxygen hypostoichiometry is confirmed indirectly through lattice distortion. In another study, Kim et al.230 examined the microstructure, crystallographic orientation, and strain state of a cubic Sr(Ti,Fe)O3 film grown epitaxially on a (100)-oriented CeO2 on a Y2O3-stabilised ZrO2 buffer layer on an Si substrate. In this work, HRTEM, SAED, and FFT were applied across the interfacial region in order to investigate the preferred growth direction of the perovskite crystal facets and the effect of substrate orientation on the growth.


3.8.2. Scanning transmission electron microscopy (STEM). 
STEM images by the two modes of high-angle annular dark-field (HAADF) and low-magnification annular bright field (ABF). STEM is effective in distinguishing bulk defects from surface defects, which is difficult using TEM.231 Lattice ions and ionic defects in crystalline materials can be differentiated by their differences in brightness, where intrinsic defects are detected on the basis of brightness distribution irregularities, and extrinsic defects are detected on the basis of brightness differences resulting from differing atomic weights.
Types of defects. 
0D point defects (e.g., cation and anion interstitials and vacancies, antisite substitution, Frenkel pairs, Schottky pairs), 1D defects (e.g., line defects), 2D defects (e.g., twinning, edge dislocations, screw dislocations, grain boundaries), and 3D defects (e.g., superlattices, precipitates, pores).
Advantages. 
STEM imaging enables the detection of essentially all 0D and 1D defects. This method is suitable for semi-quantitative defect concentration analysis, where the number of defects can be counted and extrapolated for the entire material. STEM also yields images of higher resolution and magnification compared to those obtained using TEM. Further, owing to the capacity to change the probe convergence angle, STEM imaging has greater lateral and depth resolutions compared to HRTEM.
Challenges. 
Similar to TEM, sample preparation and the previously mentioned limitations with unstable perovskites under the electron beam are equally relevant.
Examples. 
Liu et al.232 used STEM-HAADF and STEM-ABF to determine the homogeneity and lattice positions across the NaNbO3/(Na1−xNb)O3+δ interface of perovskite thin films grown on single-crystal Sr(Ti1−xNbx)O3−δ. The effect of Nb doping of the substrate on the thin films was analysed. The resultant defects at the interfacial region of the thin film included dislocations and Na/Nb antisite substitution.


3.8.3. Atomic force microscopy (AFM)/Kelvin probe force microscopy (KPFM). 
The probe tip used in AFM is sensitive to materials with demonstrated sub-nanometre resolution, so it can be used to detect the presence of defects.233 While KPFM is similar to AFM, it offers the additional ability to map the chemical potential of perovskites alone or relative to that of the substrate. KPFM can also determine the work function change, which enables the determination of the position of the conduction band. So the combination of KPFM with ultraviolet-visible (UV-Vis) spectrophotometry, which provides the band gap, allows determination of the valence band. Further, the combination of KPFM, UV-vis, and XPS, the last of which provides the relative positions of the valence band and Fermi level, then allows the assignment of the absolute Fermi level.
Types of defects. 
0D point defects (e.g., cation interstitials and vacancies, antisite substitution, Frenkel pairs, Schottky pairs), 1D defects (e.g., line defects), 2D defects (e.g., twinning, edge dislocations, screw dislocations, grain boundaries, segregation), and 3D defects (e.g., superlattices, precipitates, pores).
Advantages. 
Although there are many variant modes of AFM, the contact AFM method, where the probe is in direct contact with the material, is commonly used for thickness and particle size measurements. It is particularly useful in the characterisation of 2D materials. It provides atomic-resolution 2D and 3D topographical images of surfaces. AFM/KPFM offers a field of view (∼20 μm2) that is larger than those of TEM and STEM. The height resolution is ∼0.5 nm. In contrast, non-contact AFM (nc-AFM) allows direct imaging of domain structures and point charge potential with sub-nanometre resolution. Since AFM/KPFM is not constrained by the requirement of electrical conductivity, it can be applied to all types of perovskite materials, including conducting, semiconducting, and insulating materials. Other advantages are that it requires only simple sample preparation, it can be carried out in air and controlled atmospheres, heating and cooling stages can be used, and sample sizes up to several centimetres can be examined.
Challenges. 
This method is limited to bulk and 2D materials of lateral size no more than several microns as well as of limited roughness (≤500 nm). It is important to match the type of probe to the nature of the substrate as the shape of the former can determine the precision of measurement. A major shortcoming is that, until recently, the area probed could not be observed directly, so siting on a specific location was very difficult. However, systems that integrate AFM and SEM have recently become available in order to overcome this shortcoming. Also, the analyses can be time consuming, particularly when high resolutions are required for defect detection. Further, the relatively rapid probe deterioration tends to make the technique expensive. Finally, KPFM has the limitation that a conducting substrate is required, and its work function and that of the probe must be known.
Examples. 
Leng et al.234 reported in-depth characterisation of a stratified Ruddlesden–Popper perovskite using simultaneous nc-AFM and scanning tunnelling microscopy (STM). This work reported the relaxation of the organic chains of the perovskite upon the application of thermal stress by laser irradiation. More importantly, in situ reconstruction of the surface atomic assembly and the formation of vacancies were observed during heat treatment of the perovskite.


3.8.4. Scanning tunneling microscopy (STM). 
STM can image at atomic resolution, but it requires a conductive material for electron tunnelling from the tip of the scanning probe to the sample.235,236 STM can be used to detect surface molecular compounds and surface defects as well as to measure the band gaps of semiconductors.237
Types of defects. 
0D point defects (e.g., cation and anion interstitials and vacancies, antisite substitution, Frenkel pairs, Schottky pairs), 1D defects (e.g., line defects), 2D defects (e.g., twinning, edge dislocations, screw dislocations, grain boundaries, segregation), and 3D defects (e.g., lattice distortion, superlattices, precipitates, pores).
Advantages. 
The STM method can provide topographical images of atoms, growth artefacts, crystal interfaces, and atomic arrangements with subnanometre resolution. STM is one of the most powerful tools for the determination of local crystallographic and defect data.
Challenges. 
The sample preparation process is demanding as it requires extremely clean and stable surfaces, sharp probe tips, excellent vibration isolation, and sophisticated electronics. Nonetheless, many STM units are built in-house rather than being purchased. Cross sectional STM of heterostructures is handicapped by the necessity of having sufficiently large and discrete terraces present on cleaved surfaces, which typically are rough owing to induced strains. The tip represents a third challenge to STM since, with surfaces with an unpinned Fermi level, the presence of the tip and the voltage applied to the tunnelling contact significantly modify the spectral positions of observed electronic states and bands.238
Examples. 
Liu et al.239 used combined STM and DFT simulation to characterise the nature of intrinsic defects formed on the surface of the halide perovskite CH3NH3PbBr3 and their role in stability. It was found that decomposition occurs more readily in the presence of surface vacancies for Br or Br-methylammonium (Br-MA) rather than with pristine sites. The STM images showed that the pristine single-crystal CH3NH3PbBr3 perovskite with the preferred (010) growth plane was stable against adsorption by molecules and atoms, whereas the defective CH3NH3PbBr3 perovskite exhibited significantly increased adsorption energies for water, oxygen, and acetonitrile molecules. Similarly, She et al.240 reported combined STM and DFT in the surface analysis of the atomic structure of the CH3NH3PbI3 perovskite. The STM results showed variations in the dipole pattern of surface methylammonium (CH3NH3) and its interactions with surface iodine anions, both of which played critical roles in the stability of the surface structure.


3.8.5. Aberration corrected transmission electron microscopy (ACTEM). 
ACTEM is one of the most sensitive atomic-resolution techniques for the identification and mapping of ions and their bonding in three dimensions. ACTEM is a TEM with objective lens correction, which significantly improves the resolution of TEM images by eliminating the spherical aberration of some defect structures.241,242 Detailed measurements by ACTEM show a remarkable signal-to-noise ratio, resolution, and sensitivity, which allow detailed calculation of extrinsic interstitial and intrinsic vacancy concentrations. Further, ACTEM is suitable for complex non-periodic structures, where there is a wide range of atomic interfaces, dopants, and point defects.
Types of defects. 
0D point defects (e.g., cation and anion interstitials and vacancies, antisite substitution, Frenkel pairs, Schottky pairs), 1D defects (e.g., line defects), 2D defects (e.g., twinning, edge dislocations, screw dislocations, grain boundaries, segregation), and 3D defects (e.g., lattice distortion, superlattices, precipitates, pores).
Advantages. 
ACTEM is relatively easy to operate, and the data are straightforward to interpret. It provides direct images with atomic-scale sensitivity, so it can detect single ions. It is distinguished from other analytical tools in that it can image both cations and anions, and the detection extends to lithium. It can be operated in situ with heating and cooling stages and it can be operated in gaseous and liquid environments. Sample heating by the electron beam can be mitigated through the use of a single-electron camera. Magnetic materials can be examined as well. Its greatest power comes when it is coupled with electron energy loss spectroscopy (EELS), where elemental, defect, and band gap mapping are possible.
Challenges. 
Outside of the considerable expense of the unit, its principal limitation is that grain sizes and thin film thicknesses are limited to ∼40–50 nm. Further, the lateral sample size is limited by the requirement of a grid of dimensions 3 mm × 3 mm. A final issue of concern is the importance of sample cleanliness, where adsorbed organics and other species must be avoided. In particular, when a Ga-based FIB is used for sample thinning, the sample is likely to be contaminated by Ga. The alternative is the use of a Xe-based FIB.
Examples. 
Nelson et al.243 used spherical ACTEM to study the polarisation of the ferroelectric (001)-oriented BiFeO3 affected by electric boundary conditions across the interfacial region with (001)-oriented insulating TbScO3. The ACTEM images, with <10−10 m resolution, showed that an oxygen ion in the octahedral position and an Fe cation at the A site of the rhombohedral BiFeO3 crystal were displaced along one of the 〈111〉 directions relative to the centre of the A-site Bi sublattice (i.e., Frenkel defect). This was shown to be followed by counterrotation of the oxygen (13.8°) around the 〈111〉 axes. This resulted in alteration of the polarisation direction, thus changing the local ferroelectric properties of the BiFeO3 perovskite.243 In this study, the TEM focal series was used in order to image light oxygen ions in the presence of strongly scattering heavy Bi. In another study, Jia et al.244 used negative spherical ACTEM to demonstrate electric dipoles formed in response to the relative displacement of Zr/Ti cation columns and oxygen anion columns in a SrTiO3/Pb(Zr0.2Ti0.8)O3/SrTiO3 thin film heterostructure. The work suggested the feasibility of imaging the local polarisation of dipoles and quantitative measurement of the local polarisation in order to investigate possible mechanisms of polarisation switching and resultant properties, including coercive field and ferroelectric fatigue.

3.9. Spectroscopy-based techniques for the characterisation of defects
As previously discussed, the observation of structural defects can be done using microscopy methods involving nanoscale imaging of crystal structures. In contrast, spectroscopic methods do not involve direct imaging and provide indirect analyses of structure, chemistry, electronic states, valence, bonding, and defects.

3.9.1. X-Ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS). 
XPS has emerged as one of the most useful and hence commonly used techniques for the characterisation of surface and near-surface chemistries.245 The principal data provided by XPS are the direct identification of surface and sub-surface ions and their valences as well as the indirect identification and quantification of cation and anion vacancies through the assumption of charge-compensating redox effects. Hence, this facility provides a powerful tool for characterisation relevant to surface catalysis. Since the mean free path of electrons is sub-nanometre, ultra-high-vacuum conditions are required. However, near-ambient pressure XPS (NAPXPS) can be used to characterise materials in controlled ambient environments, thereby allowing monitoring of the surface properties of materials under in situ conditions.246 Further, deep XPS can be applied for in-depth quantitative analysis of elements in perovskites, which represents a powerful tool for mapping defect concentration variations between the surface and inner layers. As mentioned earlier, XPS valence band measurements are a variant tool for XPS that can (1) derive the difference between the energy levels of the valence band and the Fermi level and (2) derive the midgap states formed from the presence of defects in the system.
Types of defects. 
Indirect identification and semi-quantification of 0D point defects (e.g., extrinsic cation and anion interstitials and substitutionals, intrinsic cation and anion vacancies, Schottky pairs) and 2D defects (e.g., segregation).
Advantages. 
A major advantage of XPS is that it analyses over a very wide field of view (500 μm2), so it provides relatively representative data. The technique is sensitive, providing reliable data to 0.01 at% precision. It also detects fractional valence changes, which are a reflection of sample inhomogeneity. XPS also has the advantage that it allows differentiation between the surface composition (using ionic data) and the sub-surface composition (using ion-oxygen data). The requirement of vacuum can be overcome through the use of NAPXPS, which uses soft X-ray irradiation; here, the sample is placed inside a cell with controlled gas pressure (typically up to 25 mbar) and the use of a heating/cooling stage in the range 0–700 °C enables in situ analyses, which are extremely useful in energy and catalysis applications.
Challenges. 
The analyses are limited to the surface and sub-surface of perovskites unless deep XPS is used. However, the ablation required for this variation can alter the perovskite by reduction and hence increase the oxygen vacancy concentration. General analyses by XPS must be done in the knowledge that the technique cannot differentiate between intrinsic surface solutes and extrinsic precipitates. The analyses are often hindered by overlap of peaks for different valences of individual ions, so clear identification is often precluded. A similar limitation arises from the fact that the XPS database is of limited size. Also, NAPXPS requires an electrically conducting solid, so insulating samples cannot be examined.
Examples. 
Vovk et al.247 used in situ XPS under vacuum to analyse the binding energies for the 2p and 3d orbitals of O and Ni, respectively, in LaNiO3 and La(Ni0.4Cr0.6)O3 perovskites during Ni2+ ↔ Ni3+ redox cycling. The shifts in binding energies and broadening of the peaks for the La 3d, Ni 3p, O 2p, and Cr 3s orbitals indicated delocalisation of their electronic states, resulting in the formation of midgap states. Merino et al.248 used XPS for the quantitative analysis of surface oxygen vacancies in (La1−xCax)CoO3−δ. This was done through comparative XPS analyses of the O 1s orbital in pristine LaCoO3, (La0.8Ca0.2)CoO3−δ, (La0.6Ca0.4)CoO3−δ, and (La0.5Ca0.5)CoO3−δ.


3.9.2. Electron paramagnetic resonance spectroscopy (EPR). 
EPR, which is also known as electron spin resonance (ESR), characterises the types of oxidation states and defects in the bulk. The magnetic field relative to the EPR peak signal indicates the type of defect, and the intensity of the peak signal indicates the defect concentration.249 The latter is obtained by integration of the vacancy peaks, which are generated by the unpaired electrons of the oxygen vacancies.250 This technique is widely used for transition metal perovskites owing to the redox flexibility.
Types of defects. 
0D point defects with unpaired electrons (e.g., cation and anion interstitials and vacancies, antisite substitution, Frenkel pairs, Schottky pairs).
Advantages. 
A great advantage of EPR is its high penetration depth, which enables true bulk analysis; the procedures are also relatively rapid and uncomplicated. Its identification and quantification of point defects are very sensitive, being ∼1000 times more sensitive than nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR). This sensitivity derives from the flipping of the electron spins in EPR rather than the excitation of atomic nuclei in NMR, so the former yields larger quanta of energy absorbed in given magnetic fields. In addition, EPR is widely used to study polymeric chain growth and depolymerisation reactions.251
Challenges. 
The principal limitation of EPR is that only defects with unpaired electrons are EPR-active. Since most stable molecules exhibit paired electrons, EPR is less widely used than NMR. Another issue for consideration is that the measurement of defect concentrations is dependent on the loading mass of the sample, so comparative data must begenerated using samples of identical masses. Analyses are often hindered by the existing database, which is limited to simple structures, thus favouring comparative rather than absolute experimental approaches.
Examples. 
Eichel252 reported a perspective study on the defect structures of multivalent transition-metal-doped oxide perovskites using multi-frequency and pulsed EPR. The data revealed the formation of defect complexes between acceptor-type transition-metal centres in combination with either single  or multiple . Such defect complexes orientated along the polarisation direction and dynamically altered their orientation in terms of domain switching upon application of high electric fields. Further, the multivalent nature of the manganese provided trapping centres for electronic and ionic charge carriers (e′, ) such that valency-altered  acceptor states or  defect complexes were formed. Additionally, the trapping of charge carriers at the intrinsic reduced B-site ions,  and , was observed by EPR.


3.9.3. Positron annihilation lifetime spectroscopy (PALS). 
PALS is an atomic-resolution method that utilises positrons (antimatter counterparts of electrons) to release radiation from solids, thereby providing information about vacancies and their relative concentrations from the positron lifespan.253 Following injection, positrons are thermalised (thermal equilibration) and annihilated, resulting in the release of gamma radiation. At the moment of annihilation, the lifetimes of positrons are obtained by calculating the local electron densities of defects, including vacancies, dislocations, and voids.254 It effectively provides a comparative measure of lattice perfection vs. imperfection.
Types of defects. 
0D point defects (e.g., cation vacancies, charge-compensating anion vacancies, antisite substitution, Frenkel pairs, Schottky pairs), 1D defects (e.g., line defects), 2D defects (e.g., twinning, edge dislocations, screw dislocations, grain boundaries, segregation), and 3D defects (e.g., lattice distortion, superlattices, precipitates, pores).
Advantages. 
PALS can be done in air at room temperature and ambient pressure. It is highly sensitive to cation type and vacancy concentration, with a sensitivity of the order of 0.1 ppm.255–257 It can be used for depth profiling and, on a coarser scale, it can resolve voids (<1 nm) in metals, semiconductors, and polymers.
Challenges. 
The limitations of PALS include weak positron production and slow acquisition rates, where typical measurements require ∼5–10 days. The general technique is one of bulk measurement without depth resolution capability, although equipping with variable-energy capability can overcome this limitation. Further, relatively large samples, typically >1.27 cm diameter, are required in order to provide complete coverage of the instrument's necessarily large aperture.
Examples. 
There are many studies reporting the use of PALS to assess vacancy-related defects in oxide perovskites. Measurements on ferroelectric PbTiO3, Pb(Zr0.42Ti0.58)O3,255,256 SrTiO3 thin film, and Nb-doped SrTiO3 single crystals257,258 have shown close agreement in the experimental and DFT results for positron lifetimes for two types of cation vacancies. The negatively charged cation vacancy at the octahedrally coordinated B-site typically has a positron lifetime of 180–195 ps, which can be differentiated from the lifetime of 280–290 ps of the larger 12-coordinated A-site vacancy.255–258 Keeble et al.259 reported in-depth PALS characterisation of donor-doped SrTiO3 perovskites, where La3+ was substituted partially at A sites (Sr). The dominant positron trapping vacancy defect was shown to be charge-compensating Sr vacancies. The concentration of the Sr vacancies was calculated using a defect-specific trapping coefficient, and the corresponding La substitutional concentration was determined from this. These measurements also revealed the presence of a vacancy cluster defect in the upper layers (∼20–50 nm) of the films.


3.9.4. Energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS). 
EDS is an X-ray fluorescence technique used for the elemental detection of dopant and matrix ions by point and line scanning and areal mapping. It invariably is integrated with scanning electron microscopy (SEM) or TEM.
Types of defects. 
0D point defects (e.g., cation and anion interstitials and vacancies, extrinsic antisite substitution, Frenkel pairs, Schottky pairs), 1D defects (e.g., extrinsic induced line defects), 2D defects (e.g., grain boundaries, segregation), and 3D defects (e.g., precipitates, pores).
Advantages. 
EDS is capable of scanning large areas, so it is particularly useful for bulk analyses. Line scanning is often used for chemical analyses across interfaces, thereby providing not only chemical data but also nanostructural information. Examination of the cation:anion stoichiometry can provide approximations of both the compound identity and defect concentrations.
Challenges. 
EDS has several major limitations. The first is that it is of relatively low sensitivity so that analyses should not be considered more than semi-quantitative. The second is that it extends only to carbon. The third is that the X-ray beam penetrates below the surface, thus analysing volumetrically. Consequently, quantitative data are accurate only with full dense and single-phase materials. Further, while the beam in SEM is relatively wide and so lacks resolution, the beam in TEM is narrow and so provides good resolution.
Examples. 
Zhong et al.260 reported the use of TEM EDS line scanning and mapping applied to pristine and defective SrTaO2N. The results of the line scanning revealed that the SrTaO2N heat-treated in H2 exhibited a lower oxygen concentration owing to the formation of oxygen vacancies. This result was confirmed by corresponding higher bulk electrical conductivity. Furthermore, TEM EDS mapping was used to indicate the atomic positions of Sr and Ta.


3.9.5. X-Ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS). 
XAS is a core-level spectroscopic technique used to examine the chemical structures and electronic states of materials using a form of inelastic scattering of X-rays.261,262 This method is very sensitive and can characterise crystalline and amorphous materials at subatomic resolution with considerably more sensitivity than XPS. X-Ray absorption near-edge structure (XANES) data (≤40 eV) and extended X-ray absorption fine structure (EXAFS) data (>40 eV) can be extracted from XAS data. All these types of data are exceptional in that they provide information about interatomic distances, coordination types and numbers, bond densities, and atomic disorder.263 Owing to the importance of the defects in the catalytic performance of perovskites, XAS, XANES, and EXAFS are used increasingly for the in-depth characterisation of catalytic materials. XANES provides information about the valence state, energy bandwidth, and bond angles in a structure, while EXAFS, which has 10−11 m resolution, is widely used for the analysis of single-atom catalysts.
Types of defects. 
Indirect identification of 0D point defects (e.g., ordered and non-ordered cation and anion interstitials and vacancies, antisite substitution, Frenkel pairs, Schottky pairs), 1D defects (e.g., line defects), 2D defects (e.g., twinning, edge dislocations, screw dislocations, grain boundaries, segregation), and 3D defects (e.g., lattice distortion, superlattices, precipitates, pores).
Advantages. 
This powerful technique provides information on pre-catalysts, intermediate reactants, and reaction mechanisms. It typically is equipped with a synchrotron radiation source, allowing it to capture the electronic structures and local environments of perovskites. The emerging operando XAS techniques have been shown to yield valuable information regarding the chemical, electronic, and structural alterations, chemical bonding, and oxidation states of perovskites during the reactions.264 A major advantage of EXAFS analysis is that it can be applied for the calculation of average particle size. This can apply to particles of <5 nm, where the coordination number has a nonlinear relationship with the particle size diameter.265
Challenges. 
The application of soft XAS in liquid cells is limited since such characterisation requires vacuum conditions, owing to the absorption of generated photons, within the soft X-ray energy range, by water and air. To overcome such limit, the perovskite powder should be pressed into a pellet followed by mechanical fracture under UH-vacuum conditions (∼10−10 mbar).266
Examples. 
Guan et al.267 examined oxygen-vacancy-ordered RBaCo2O5.5+δ, where R is La, Pr, Sm, and/or Gd, using soft XAS spectra at the Co-L2,3 edges in the surface-sensitive total electron yield (TEY) mode. The purpose was to obtain information about the electronic structures and local coordination environments at the near-surface (∼5 nm). For these compositions, δ was determined by measuring the oxidation state of Co from the Co-L2,3 data. The results showed that despite the consistent 3+ valence of all A-site species, the charge-compensating Co oxidation state varied. That is, the authors noted that the corresponding oxygen vacancy concentration increased with decreasing ionic radius corresponding to the sequence of substitutions. However, as ionic size does not lead to an obvious mechanism, an alternative explanation can be proposed. That is, as the electronegativities of the dopants increase slightly with this progression, their substitution provides an additional component to remove electrons from oxygen, thereby introducing progressively greater acceptor-like behaviour and increasing the oxygen vacancy concentration in addition to the same effect by cobalt. The same authors268 reported combined XAS and DFT studies in the attempt to optimise Co-based perovskites for HER performance as a function of A-site ionic electronegativity (AIE). It was found that in order to fulfil Sabatier's principle, the most efficient electron exchange interactions between A- and B-sites were obtained with an AIE value of ∼2.33, which correlated with (Gd0.5La0.5)BaCo2O5.5+δ.


3.9.6. Low energy ion scattering spectroscopy (LEIS). 
LEIS spectroscopy is used to examine the outermost (∼1–3 nm) and outer layers (∼10 nm) of the composition and structure of surfaces.269 In this technique, the surface is bombarded by noble gases, such as 4He+ and 20Ne+, at energies in the range 1–10 keV. Owing to the probability of ionic neutralisation at a surface, the probe depth of LEIS can be as little as one or two atomic surface layers, making LEIS one of the most surface-sensitive analytical tools.
Types of defects. 
Indirect identification and semi-quantification of 0D point defects including cationic and anionic vacancies, extrinsic substitution, Frenkel, Schottky, molecular and bonding variations.
Advantages. 
The biggest advantage of LEIS is that it provides averaged but sensitive information across a few nanometres (∼3–5 atomic layers) of the surface. This is in contrast to XPS and time-of-flight secondary ion mass spectrometry (ToF-SIMS), which have limited capacity to provide definitive information about the outermost atomic layers of a material. Consequently, LEIS is useful for monitoring phenomena such as catalysis, wetting, diffusion, adhesion, and adsorption. Dynamic depth profiling can also be done using LEIS equipped with a sputter gun. Using this approach, quantification of the surface elements can be done as there is no matrix effect in LEIS, and so the signal is directly proportional to the surface coverage of elements.
Challenges. 
LEIS provides no information about molecules and oxidation states and it is an expensive characterisation technique (as expensive as XPS and ToF-SIMS). LEIS analysis, which is from the outermost layer of a material, can be super challenging since any exposure to the atmosphere contaminates a surface with adventitious carbon, typically hydrocarbons.
Examples. 
The importance of using LEIS in perovskite characterisation is based on the significant role of contaminant diffusion from the bulk to the surface at elevated temperatures, viz., segregation, which can affect adversely the catalytic performance of perovskites. Consequently, characterisation of the terminating layer and defining the dominant species at the surface are of considerable importance for understanding the ionic conductivity and related performances of perovskites.269 Druce et al.270 used deep-profiling in situ LEIS measurement on three polycrystalline perovskites, including A- and B-site substituted (La0.6Sr0.4)(Co0.2Fe0.8)O0.3−δ, A-site substituted (GdxBa1−x)Co2O5+δ, and Ruddlesden–Popper La2NiO4+δ. The results revealed that Sr, Ba, and La, respectively, were the dominant elements in the terminating layers. Further, at the outer surface of La2NiO4+δ, a small Pb peak was detected, revealing its segregation to the surface.


3.9.7. Laser Raman microspectroscopy (Raman). 
Laser Raman microspectroscopy is another widely used method to determine defects in materials and analyse chemical bonding energies on the surfaces of perovskite materials.271 Raman patterns of defective materials reveal the presence of peaks specific to oxygen vacancies, the area under which is proportional to the concentration.
Types of defects. 
0D point defects (e.g., cation and anion interstitials and vacancies, antisite substitution, Frenkel pairs, Schottky pairs), 1D defects (e.g., line defects), 2D defects (e.g., twinning, edge dislocations, screw dislocations, grain boundaries, segregation), and 3D defects (e.g., lattice distortion, superlattices, precipitates, pores).
Advantages. 
There are numerous advantages of Raman analyses, such as (1) no sample preparation or alteration is required; (2) there are no size, type (powder or film), or mass limitations; (3) the process is very rapid, requiring seconds to minutes; (4) the analyses are done in air; (5) operation of the instrument is straightforward, with little training required; (6) the laser beam used for detection has a relatively large diameter of ∼1 μm; (7) it has confocal imaging capability, so subsurface features in transparent materials can be detected; (8) it is highly sensitive to the detection of impurities; (9) multiple lasers can be selected for greater detection sensitivity for different materials; (10) peak shifts can be used to detect residual surface stress; (11) it can be fitted with heating and cooling stages as well as controlled-atmosphere chambers for in situ reaction assessment; and (12) the database of patterns is large and growing.
Challenges. 
Raman analyses are limited to crystalline ceramics and polymers. Metals and amorphous materials generally are not examined using Raman, and similarly, some organic-based perovskites are not suitable as some possess organic bonds that are Raman-inactive. In such cases, Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (FTIR) is recommended as a complementary analytical technique. As the laser is a source of energy and heating, it can degrade and oxidise materials, especially if the intensity is increased in order to enhance sensitivity. It can also induce some phase transformation, as in the case of anatase → rutile.
Examples. 
Kumar et al.272 used Raman analyses to study a new Raman mode that appeared in orthorhombic B-site substituted Pr(Fe0.7Cr0.3)O3−δ. The Raman characterisation revealed that the electron–phonon coupling was highly sensitive to the presence of mixed Fe–Cr ions, while such multi-phonon scattering was absent in defect-free PrFeO3. The Raman results also showed variations in the peak intensities of the Raman octahedral breathing mode at ∼660 cm−1, in response to different excitation wavelengths, which was attributed to the lattice rearrangement arising from electron transfer from Cr3+(d3) to Fe3+(d5), thereby resulting in a new d2–d6 (Cr4+–O−2–Fe2+) configuration and consequent activation of a new Raman mode. Further, Dama et al.273 studied the structural alterations of AZrO3 perovskites using A-site substitutions by Ca, Sr, and Ba. This work showed that the number of Raman vibration modes decreased during the orthorhombic to cubic phase transition from Ca- to Sr- to Ba-substituted perovskites.


3.9.8. Photoluminescence (PL). 
PL represents a simple and quick characterisation method to determine defects, including colour centres and coordination changes in perovskites. The technique is based on the capture of the light generated from the transition of photons from the excited state to the ground state, where the optical properties, which are determined by the electronic and structural properties, are themselves determined by intrinsic and extrinsic defects affecting the long-range order and chemical bonding of perovskites.274 Similar to Raman analysis, the area under a peak is proportional to the concentration of the defect. Resonance Raman and PL imaging can also be used separately or together to reveal 0–3D defects, such as variation in localised coordination.
Types of defects. 
0D point defects (e.g., cation and anion interstitials and vacancies, antisite substitution, Frenkel pairs, Schottky pairs), 1D defects (e.g., line defects), 2D defects (e.g., twinning, edge dislocations, screw dislocations, grain boundaries, segregation), and 3D defects (e.g., lattice distortion, superlattices, precipitates, pores).
Advantages. 
There are numerous advantages of PL, such as (1) no sample preparation or alteration is required; (2) there are no size, type (powder or film), or mass limitations; (3) the process is very rapid, requiring seconds to minutes; (4) the analyses are done in air; (5) operation of the instrument is straightforward, with little training required; (6) the laser beam used for detection has a relatively large diameter of ∼1 μm; (7) it has confocal imaging capability, so subsurface features in transparent materials can be detected; (8) it is highly sensitive to the detection of impurities; (9) multiple lasers can be selected for greater detection sensitivity for different materials; (10) peak shifts can be used to detect residual surface stress; (11) it can be fitted with heating and cooling stages as well as controlled-atmosphere chambers for in situ reaction assessment; (12) the database of patterns is large and growing; (13) it can be used to determine the band gap; and (14) it provides comparative measurement of electron/hole recombination.
Challenges. 
Similar to Raman, the laser is a source of energy and heating, so the laser power must be held at relatively low power in order not to degrade or oxidise some materials. Another limitation is that many optical centres in materials have multiple excitation stages, which makes the interpretation of the peaks challenging.
Examples. 
Zhang et al.275 reported a fundamental study of the kinetics of the chemical transformation mechanisms in single-crystal CsPbX3 (X = Cl, Br, I) halide perovskite nanoplates through quantitative imaging of the anion exchange reaction using confocal PL microscopy. The anion exchange pathway on the nanoplates was shown to be dependent on the reaction time and the nanoplate thickness, both of which are governed by the crystal structure and the diffusion-limited transformation mechanism. Further, PL lifetime imaging microscopy was used to assess the stability of the optical characteristics of the perovskites, which was demonstrated by the recombination rates of the carriers and their lifetime distributions across the nanoplates during anion exchange. Further, Wang et al.131 indirectly demonstrated the presence of oxygen vacancies in a Ruddlesden–Popper Pr3+-doped Na2(Pr0.005La1.995)Ti3O10 layered perovskite using temperature- and high-pressure-dependent PL measurements. Kudo et al.276 undertook a comparative study on the luminescence properties (PL, phosphorescence, and thermoluminescence) of pristine K2La2Ti3010 and lanthanide-doped K2(Ln0.02 La1.98)Ti3O10 (Ln = Pr, Sm, Eu, Tb, Dy, Er, and Tm). The resultant hole trapping in both K2 Pr0.02(La1.98)Ti3O10 and K2(Tb0.02 La1.98)Ti3O10 led to the oxidation of Tb and Pr to a higher valence of 4+ and consequent long-term charge separation, a major advantage for the catalytic/electrocatalytic applications of perovskites.

3.10. Bulk analytical techniques
In addition to TEM, HAADF, and EPR (ESR), there are other well-established methods to detect the presence and concentrations of dopants. These include inductively coupled plasma optical emission spectrometry (ICP-OES), inductively coupled plasma atomic absorption spectrometry (ICP-AES), and inductively coupled plasma mass spectrometry (ICP-MS). It is not uncommon for complementary bulk and surface analytical data to be provided as these may exhibit similarities as well as differences. In the same vein, real-time techniques, such as synchrotron powder X-ray diffraction (SPXRD) and Operando Raman, to generate and analyse defects are used.

Fig. 12 illustrates the range of typical beam diameters and penetration depths of these techniques. Table 8 lists the standard microscopy and spectroscopy techniques that are used to detect defective materials.
 Fig. 12  Imaging limitations of characterisation techniques applied to defects.

3.11. Cautionary tales
Examination of the literature on the defect engineering of perovskites reveals that there are some frequently encountered issues that may cloud the rationale of the experimental design and the interpretation of the results:
• Reaction equilibrium: it is generally assumed that heat treatment at high temperatures achieves a thermodynamically equilibrium state. In reality, this is rarely the case, with relative states of nonequilibrium being the rule rather than the exception. Hence, alteration of the catalytic properties over time can be expected to result partly from the act of continued equilibration.
• Defect equilibrium: it is also commonly assumed that the diffusion of defects caused by substitution results in the exit of the substituted ion to the surface of the solid. However, under nonequilibrium conditions, this is not necessarily the case. Here, the cation would remain as an interstitial dopant, which would be ionically charge compensated by the annihilation of anion vacancies.
• Ionic radii: the publication by Shannon287 that sets out the standard database for ionic radii lists both crystal radii and ionic radii. However, although the publication states unambiguously that the crystal radii are to be used in solids, the ionic radii are regularly used in error.
• Cation solid solubility: substitutional cation solid solubility is assumed widely, and stoichiometric compensation is made accordingly. However, there are alternatives in the form of (i) interstitial solid solubility, (ii) insolubility, (iii) partial solubility, and (iv) chemical reaction. Although the Goldschmidt tolerance factor19 is commonly considered, Hume-Rothery's rules196 can also provide some guidance concerning the probability of substitutional vs. interstitial solid solubility and substantial vs. limited solubility. Data from X-ray diffraction, neutron diffraction, and XPS can clarify the nature of the solid solubility mechanism.
• Anion solid solubility: although the substitution of anions, such as nitrogen, for oxygen is assumed widely, in fact, the oxide sublattice is so stable that it is quite difficult for such substitution to be effected. Thus, such solubility is frequently overestimated in that it may be very limited or non-existent. In such cases, such dopants are likely to act simply as reducing agents, thereby causing metal reduction and/or forming oxygen vacancies.
• Donor/acceptor: dopants can be electron donors, neutral, or electron acceptors. These distinctions are often not made, leading to misconceptions concerning the defect equilibria. In general, if ionic charge compensation occurs, then cation donor dopants generate cation vacancies, neutral dopants do not generate vacancies, and acceptor dopants generate anion vacancies. Anion interstitial formation upon donor doping is rare owing to the large radii of anions.
• Charge compensation: ionic charge compensation is assumed widely, and the defect equilibria assumed accordingly. However, there are alternatives in the form of (i) electronic charge compensation and (ii) redox charge compensation. The latter mechanism assumes increasing importance when transition metal and rare earth metals of stable valences are used as dopants. The conventional Kröger–Vink126 notation generally does not consider redox charge compensation.
• Charge transfer: the observation of altered and variable valence states is assumed widely to be associated with charge-compensating vacancy (or interstitial) formation. However, intervalence charge transfer288 and multivalence charge transfer101 as stabilisation mechanisms are often overlooked.
4. Defect engineering of perovskite thermocatalysts
Catalysis is the increase in the rate of chemical reaction with the use of a catalyst in any chemical process where high amounts and rates of conversion, selectivity, and stability are desired. Within the technical field of catalysis, thermal catalysis (also known as environmental catalysis) is defined as pure chemical catalysis at a certain temperature, where thermal energy initiates a reaction, such as hydrogenation or dehydrogenation, partial oxidation, or complete oxidation. One of the key determinants of catalytic behaviour is the presence of different types of structural defects, which may act as active sites. There are four categories of structural defects, which are point, line, surface, and volume.
4.1. Point defects
As mentioned previously, point defects can be divided into three categories: substitutionals, interstitials, and vacancies; a Schottky pair combines cation + anion vacancies and a Frenkel pair combines cation or anion interstitial + vacancy. Point defects can occur from the presence of impurities but, more particularly, from intentionally added dopants. Point defects typically play crucial roles in altering or controlling the electrochemical, mechanical, magnetic, electrical, and catalytic properties of perovskite materials. Point-defect models have been used successfully to explain oxygen nonstoichiometry in perovskites, including (La1−xSrx)FeO3−δ,289 (La1−xCax)AlO3−δ,290 LaMnO3+δ,291 LaMO3−δ (M = Mn, Fe, Co),292 and (La1−xSrx)CrO3−δ.293

4.1.1. Cation site substitution. 
Ionic substitution at the A and/or B sites is the most widely practised strategy for modification of the properties of perovskites, but the number and type of systematic trends that have been identified are very limited. More broadly, the guiding principles for the relevant considerations are provided in Section 3.4. However, another tool is crystal field theory, which describes the concepts of metal ions and their ligands, how the ligands coordinate with the metal ions, and the stability of these coordinations.
As shown in Fig. 13, metal ions have five d orbitals, where dxy, dxz, and dyz (t2g orbitals) are oriented between the subscripted axes and dx2−y2 and dz2 (eg orbitals) are oriented on the subscripted axes.294 In the absence of a ligand, the d orbitals are of equal energy. However, when ligands coordinate octahedrally or tetrahedrally, the orbitals split, viz., become degenerate, where the three t2g and the two eg orbitals are triply and doubly degenerate, respectively. The splitting energy is defined in terms of Δo (octahedral splitting) or Δt (tetrahedral splitting). The coordinating ligands are divided into weak-field (small Δ, high electronegativity) and strong-field (large Δ, low electronegativity), which are in the general order known as the spectrochemical series, which is given in Table 9. This ranks the most common ligands as is shown in Table 9.
 Fig. 13  Energy levels of the degenerate d orbital in octahedral and tetrahedral coordinations. 
Table 9 Spectrochemical series according to ligand strengths and principal bonding












Fig. 13 shows that the splitting of Δ differs between the coordination polyhedra, where the different electrons are stabilised (−Δ) or destabilised (+Δ) by different amounts, depending on the nature of the metal, the valence of the metal, the coordinating ligand, the coordination number, and the shape of the coordination polyhedron. These considerations can provide indication of the relative stability of structures differentiated by these parameters.
Of more direct relevance is the impact of the spectrochemical series on the ionic radii. Since the metal ions in oxide (and halide) perovskites are coordinated by oxygen (and halides), these structures involve weak field ligands. This differentiation can be helpful because d4 to d7 metals coordinated by weak-field ligands favour high-spin states, which are alternatives to the ionic radii from those of low-spin states (d1, d2, d8 to d10).295
4.1.1.1. A-Site substitution. 
The partial substitution of A-site cations is an established approach to improve the properties of perovskites in terms of decreasing the operating temperature,296,297 development of the active catalysts,298 and boosting the energy and power densities of batteries through enhancement of the electrical conductivity299 and cathodic overpotential.300 Defect generation by A-site substitution can affect functionality through various means, including introduction of active surface sites,3 facilitation of redox of B-site cations,133 creation of charge-compensating B-site cation vacancies (donor doping), creation of charge compensating oxygen vacancies (acceptor doping),134 reduction of the activation energy for cation, oxygen, or vacancy diffusion,301 cation diffusion,302 and reduction of the oxygen vacancy formation energy.303
4.1.1.2. B-Site substitution. 
As mentioned previously, although B-site deficiency is not energetically favourable, there are many studies of substitution for this site.304 Tan et al.305 stated that the electronic factor variation induced by B-site substitution of Sr2(Sn1−xRux)O4 (0 ≤ x ≤ 1) is significant for isopropanol conversion. Sr2(Ru0.7Sn0.3)O4 showed the best yield of acetone at atmospheric pressure in the temperature range of 280–320 °C. This is ascribed to the optimal eg filling state of the B-site for appropriate overlapping between B-element σ*-antibonding and O 2pσ orbitals.
4.1.1.3. Simultaneous A and B site substitution. 
Substitution of both A and B sites has also been applied frequently in order to modify the properties of catalysts. Rousseau et al.306 synthesised a series of (La1−xSrx)(Co1−yFey)O3 (0 ≤ x ≤ 0.3, 0 ≤ y ≤ 0.5) by the combined citrate–(ethylenedinitrilo)tetraacetic acid (EDTA) complexing method, using La3+, Sr2+, Co2+, and Fe3+ as starting ions, and calcining at 800 °C in air. Upon equilibration, it would be expected that the final valences would be La3+, Sr2+, Co3+, and Fe3+. They observed that the substitution of La3+ by Sr2+ (x = 0.3) and of Co3+ by Fe3+ (y = 0.2) enhanced the catalytic performance for the oxidation of toluene. Laser Raman microspectroscopy revealed strong dynamic structural distortion through the Jahn–Teller effect, and Mössbauer spectroscopy demonstrated the presence of Fe4+ cations. These results again can be interpreted in terms of a dual charge compensation mechanism of (i) oxygen vacancy formation (ionic charge compensation) –  and (ii) IVCT – Co3+ + Fe3+ → Co2+ + Fe4+. Although not noted by the authors, the XPS data revealed that Fe doping caused a reduction in the Co valence, consistent with the IVCT mechanism. The active species was suggested to be O−, which was identified by XPS. If this is correct, then this species, which corresponds to the F+ colour centre ,307 raises the possibility that instead of charge compensation between  and , it is between  and . The optimal composition of (La0.7Sr0.3)(Co0.8Fe0.2)O3 + 5% Co (weight or atomic percent not specified, oxide assumed owing to calcining temperature) resulted in a decrease in T20% toluene conversion from 225° to 190 °C. However, without the 5% Co, the T20% conversion was at 243 °C. This observation suggests that the improved performance derived from heterojunction formation (Co oxide).


4.1.2. Anion substitution. 
Substitution in the oxygen sublattice in perovskites is limited to the Ruddlesden–Popper phases, which is discussed subsequently in Section 4.7.


4.1.3. Charge-compensating oxygen vacancies. 
Charge-compensating anion site vacancies, as well as intrinsic anion site vacancies, in perovskites are typical 0D point defects. They exhibit the capacity to adsorb reactant molecules and to form reactive oxygen species (ROS), including the radicals ˙O−, ˙O2−, and ˙O3−. Consequently, oxygen vacancies are important active sites for catalytic reactions.223 Oxygen vacancies are readily introduced into perovskites through various mechanisms, such as annealing in reducing atmospheres. Reducing or oxidising conditions can establish hypostoichiometric (LaMnO3−δ) or hyperstoichiometric (LaMnO3+δ) oxygen concentrations, respectively. However, it is common to generate these stoichiometries in perovskites by p-type acceptors (La3+(Mn1−x3+Cux2+)O3−δ2−) or n-type donors (La3+(Mn1−x3+Zrx4+)O3+δ2−), respectively. Using these approaches, modification of the catalytic efficiencies of many perovskites has been investigated through A-site and B-site doping and A- and B-site codoping.
However, another potential source of oxygen vacancies is the grain boundaries, which exhibit terminating dangling bonds associated with enhanced concentrations of both cation and oxygen vacancies,308 which are charge-compensated by the adjacent space charge layer. In this case, these defects do not derive from a chemical mechanism but a microstructural one. Therefore, this requires reporting of the relevant parameters, including surface area, although the grain size is often reported as an alternative. Further, it is well known that oxygen vacancies can also form irregular clusters as well as ordered arrays,143 which can affect the catalytic properties significantly.
O2 temperature-programmed desorption (O2-TPD) is usually used to determine the nature of oxygen species and hence the stoichiometries of defective perovskites. Through examination of the processes of oxygen transfer, three types and amounts of oxygen species can be detected by O2-TPD, which are chemically adsorbed oxygen (O2−), lattice oxygen (O2−), and adsorbed oxygen on the oxygen vacancy (O−).4 Further, XPS is also used to identify oxygen species but its accuracy in quantitative analysis is limited owing to the variability of sample morphologies. More sensitive techniques that are applied for such analyses include the synchrotron methods XAS, XANES, and EXAFS.
4.1.3.1. Organic oxidation. 
Luo et al.169 used a sol–gel method to synthesise what was considered to be B-site substituted La(Co1−xPx)O3 with phosphorus, followed by calcining in air at 500 °C. The source of phosphorus was ammonium dihydrogen phosphate. Abdel-Kader et al.309 studied the thermal decomposition of this material and observed its decomposition at ∼180 °C to release NH3, followed by continuous weight losses up to 600 °C, which were attributed to evolution of H2O. Further work by Pardo et al.310 clarified the matter by reporting that only nitrogenous species were released up to 430 °C; a very significant weight loss that was initiated at this temperature was not characterised. This point was elucidated by Su et al.,311 who used differential scanning calorimetry (DSC) to indicate the release of P2O5 and H2O at 430 °C. It is well known that P2O5 develops high vapour pressures at temperatures >200 °C, which cause a serious risk of mass loss by volatilisation. Consequently, it is possible that Luo et al.169 failed to incorporate phosphorus in their samples. Assuming that phosphorus was incorporated, further consideration of the nature of its incorporation is relevant. The ionic radius of sixfold-coordinated Co3+ is 0.075 nm (it would be expected to be in high spin as its electronic configuration is d6, as explained in Section 4.1.1) and that of the common valences of P3+ and P5+ is 0.058 and 0.052 nm, respectively. According to Hume-Rothery's rules for substantial solid solubility, the ionic size mismatches of 23% and 31% as well as the valence mismatch of 2 for P5+ (the electronegativities are relatively close) suggest that the potential for substitution can be questioned. Consequently, alternative mechanisms for phosphorus incorporation must be considered. One is A-site substitution, which is unlikely owing to the very large ionic radius of La3+ (0.1172 nm)287 and the valence difference. The second mechanism is interstitial solid solubility, which is feasible owing to the small ionic radii of P3+ and P5+. The third mechanism is anion substitution, which is also quite likely since the formation of phosphate glasses is well known.312 These points being made, it is also possible that phosphorus is insoluble in LaCoO3.
Luo et al.169 reported lattice expansion, suggesting phosphorus incorporation. However, phosphorus substitution for Co3+ would be expected to cause lattice contraction from size considerations. Further, the X-ray diffraction (XRD) peak shifts cannot be considered definitive because the authors did not use an internal standard or Rietveld refinement. The authors also concluded phosphorus incorporation on the basis of an XPS peak at 133.1 eV. This identification was based on the work of Wang et al.,313 who identified an XPS peak at 133.3 eV, which was attributed to surface-passivating phosphorus. Further, the standard XPS database of the National Institute of Standards and Testing (NIST) reveals that the peak locations for the intermediate and final reaction products (H3PO4, H4P2O7, HPO3, and P2O5311) exhibit XPS peaks only in the range 134.9–135.6 eV. In conclusion, the peak at 133.1 eV is unlikely to indicate P incorporation but rather more likely to demonstrate its redeposition on the surface following P2O5 volatilisation.
Luo et al.169 reported XPS and XANES data showing the presence of both Co2+ and Co3+ and attributed this to the high electronegativity of P relative to that of Co, thereby causing electron transfer to P. This is a driving force for the IVCT, which can be expressed as P5+ + 2Co2+ → P3+ + 2Co3+. IVCT does not alter defect formation or annihilation otherwise; it is a mechanism of structural stabilisation.98 However, Luo et al.169 suggested that phosphorus incorporation resulted in the formation of oxygen vacancies owing to the lattice distortion deriving from B-site (MO6) substitution to form the PO5 coordination polyhedron. However, structural distortion would occur from the introduction of any vacancy or interstitial. More convincing were the TPD data, which showed the presence of lattice oxygen vacancies. These were attributed to Co3+ → Co2+ reduction, which contradicts both the suggestion that this resulted from electronegativity differences and the corresponding IVCT reaction, which require Co oxidation, not reduction. Returning to the four possible mechanisms for phosphorus incorporation and the resultant formation of oxygen vacancies:
• A-Site substitution: this is unlikely owing to the ionic size and valence differences between La3+ and P5+.
• Interstitial solid solubility: the relevant representation of defect relations (assuming ionic charge compensation) would not involve oxygen vacancy formation:
• Co vacancy: 
• O interstitial: 
• Anion substitution: the relevant representation of the defect relations (assuming ionic charge compensation), where phosphorus acts as P3−, would involve oxygen vacancy formation:
• O substitution: 
• Phosphorus insolubility: the relevant elaborated defect equilibria (assuming ionic charge compensation) would involve oxygen vacancy formation:
Cation deficiency: 
In conclusion, examination of the published literature and consideration of the experimental data indicate that (i) phosphorus solid solubility is unlikely, and its volatilisation resulted in its removal from the composition and (ii) the stoichiometric compensation for phosphorus substitution created B-site hypostoichiometry, which was charge compensated by oxygen vacancies.
4.1.3.2. Carbon monoxide oxidation. 
High levels of ambient carbon monoxide represent significant health and environmental hazards, so many methods have been used to attempt to minimise the concentration of CO.314 Catalytic CO oxidation uses catalysis to oxidise toxic CO gas into nontoxic CO2.315,316 CO is present commonly due to sources such as industrial processes, vehicle exhaust emission,317 gaseous wastes in the petrochemical industry,318 by-product of ethanol and other fuel syntheses, and production of H2 in proton exchange membrane (PEM) fuel cells.319 In addition, owing to the uncomplicated reaction step of CO oxidation to CO2, this reaction has been used extensively as a probe in other complex oxidation reactions.
CO has been used commonly as a reducing agent to create oxygen vacancies in perovskites and their parent structural group of spinels, such as LaCoO3,320 Mn2O3,321,322 (La0.6Sr0.4)MnO3,323 Co3O4,324 and Co3−xMnxO4.325 Xiao et al.326 reported that, relative to bulk LaCoO3, the increased surface area of porous LaCoO3 provided more accessible active Co sites, which improved the oxidising capacity and resulted in enhanced catalytic reactivity towards CO oxidation. From the temperatures of the lattice oxygen desorption of the determined O2-TPD spectrum, it was inferred that lattice oxygen is more active in porous LaCoO3 than in bulk LaCoO3.326 When an oxygen vacancy in a perovskite is filled, it can dissociate O2 into atomic O,327 thereby facilitating the CO + O → CO2 reaction. The role of oxygen vacancies in perovskites in facilitating CO oxidation has been demonstrated in many studies that examined the dependence of the CO oxidation in terms of the oxygen vacancy concentration.320,321,323,328 Consequently, the deliberate creation of oxygen vacancies in perovskites has become a standard strategy to attempt to increase the catalytic activity for CO oxidation.329
4.1.3.3. Carbon dioxide reforming. 
The carbon dioxide reforming of methane can produce syngas with a high hydrogen/carbon monoxide ratio according to the reaction CO2 + CH4 ↔ 2H2 + 2CO (ΔHo298 = 247 kJ mol−1, ΔG° = 61770–67.32T kJ mol−1330), which can be used effectively in the industrial production of many oxygenated compounds, including acetic acid, dimethyl ether, aldehydes, alcohols, etc.331 The majority of hydrogen produced by methane reforming is consumed in the production of ammonia332 and is used for different hydrogenation processes in oil refining and food production. Therefore, this reaction has a critical environmental footprint since both methane and carbon dioxide contribute to the greenhouse effect.333 Consequently, converting these gases into valuable raw materials can reduce significantly CO2 and CH4 emissions in the atmosphere.334 Many research groups have investigated perovskites for CO2 reforming, e.g., La(NixFe1−x)O3 (0 ≤ x ≤ 1),335 (La1−xCax)(Al0.7Ni0.3)O3−δ,336 (SrxLa1−x)(Al0.7Ni0.3)O3−δ,336 (MxLa1−x)(Al0.7Ni0.3)O3−δ (M = Li, Na, K),337 LaM(AlxNi1−x)O3 (M = Pt, Pd, Ru, Rh, Ir),338 and La(MoxV1−x)On.339 These studies have shown that the catalytic activities can be enhanced by high surface areas rich in oxygen vacancies and that low-temperature operation is advantageous.340–345
Gómez-Cuaspud et al.346 fabricated the (La0.8Sr0.2)(Fe0.8Cr0.2)O3 perovskite by a polymerisation method, and the oxygen species adsorbed on the oxygen vacancies were detected by XPS. The oxygen vacancies that formed in (La0.8Sr0.2)(Fe0.8Cr0.2)O3 acted as active sites for the methane reforming reaction by adsorbing reactants, triggering the formation of intermediates, and dissociating products. Shetty et al.347 studied A-site substitution of LaCoO3 with Sr and observed efficient stabilisation of Co0 (from H2 reduction) and Co2+ active sites, which improved the hydrogenation performance of the C–O bonds. They found that the prevalence of Co0 sites influenced the C–C hydrogenolysis process at high temperature and that the Co2+ sites, which were associated with oxygen vacancies, showed extraordinary reactivity for C–O hydrogenolysis at low temperature.
During the reforming of alkanes over the La(Fe0.9Ni0.1)O3 perovskite, it was found that abundant oxygen vacancies facilitated the activation of CO2 and that metal sites promoted the activation of C–C and C–H bonds, resulting in good catalytic performance, anti-coking, and durability.188
4.1.3.4. Carbon dioxide reduction. 
From an environmental and economic point of view, closing the loop of carbon recycling through the catalytic reduction of CO2 into fuels and chemicals, such as methanol, hydrocarbons, formic acid, and ethylene, is important.348–350 However, owing to the high molecular stability of carbon dioxide (CO bond energy = 806 kJ mol−1), its chemical conversion is challenging, so effective catalysts are required.351
The oxygen vacancies in perovskite-type mixed oxides (A- and B-site substitution) have recently been shown to be active sites for the chemisorption of CO2 owing to the strong adsorption affinity of oxygen vacancies for nonpolar CO2, which is the basis for the high-temperature conversion of CO2 into useful products.352 Wang et al.351 prepared three-dimensional draped porous Ni-loaded Co3O4 (Ni/3DDP-Co3O4) catalysts, as shown in Fig. 14a, as the basis for low-cost transition-metal-based catalysts for CO2 reduction. The catalyst consisted of Ni clusters uniformly coated on the 3DDP-Co3O4 support, as shown in Fig. 14b–f. It was found that 3DDP-Co3O4 provided a large surface area, which was suitable for accommodation of Ni nanoclusters, facilitating Ni–Co interaction, and accommodating oxygen vacancies. Reduction by H2 caused electron transfer from Ni to 3DDP-Co3O4 and consequent oxygen vacancy formation, as shown in Fig. 14g, which was responsible for increased catalytic activity for CO2 reduction and CH4 selectivity, as shown in Fig. 14h.351
 Fig. 14  (a) Schematic of the role of oxygen vacancies in three-dimensional draped porous Ni-loaded Co3O4 (Ni/3DDP-Co3O4), (b and c) TEM images of the catalyst, (d–f) EDS mapping of the catalyst, (g) comparison of the relative O1, O2, O3, and O4 levels on the surface of reduced (R) catalysts (O1, O2, O3, and O4 represent lattice oxygen, oxygen in hydroxyl groups, oxygen vacancies, and oxygen in water molecules, respectively), and (h) CH4 and CO selectivity (3DDP-Co3O4 = Ni-free Co3O4 catalyst, Ni/3DDP-NCO = Ni-loaded Ni-substituted (Ni0.5Co2.5)O4 catalyst, Ni/3DDP-Co3O4 = Ni-loaded Co3O4 catalyst) (reproduced from ref. 351 with permission from John Wiley & Sons). 
4.1.3.5. Catalytic nitrogen oxide (NOx) decomposition. 
NOx are a major cause of photochemical smog and acid rain,353,354 and so strategies for the reduction of NOx emissions have been under intensive development.355 For example, the removal of NOx from mobile sources, NOx storage-reduction (NSR), and three-way catalysts (TWC) have been investigated. Selective catalytic reduction (SCR) and selective non-catalytic reduction (SNCR) have also been used to remove NOx gas from fixed sources.356–359 Although the direct decomposition of NOx into N2 and O2 (2NO → N2 + O2, ΔGo298 = −86.6 kJ mol−1) has high negative Gibbs free energies,360 the respective reaction kinetics are slow (ΔHo298 = 90.2 kJ mol−1)361 and they have high activation energies (∼335 kJ mol−1).362 While many different catalyst materials have been studied with the aim of resolving these problems, perovskites have emerged as lead candidates for NOx decomposition catalysts owing to their flexible compositions and redox characteristics.363
Liu et al.223 showed that NO was readily activated by oxygen vacancies by directly correlating the volume of NO adsorbed with the oxygen vacancy concentration in (La2−xRbx)CuO4−δ perovskites. Partial replacement of La by Rb increased the Cu2+ → Cu3+ oxidation and formed oxygen vacancies. In effect, this process involved the two simultaneous mechanisms of charge compensation mentioned previously: (i) oxygen vacancy formation (ionic charge compensation) –  and (ii) IVCT – La3+ + Cu2+ → La2+ + Cu3+. The authors used in situ diffuse reflection infrared Fourier transformed (DRIFT) spectroscopy and TPD of NO coupled with mass spectrometry to show the dependence between the amount of Rb+ substitution, adsorption capacity of NO, and .223
Complementary results were obtained by Zhao et al.,364 who showed that NO dissociated at the oxygen vacancies of the (La0.1Sr0.9)NiO3 perovskite, which was suggested to exhibit two active sites in the form of the low-valent metal ions (Ni2+) and oxygen vacancies. The relevant redox reaction involved , which suggests two active sites as well as the liberation of O2. The authors also suggested that the oxygen vacancies increased the mobility of lattice oxygen, which not only promoted the NO redox reaction but also generation of active sites.364
4.1.3.6. Soot combustion. 
Soot, which is a fine black or brown powder produced by the incomplete combustion of hydrocarbons, is a particulate pollutant that causes environmental and health hazards365 and so its removal has become a pressing safety issue.366 Catalytic soot oxidation is a very effective technique to reduce soot particle emissions using perovskite catalysts such as (Sr1−xKx)TiO3,365 La(CoxFe1−x)O3,367 (La1−xKx)(Co1−yFey)O3,368 (La1−xKx)MnO3,369 (La2−xRbx)CuO4−δ,223 (La1−xCsx)CoO3,370 and (La1.8K0.2)(Cu0.9V0.1)O4.371
Sun et al.372 prepared a hybrid core–shell material comprised of cobalt metal nanoparticles with the surface of layered (Pr0.5Ba0.5)(Mn0.9Co0.1)O3 perovskite, which exhibited high catalytic activity for soot combustion. The Co nanoparticles and oxygen vacancies in the defective perovskite exhibited synergism to activate O2 and form adsorbed ROS (O2−, O22−, O2−), which are known to be electrophilic. It was considered that the strengthening of C–O bonds and the breakage of C–C bonds underpinned the catalytic activity for soot oxidation.
Recently, Tang et al.167 produced a (La1−xKx)CoO3/γ-Al2O3/cordierite catalytic convertor and observed that the catalytic performance for soot combustion was improved owing to the formation of Co4+, high , and increased lattice oxygen mobility. As in the case of (La2−xRbx)CuO4−δ perovskites,223 the same charge compensation mechanisms are likely to have occurred: (i) oxygen vacancy formation (ionic charge compensation) –  and (ii) IVCT – La3+ + Co3+ → La2+ + Co4+. The catalytic convertor exhibited a 90% conversion rate at 390 °C, and the selectivity for carbon dioxide was 99.8%.167
Wang et al.373 conducted a study of soot and NOx removal using the (La1−xKx)CoO3 perovskite, and the results showed that the amount of adsorbed oxygen and the  increased with increasing K substitution. Again, the existence of oxygen vacancies was considered to increase the mobility of lattice oxygen and accelerate the generation of more oxygen vacancies; the oxygen vacancies also enhanced the activation of NO on the catalytic surface. Therefore, the catalytic activity was applicable simultaneously to both soot and NOx removal, yielding a CO2 selectivity of 98.4% and a NO to N2 conversion rate of 34.6%.373
The reported catalytic activities as a function of temperature for various perovskite and spinel catalysts are summarised in Table 10.
