4.2. 1D linear defects
Edge and screw dislocations, which are 1D linear defects,9 tend to form during crystal growth382 and upon stress application.383 They tend to occur simultaneously and, in fact, often appear as mixtures of the two types. These defects are illustrated schematically in Fig. 11. These defects are found in the bulk,384 2D and nanoscale structures,385 nanowires,386 and thin-films.387 These dislocations can have an impact on the performance of materials used in catalysis,388 solid oxide fuel cells,389 sensors,390 Li-ion batteries,391,392 ferroelectrics,393 photovoltaics and thermoelectrics,394 and optical devices.395 Dislocations have also been shown to improve the electrochemical performance by accelerating ion conduction (8Y-ZrO2/SrTiO3).396 Strain engineering applied to thin films, e.g., LaAlO3/SrTiO3,397 through lattice mismatch is used widely to manipulate and generate high concentrations of dislocations.
Szot et al.398 explained the nature of dislocations in SrTiO3 in terms of two potential mechanisms based on the intercalation of a half-plane of either SrO or TiO2. The half-planes alter the local atomic stacking arrangement, interatomic spacings, residual stress distribution, and chemical environment. The last of these effectively can result in enrichment in Sr or Ti.
Marrocchelli et al.399 illustrated the application of atomic simulations to show how edge dislocations affect the chemical properties of SrTiO3 through the defect equilibria and oxide ion transport. They found that the core of the dislocation was associated with undercoordinated oxygen ions (∼3 instead of 6), thereby creating oxygen vacancies of formation energies substantially lower than that of the intrinsic oxygen vacancies. When the concentration of the defects in the core of the dislocation was low, the accumulated oxygen vacancies increased the oxygen diffusion coefficient.
Ryu et al.400 demonstrated the use of metal seed-mediated growth in order to incorporate Ag into flaky mixed manganese oxides using an approach summarised in Fig. 15a and b. The resultant Ag2MnO4 nanoplates were highly defective, as shown by the TEM images, which revealed ordered (striped) Ag and Mn defects (Fig. 15c) and edge dislocations (Fig. 15d and e). While these defective nanostructures were highly stable, they were also found to be catalytically active for the ORR in alkaline media owing to high concentrations of active sites for ion adsorption, high electron-transfer rates, high electrical conductivity, and low electrode resistance.
 Fig. 15  (a and b) Schematic diagrams of synthesis and defect formation, and (c–e) TEM images of silver manganate nanoplates with defects (reproduced from ref. 400 with permission from Nature Publishing Group). 
4.3. 2D planar defects
2D planar defects, the main types of which are summarised in Section 3.3.3, essentially represent interfaces, which are well known to be concentrated sources of defects.148,149 Consequently, in principle, they may have the potential to be beneficial to catalytic performance. However, when they are of coarse scale, as in the case of surfaces and grain boundaries, they may have the opposite effect because the associated space charge layer effectively obstructs charge carrier diffusion.
The terminology to define interfaces is given in terms of the extent of lattice mismatch:401
• Coherent: when the lattice spacings of two phases match perfectly or are sufficiently similar so that the strain is low enough to avoid the formation of dislocations.
• Semicoherent: when the lattice mismatch (viz., atomic mismatch strain) is in the range ∼5–25%, resulting in the formation of periodic dislocations.
• Incoherent: when the mismatch is >25% (i.e., a grain boundary).
Mariano et al.402 studied the electroreduction of CO2 to CO on films of polycrystalline Au with a large grain size of ∼200 μm. By annealing the films to produce larger grains, they reduced the relative proportion of the catalytically active grain boundary area, thus monotonically decreasing the electroreduction current density. Other studies have confirmed this linear relationship.403,404
4.4. 3D volumetric defects
3D defects have been mentioned previously in Section 3.3.4 and can be divided into the following categories:
• Negative large-scale defects: pores, pits, microcracks, and cracks
• Positive large-scale defects: inclusions and order–disorder transformations
• Negative small-scale defects: vacancy clusters
• Positive small-scale defects: cation clusters
Eom et al.405 reported the existence of oxygen vacancy clusters and their orientation along specific crystallographic directions in SrTiO3 thin films fabricated by pulsed laser deposition (PLD) under 1.3 × 10−8 kPa vacuum. Photoemission spectroscopy (PES) studies showed that annealing (200–500 °C under 1.33 × 10−3 kPa vacuum) resulted in the presence of Ti4+, Ti3+, and Ti2+, the last of which was associated with the presence of (001) ordered oxygen vacancy clustering in these highly reduced SrTiO3−δ films. The DFT studies of Cuong et al.406 support the conclusion that such linearly oriented oxygen vacancy clusters form in SrTiO3.
4.5. Exsolution and segregation
There are six main mechanisms that can be applied in order to impose nonstoichiometry by thermal and/or atmospheric processes during processing, which can result in the formation of heterojunctions, cocatalysts, lattice defects, and/or surface catalytically active sites:
• Normal exsolution: ejection of solute ions upon cooling such that the solute concentration exceeds the solubility limit at room temperature.
• Redox exsolution: ejection of solute ions upon cooling as a result of cation redox and associated alteration of the solubility limit.
• Schottky exsolution: ejection of lattice ions upon Schottky defect formation and subsequent migration to the surface.
• Normal segregation: diffusion of dopant (and impurity) ions from the lattice to the surface owing to structural stress imposed during heating.
• Mismatch segregation: diffusion of dopant (and impurity) ions from the lattice to the surface owing to structural stress imposed by lattice mismatch between the film and the substrate.
• Redox segregation: diffusion of dopant (and impurity) ions from the lattice to the surface owing to cation valence change and associated alteration of structural stress imposed during heating.
Exsolution has emerged as an innovative platform to synthesise novel nanostructures of high catalytic activity and reliability for many applications, including fuel cells,184 CO2 splitting and hydrogen production,407 CO2 reduction,408 CO oxidation,409 photocatalysis,410 CH4 conversion to syngas,411 membrane separation,412 methane steam reforming and CO methanation,413 chemical looping H2 production,414 CO2 methanation,348 and (co)electrolysis of H2O, CO2, and/or CH4.415 Over the last decade, researchers generally have focussed on B-site substitution owing to the combined effects of oxygen vacancy formation and IVCT. However, as suggested by Table 6, further benefits can be gained by A-site substitution, as indicated in studies on (La0.8Sr0.2)(ScxMn1−x)O3−δ,416 and (La0.7Sr0.3)(TiyCr1−y)O3−δ.417
Neagu et al.418 reported normal exsolution from A-site deficient (LaxSr1−3x/2)(Ti0.94M0.06)O3−δ (M = Mn, Fe, Ni, Cu, Ce) through cyclic redox at 930–1100 °C under 5 vol% H2 or air. The exsolution of M ions resulted in surface restructuring, which facilitated a significant decrease in the temperature required for steam electrolysis potential, which varied depending on the type of the exsolved ion.
Nishihata et al.419 pioneered the redox exsolution of metal nanoparticles from perovskite lattices loaded on a honeycomb catalytic convertor. The authors examined reversible Pd exsolution from La(Fe1−x−yCoxPdy)O3 (x = 0.38, y = 0.05) exposed to alternating redox conditions from exhaust gas at 900 °C of different air-to-fuel ratios. Subsequent investigators have examined other perovskites and metals exsolved by redox, including Rh from La(RhxFe1−x)O3 (x = 0.05),420 Rh from A-site-deficient (LaxCa0.8−x)(Ti1−yRhy)O3 (x = 0.43, y = 0.06),421 and Fe2+/3+ from (La0.6Sr0.4)FeO3−δ).422
De Souza et al.225 used atomistic defect calculations according to Mott–Littleton principles to consider Schottky and Frenkel pair formation in (La1−xSrx)MnO3. They argued that the formation of oxygen vacancies precipitated the formation of isolated charge-compensating cation vacancies. Hence, their conclusion was that while both isolated La and Mn ions are exsolved, neither intrinsic Schottky nor Frenkel pairs are likely to form, thus necessitating electronic charge compensation. In contrast, exsolution from Schottky pair formation has been demonstrated in studies of Co from Sr(CoxTi1−x)O3−δ (x = 0.25),183 Ni from (LaxSr1−x)(NiyTi1−y)O3−δ) (x = 0.2, y = 0.1),423 and Ni from A-site-deficient (LaxSr0.8−x)(NiyTi1−y)O3−δ (x = 0.4, y = 0.06)424 The last study revealed that the diffusion mechanism was B-site hopping.
Both A-site and B-site cations in perovskites can undergo normal segregation and concentrate on the outer few monolayers of the surface, oxidising and forming secondary phases on the surface.425 The segregation of A-site cations has been reported in perovskites, including Sr2+ segregation in (La0.7Sr0.3)MnO3,426 (SrxSm1−x)CoO3,427 (SrxLa1−x)MnO3 (x ≤ 0.5),375 and (La0.6Sr0.4)(Fe0.8Co0.2)O3−δ;428 Ba2+ segregation in GdBaCo2O5+δ;429 and Pb2+ segregation in (La0.65Pb0.35)MnO3.430
Pecchi et al.431 investigated the effect of B-site substitution in La(NixFe1−x)O3 with Ni (x = 0.1, 0.2, 0.3) on the catalytic performance for combustion of acetyl acetate and ethanol. They observed that the intrinsic activity was increased substantially upon Ni substitution but decreased with complete replacement of Fe by Ni. By analysing the changes in structure and catalytic activity, they concluded that the normal segregation of Ni occurred only with partial substitution with x > 0.1. Although the authors referred to a cooperative effect between the perovskite and the NiO that formed at the surface, this in effect is a heterojunction.
Jalili et al.432 prepared (La0.7Sr0.3)MnO3 thin films on (001) SrTiO3 and LaAlO3 substrates and complemented these data with DFT simulations. They concluded that oxygen vacancy formation resulted in the application of a tensile stress on the film by the substrate, which resulted in normal segregation of Sr. Sun et al.433 observed another fundamental cause of normal segregation, which is the stress associated with the presence of linear defects in the form of edge dislocations.
Lee et al.434 synthesised M-doped (Ln1−xMx)O3 (Ln = La, Sm; M = Ca, Sr, Ba) in the form of dense, epitaxial, thin films on (100) SrTiO3 substrates; complementary DFT simulations were also done. The authors observed both (i) normal segregation of the A-site dopant cations resulting from ionic size mismatch between dopant and lattice ions and (ii) mismatch segregation of the B-site dopant and lattice ions resulting from the strain between the substrate and the thin film.
Kwon et al.435 synthesised (Pr1−xBax)(Mn2−yMy)O5+δ layered perovskite powders (M = Fe, Co, Ni; x = 0.5, y = 0.3) by calcining at 950 °C in air. Reducing conditions were imposed by heating at 800 °C in humidified H2 (3 vol% H2O). The H2 treatment resulted in the reduction of the B-site cations, including Mn, Fe, Co, and Ni. These transition metals undergo reduction relatively readily at this temperature, leading to an increase in the ionic radius. These size increases precipitated the segregation of all four ions, although the authors referred to the process as exsolution.
4.6. Surface reconstruction
Surface reconstruction is the modification of surface microstructures during heating through the processes of436
• Point defect generation: cation and/or anion vacancies
• Surface area minimisation: faceting
• Crystal growth artifact formation: steps and kinks
As suggested in Section 4.5 on exsolution and segregation, the surfaces of perovskites can be modified by these alterations in stoichiometry. Further, surfaces are well known as sinks for structural defects,308 which differentiate them from the bulk. Also, during conventional heating, the presence of a thermal gradient within powder compacts results in grain size and bulk density variations from the bulk to the surface. Similarly, when heating in air, the bulk and the underside of the compact are starved of oxygen, which establishes redox gradients through the solid. These and other factors require that the surface of a solid be considered as possessing different characteristics compared to the bulk.
The detection of surface reconstruction remains a challenge in catalysis, which derives largely from applicability and sensitivity limitations of current techniques for the characterisation of surfaces in conditions that duplicate the operating conditions for catalysis.437 Some resolution of this problem has been addressed by the development of what are known as operando techniques, which are in situ microscopy and spectroscopy methods of analysis.
Near-ambient-pressure STEM, near-ambient SEM, in situ TEM, and infrared spectroscopy have provided unambiguous evidence of surface reconstruction.438 Also, DFT can be used to calculate activation energies for reconstructed surfaces.439 In general, DFT should be sufficient to cover considerations of the surface, subsurface, and bulk142 because it is capable of examining to a depth of 10–20 atomic layers.440 Time-resolved in situ XAS under practical operating conditions has been used to obtain complex local electron configurations and highly active oxygen evolution reaction (OER) data (Ba0.5Sr0.5)(Co0.8Fe0.2)O3−δ).441
Many studies have focussed on elucidating the effects of surface reconstruction on the catalytic performance before and after operation, including N2O decomposition (La(Fe0.8Co0.2)O3),442 OER (SrTiO3,439 SrIrO3,443 (Ba0.5Sr0.5)(Co0.8Fe0.2)O3−δ,441 and La2LiIrO6444), water adsorption (BaTiO3445 and SrTiO3446), CO oxidation ((LaxSr1−x)CoO3−δ, x = 0.3–0.7),172 and ethanol dehydrogenation (SrTiO3).447
Lee et al.448 used in situ mass spectrometry (XAS-DRIFTS-MS) measurements under reaction conditions to examine the oxidation state and coordination number of surface ions. Using a hybrid reactor configuration, the oxidation state of the catalyst (determined by XAS), the types of adsorbates (DRIFTS), and the formed gas phase components were monitored simultaneously in a sub-second time frame. The authors used time-resolved synchrotron XAS to reveal the reversible surface restructuring of nanoparticulate Pd to PdOx, where PdOx catalysed the oxidative dehydrogenation of crotyl alcohol, but metallic Pd catalysed the decarbonylation of crotonaldehyde.
Dagdeviren et al.449 combined Auger electron spectroscopy (AES), nc-AFM, and low-energy electron diffraction (LEED) to examine the surface reconstruction of SrTiO3(100) single crystals, where it was observed that the surface reconstruction was reversible by atmospheric redox (997 °C in O2vs. 1.07 × 10−8 kPa vacuum). AES revealed the relative surface concentrations of all three ions, which allowed assessment of the stoichiometries and the nature of the terminations. The nc-AFM revealed the surface roughness and step heights. LEED was used to determine the structural crystallography and the periodicity of the surface ions.
Wang et al.172 synthesised a series of (LaxSr1−x)CoO3−δ (n = 0.3–0.7) perovskites for the study of CO oxidation. They used XPS and XAFS to examine the results of surface reduction using KBH4 solution to generate oxygen vacancies and the reconstructed surface enriched in segregated Sr. This enrichment increased the , which in turn increased the number of active sites as well as the surface oxygen exchange.
Polo-Garzon et al.450 used HAADF-STEM, LEIS, and DRIFTS to examine the surface reconstruction of SrTiO3 and its effect on CH4 combustion. The catalytic performance was observed to depend on both the composition and morphology in that the rate of CH4 combustion was proportional to the amount of Sr surface segregation and the active sites were Sr step surfaces formed by reconstruction. Fig. 16a shows an HAADF-STEM image of SrTiO3, with Sr surface steps apparent. Fig. 16b shows LEIS data revealing that Sr segregation resulted from annealing at 750 °C. Fig. 16c and d show that CH4 conversion and CO2 selectivity, respectively, were enhanced by surface Sr.
 Fig. 16  (a) HAADF-STEM image of SrTiO3, showing surface steps on grains, (b) LEIS compositional depth profiles (STO = HNO3 etched, Sr/STO = Sr impregnated and HNO3 etched, Sr/STO 750 °C = Sr impregnated, HNO3 etched, and annealed at 750 °C for 5 h), (c) effect of the Sr level on CH4 conversion, and (d) effect of the Sr level on CO2 selectivity (reproduced from ref. 450 with permission from the American Chemical Society). 
4.7. Ruddlesden–Popper phase defects
Unlike perovskites, whose point defects are limited to cation and anion vacancies, Ruddlesden–Popper phases exhibit both anion vacancies and interstitials.451 When the oxygen is hyperstoichiometric, these defects have the capacity to undergo oxygen ion diffusion, making them suitable in high-temperature solid oxide fuel cells (SOFCs) ((La1.2Sr0.8)MnO4+δ452 (La1.2Sr0.8)(Fe0.6Mn0.4)O4,453 (La0.75Sr0.25)(Cr0.5Mn0.5)O3−δ454 and La2NiO4+δ455) and sensors and ceramic membranes (LaSr(Fe0.75Ni0.25)O4456 and (Lan+1Nin)O3n+1 (n = 1, 2, and 3)457). Table 11 summarises the number of oxygen interstitials per Ruddlesden–Popper molecule that can be generated.

Ruddlesden–Popper phases also have been studied as electrode materials (Sr3Fe2O7−δ,458 LaSr3Fe3O10,7 and Sr3Fe2O7−δ459), oxygen storage/release materials (Sr3Fe2O7−δ460 and Sr3(Fe0.8Mn0.2)2O7−δ461), catalysts for NO decomposition (Sr3Fe2O7462) and CO2 reduction to produce CO ((La0.6Sr0.4)(Co0.7Mn0.3)O3158), as three-way catalysts (La(Fe1−xCux)O3 and La2CuO4191), and for their properties such as superconductivity ((La2−xSrx)CuO4,463 (La2−xSrx)NiO4±δ,464 La2CuO4.032,465 and (La1.85Sr0.15)CuO4466), magnetoresistance (La2CuO4467 and Sr3(Fe2−xCox)O7−δ468), and mixed ionic and electronic conductivity ((La1.9Sr0.1)NiO4+δ469 and Lan+1NinO3n+1470).
As indicated in Table 11, substitution by higher valence cations, especially at the A site, can be used to induce oxygen hyperstoichiometry and hence increase oxygen ion transport. However, Table 11 is based on ionic charge compensation, and electron charge compensation remains open as an alternative strategy, as in the case of La2NiO4+δ, which exhibits oxygen excess owing to the presence of Ni vacancies,471 the defect equilibria of which would be .
Huan et al.472 investigated Ruddlesden–Popper phases of the type An+1BnO3n+1 (A = La; B = Ni, Co, Mn, Cu; n = 1, 2, 3) for OER and ORR. The sample films were prepared by PLD at 600 °C under 1.33 × 10−3 kPa vacuum. The authors considered that reduction of the B-site cations enhanced lattice oxygen ion diffusion by hopping within the perovskite-like layer. Doping of these compositions with high-valence cations was considered to generate interstitial oxygen ions, which diffused in the rock salt layer. The observed high OER and ORR activities were attributed to high concentrations of interstitial oxygen and high ionic conductivity.
Conversely, Ruddlesden–Popper phases also have been studied for different applications due to their property of oxygen hypostochiometry, such as cathode materials for CO2 electrolysis in solid oxide electrolyte batteries (Co-loaded (La1.2Sr0.8)(Co0.4Mn0.6)O4)158 and anodes for solid oxide electrolysis ((La1.2Sr0.8)(Fe0.6Mn0.4)O4).157
Recently, Zhu et al.473 investigated perovskites, including single perovskite (SP) (La0.4Sr0.6)MnO3−δ, Ruddlesden–Popper (RP) (La0.4Sr1.6)MnO4−δ, and self-assembled heterostructure nanocomposite (SP-RP) (La0.4Sr1.05)MnOx, for peroxymonosulfate (PMS)-mediated phenol degradation, as shown in Fig. 17. Of these, the SP-RP (La0.4Sr1.05)MnO4−δ showed the best catalytic performance for phenol degradation. This was attributed to higher , stronger affinity of the highly positivity charged surface for the reactants, accelerated charge transfer between organics and PMS, reduced electron transfer distance, higher electron conduction efficiency, and non-release of radicals that would compete for oxidation with PMS. Consequently, all the oxidising strength of PMS was directed at organic oxidation by exchanging electrons with non-free-radical systems rather than using the catalyst in the redox cycle.
 Fig. 17  Mechanism of peroxymonosulfate (PMS) ternary activation and phenol oxidation on the (La0.4Sr0.6)MnO3−δ (SP) – (La0.4Sr1.05)MnO4−δ (RP) heterostructure nanocomposite catalyst (reproduced from ref. 473 with permission from the American Chemical Society). 
Rechargeable metal–air batteries have been developed Takeguchi et al.7 using Ruddlesden–Popper LaSr3Fe3O10. Fig. 18a shows OER and ORR performances at 60 °C for the charging (water electrolysis mode) and discharging reactions (fuel cell mode). These data revealed that the catalytic activity of Ruddlesden–Popper LaSr3Fe3O10 is significantly greater than that of the reference materials RuO2–IrO2474 and LaMnO3/LaNiO3.475 This was attributed to the readily labile oxygen ions, which were removed by heating at 800 °C under 1 × 10−2 to 1 × 102 kPa; charge compensation took place through Fe reduction.7
 Fig. 18  (a) Electrolysis mode (top black curve) and fuel-cell mode (bottom black curve) at 60 °C for Ruddlesden–Popper LaSr3Fe3O10 (reproduced from ref. 7 with permission from the American Chemical Society); TEM images of Ruddlesden–Popper LaFeO3 synthesised by (b) combustion (cs-LF), (c) sol–gel (sg-LF), and (d) coprecipitation (cp-LF); (e) atomistic space-filling model of LaFeO3; and (f) TEM simulated images of pristine LaFeO3 (reproduced from ref. 185 with permission from the American Chemical Society). 
Bornovski et al.185 studied the effect of the processing technique on the formation of stacking faults in otherwise stoichiometric Ruddlesden–Popper LaFeO3. Characterisation by TEM, EDS, and XPS, testing by CO-TPD, and simulations by DFT were used to explore the role of stacking faults, shown in Fig. 18b–f, in the adsorption and co-adsorption of CO, O, and O2 for CO oxidation. The authors suggested two possible mechanisms by which the stacking faults could modulate Co oxidation kinetics. The first case involves Sabatier's principle,476 where increased bonding energy between reactant and catalyst surface increases the reaction rate per site up to a maximum. The second case involves oxygen ion diffusion from the bulk to the surface of the Ruddlesden–Popper phase, where the anisotropic structure, which is capable of being both oxygen-deficient and oxygen-rich, enables the stacking faults to facilitate oxygen transfer.
Dama et al.477 investigated Ruddlesden–Popper Srn+1Tin−xNixO3n+1 (n = 1–3; x increased with increasing n) for CH4 steam and dry reforming using XRD, Raman, thermogravimetric analysis (TGA), and temperature-programmed reduction (TPR). The authors determined that the n value had a pervasive influence on all of the performance parameters, where increasing n was advantageous. They used the XRD data to establish, rather than assume, that Ni substitutional solid solubility occurred. Nickel substitution and charge-compensating oxygen vacancy formation influenced the catalytic activity for steam and dry reforming, which increased not only n but also x, thereby increasing the . It was considered that the oxygen vacancies were highly labile, which allowed them to diffuse from the bulk to the surface, thereby assisting in the removal of the formed coke. This Ruddlesden–Popper phase was also relatively stable in terms of its stability in steam, dry, or bi-reforming reactions.
4.8. Dion–Jacobson and Aurivillius phase defects
Owing to the large surface-to-volume ratios and distinct optical and electronic properties, the 2D layered Dion–Jacobson and Aurivillius phases and their derivatives show unique properties, including dielectric behaviour,478 colossal magnetoresistance,479 electronic/protonic-conductivity,480 luminescence,481 superconductivity,482 ionic conductivity,483 and photocatalysis.484 However, there appears to be no literature on the application of these phases in thermocatalysis. Therefore, they are not considered further.
5. Defect engineering of perovskite electrocatalysts
The recent demand for new technologies for energy conversion and environmental protection/remediation has driven developments in electrocatalytic research. The concept of electrocatalysis involves catalytic processes that occur at an electrode–electrolyte interface, where the electrode plays dual roles of electron donor/acceptor and catalyst.485 Therefore, an electrocatalyst must meet the requirements to perform two functions simultaneously. That is, it must (i) be conductive and so transfer electrons readily and (ii) effectively activate a reactant and catalyse an electrolytic reaction.
The most important electrocatalytic processes for the conversion of electrical energy to chemical energy are the OER and the ORR.486,487 These are essential processes for both energy conversion and its storage, and so they have broad applications in water splitting, metal–air batteries, and solar fuels.210,488,489 The rates of these reactions generally are the rate-limiting steps of these renewable technologies, but their generally sluggish kinetics require the application of high overpotentials in order to compensate.490 If devices designed to effect these reactions are to progress from laboratory to pilot-plant to industrial scale, then the development of efficient electrocatalysts represents a critical goal. At present, some noble materials, such as iridium and ruthenium, demonstrate excellent performance as OER electrocatalysts; platinum is known to be a very effective ORR electrocatalyst.491 However, the high cost of these metals is a serious hindrance to their application.492
Over the last decade, perovskites have attracted increasing attention as low-cost alternatives for electrocatalysts for OER and ORR owing to their tunable compositions, structures, and microstructures.493 A complementary aspect to addressing the demands of these technologies is an understanding of the mechanisms and pathways for OER and ORR, which can be developed through theoretical and experimental studies of electrocatalytic efficiency.

Fig. 19a–c illustrate a schematic of three proposed mechanisms for OER over a perovskite in an alkaline medium.494,495 The adsorbate mechanism is considered to be applicable in general to perovskites. The oxygen coupling mechanism accommodates situations that do not include the highly energetic hydroperoxide (–OOH) intermediates. The oxygen vacancy mechanism is similar to the oxygen coupling mechanism except that lattice oxygen vacancies are involved. All three mechanisms involve the same six processes:495 (i) OH− or H2O adsorption, (ii) intermediate formation that enhances charge transfer and catalysis (viz., activation), (iii) diffusion of surface ions and adsorbates, (iv) electron/hole and H+/OH− transfer to and from the surface, (v) O–O bond formation, and (vi) O2(g) release.
 Fig. 19  Proposed mechanisms for the OER over a perovskite in an alkaline medium: (a) adsorption mechanism, (b) oxygen coupling mechanism, and (c) oxygen vacancy mechanism (purple diamonds represent metal–ligand bonds) (reproduced from ref. 494 and 495 with permission from American Chemical Society); refined crystal structures of stoichiometric and nonstoichiometric CaMnO3−x: (d) stoichiometric CaMnO3.00, (e) CaMnO2.75, and (f) CaMnO2.50 (reproduced from ref. 303 with permission from the American Chemical Society). 
The ORR over perovskites has two pathways, which are the two-electron and the four-electron496 pathways:
Two-electron reactionsO2 + H2O + 2e− → HO2− + OH−
The peroxide ion can also be reduced to OH−:HO2− + H2O + 2e− → 3OH−
Alternatively, the peroxide ion can be disproportionated:HO2− → ½O2 + OH−
Four-electron reactionO2 + 2H2O + 4e− → 4OH−Generally, the four-electron pathway is more favourable as it is energetically more efficient.497
Du et al.303 examined the ORR and OER of a series of nonstoichiometric CaMnO3−δ (0 < δ ≤ 0.5) generated from 5 mol% H2/95 mol% Ar mixtures heated at 250–350 °C. The refined crystal structures are shown schematically in Fig. 19d–f. In the stoichiometric CaMnO3.00, B-site Mn4+ is coordinated by six oxygen ions to form MnO6 octahedra (Fig. 19d). For CaMnO2.75, the perovskite framework is retained, merely introducing oxygen vacancies (Fig. 19e). For CaMnO2.50, the MnO6 octahedra convert to MnO5 square pyramids (Fig. 19f). The authors’ experimental and DFT simulation work revealed that the highly defective CaMnO2.75 exhibited the best OER and ORR characteristics owing to the effects of enhanced oxygen activation and increased electrical conductivity deriving from Mn3+ → Mn2+ reduction and the formation of charge-compensating oxygen vacancies.
A summary of examples of the range of approaches that have been used to create defects in perovskite electrocatalysts and their applications are summarised in Table 12. With suitable defect engineering, the electrocatalytic activities of some perovskite catalysts can exceed those of the benchmark catalysts Pt, Ir and RuO2 owing to the ability to modify the crystal structure, increase the electrical conductivity, and generate greater densities of active sites.498 Successful defect engineering of perovskite electrocatalysts is aimed at the fabrication of devices of lower cost, higher efficiency, and greater stability, which will enable their implementation in many energy and environmental applications.

5.1. Point defects

5.1.1. Cation site substitution. 
In common with perovskite thermocatalysts, modification of the properties of electrocatalysts has been investigated many times. The principal differences between these two technologies are that electrocatalysts (i) do not have structures with oxygen excess, and (ii) have structures that can include simultaneous cation and anion substitution.
5.1.1.1. A-Site substitution. 
Hyodo et al.535 prepared a series of A-site substituted (Pr1−xMx)MnO3 (M = Ca, Sr, Ba; x = 0–1.0) and examined their electrocatalytic activities as electrodes. The active sites for the 4-electron ORR were Mn3+ ions. More importantly, their activities increased significantly with electron exchange between Mn3+–Mn4+ pairs. While this exchange could be driven by electronegativity difference between the ions, it also could involve charge compensation by the reaction , where the oxygen vacancy was generated by the acceptor doping of Pr3+ by rare earths (2+). Another alternative would be through the generation of an F+ colour centre through the reaction .
Perovskites, such as SrFeO3, CaFeO3, and BaFeO3, potentially can exhibit oxygen vacancy ordering in the form of channels.536 While these ordered anionic deficiencies have been suggested to be responsible for high catalytic activities, they have also been found to be detrimental to OER catalysis, which is critical to metal–air batteries. One strategy to prevent the formation of ordered vacancies in BaFeO3 is substitution. Chen and Ciucii537 explored A- and B-site substitutions with the aim of disordering the oxygen vacancies. They determined that donor dopants tended to suppress oxygen vacancy ordering while acceptor and neutral dopants tended to enhance it.
Xu et al.502 coprecipitated A-site-excess (La1−xSrx)1+yMnO3−δ (x = 0.2; y ≤ 0.10), followed by calcination at 650 °C in air. The authors also undertook DFT calculations for a (001) slab using a stoichiometry of x = 0.25 and y ≤ 0.08 (the higher value was selected for computational cost considerations). Acceptor doping of the A site (and an increase in its effect owing to excess doping) would be charge compensated by oxygen vacancy formation. However, the excess A-site dopants could be charge-compensated by B-site vacancies. However, it is also possible that the oxygen vacancies generated by A-site doping could be neutralised by oxygen interstitial formation, which is an alternative charge-compensation mechanism for A-site excess. If this is the case, then this would require charge balance through IVCT since the cation stoichiometry is fixed, but the valence ratio (3/3) is not maintained owing to excess doping of A sites but constant B sites. Hence, the decrease in valence at the A site from acceptor doping would be balanced by the increase in valence at the B site by Mn3+ → Mn4+ oxidation. The experimental compositions revealed enhanced OER and ORR kinetics, which were attributed to the A-site excess or B-site deficiency. The authors evaluated by DFT the free energy profile of each reaction step of the 4-electron OER at room temperature (27 °C) and 1 atm pressure in alkaline media. They considered four adsorbates, which were *O, *OO, *OH, and *OOH on Mn. The results, which are shown in Fig. 20a and b, indicate that the endothermic (uphill) steps require an overpotential to drive the OER and make the reaction exothermic (downhill). The key comparative point of these figures is that alteration of the A/B ratio required a different overpotential to alter the OER kinetics (without altering the mechanism). It also can be seen that, following the rate-limiting step, the d-band centre (red horizontal line) is shifted more closely to the EF (red dashed line), which is a result of the reduced [Mn4+]/[Mn3+] ratio. Since this ratio does not agree with the scenario suggesting oxygen vacancy-interstitial annihilation, the Mn redox would require a synthesis of valence change/balance according to .
 Fig. 20  Free energy diagrams of four-electron OER for (001) slabs: (a) (La0.75Sr0.25)MnO3 and (b) (La0.75Sr0.25)Mn0.92O3 surface (reproduced from ref. 502 with permission from the American Chemical Society). 
5.1.1.2. B-Site substitution. 
The B site is generally considered to be the active site for OER and ORR electrocatalysts.538 Sunarso et al.539 used sol–gel to synthesise LaMO3 (M = Cr, Mn, Fe, Co, Ni) and La(Ni1−xMx)O3 (where M = Cr, Mn, Fe, Co; x = 0.5), followed by calcining at 1000 °C in air. As shown in Fig. 21a, the ORR current densities varied, depending on the ion at the B site, in the order Cr < Fe < Ni < Mn < Co. The effects of B-site substitution are shown in Fig. 21b, which show a trend for the substituting ion in the order Fe < Co < Cr < Mn. These do not correlate with either the ionic radii or electronegativities. The authors suggested that the synergy between the two transition metals enhanced the activity owing to the redox reactions during ORR, which is to say IVCT according to, for example, Mn3+ + Fe3+ → Mn4+ + Fe2+. Determination of which combination corresponds to the oxidised or reduced state could be done by comparison of the free energies of reaction, viz., 3Mn2O3 ↔ 2Mn3O4 + ½O2 (Mn oxidation) vs. Fe2O3 ↔ 2FeO + ½O2 (Fe reduction). These electron transfer reactions between two transition metals are equivalent to the intrinsic ionisation shown in eqn (7).
 Fig. 21  ORR current densities: (a) LaMO3 and (b) La(Ni1−xMx)O3 (reproduced from ref. 539 with permission from the American Chemical Society). 
Zhang et al.540 used sol–gel to synthesise La(Ni1−xFex)O3 (x ≤ 0.6), which was calcined at 750 °C in air, as a cathode for metal–air batteries. The absence of secondary phases was considered to support the view that Fe incorporation had occurred. However, as XRD generally has a limit of detection of ∼1 vol%,98,216 this is not definitive. Although the authors did not report peak shifts, the crystallinity increased with the increasing dopant level, which suggests that Ni defects acted as nucleation sites that facilitated recrystallisation;308 detection of Fe by XPS confirms its presence but not its incorporation as it is possible that it is located on the surfaces owing to insolubility. The authors observed that the surface was enriched in Ni, viz., NiO, which is inactive for both OER and ORR and so it can block the surface active sites. However, the authors also stated that Fe suppressed the formation of NiO, which was beneficial to the OER and ORR. The effect is an overt example of IVCT according to Ni2+ + Fe3+ → Ni3+ + Fe2+. This reaction is confirmed by the XPS data, which show an increase in Ni valence and a decrease in Fe valence with increasing Fe doping.

Table 13 summarises studies of cation substitution at the A- and B-sites.
