5.1.1.3. Simultaneous A and B site substitution. 
Zhu et al.532 used solid-state synthesis to fabricate Pr(Ba1−xSrx)(Co1−xFex)O5+δ (x = 0.5), which was calcined at 1250 °C in air. The oxygen concentration was varied using post-treatment in an H2/Ar plasma. Although the  was not quantified, its formation was concluded on the basis of XRD peak shifts, which are not definitive since neither an internal standard nor Rietveld refinement was used; the XPS data showing Co3+ → Co2+ reduction; and the decrease in the lattice oxygen peak intensities in the O 1s XPS data. Although the role of A-site doping appears to be irrelevant since Sr is a neutral dopant for Ba, it could potentially cause structural distortion through valence and electronegativity differences. This could facilitate partial amorphisation and structural disorder, which create unsaturated terminal bonds associated with cation interstitials and anion vacancies. The authors reported that OER activity increased significantly with increasing , which was attributed to (i) alteration of the surface adsorption characteristics that activated the OER reaction path and (ii) improved decoupling of the proton and electron transfer processes.
Yan et al.559 used sol–gel to synthesise A-site-deficient (La1−xSrx)1−y(Mn1−yIry)O3 (x = 0.20, y = 0, 0.05), followed by calcining at 650 °C in air. Consideration of the ionic radii indicates that both dopant ions would be expected to cause expansion upon substitution. Incorporation of these was suggested by XRD peak shifts that indicated lattice expansion; again, these authors did not use an internal standard or Rietveld refinement. However, HAADF-EDS mapping confirmed the incorporation of Sr and Ir. This study provides a complement to previously discussed studies in that it reports A-site deficiency, rather than excess, and charge compensation would be required for substitution at the A-site but not the B-site. In this case, the strategy to generate oxygen vacancies is overt in that acceptor Sr substitution would be likely to generate oxygen vacancies, and the A-site deficiency would supplement the effect. However, XPS data for Mn revealed that Ir doping caused a decrease in Mn valence, which the authors suggested was charge compensated by oxygen vacancy formation. However, in light of the strong driving forces for oxygen vacancy formation through the doping and the low [Ir], it is more likely that these valence changes are, yet again, the result of IVCT, here Mn3+ + Ir3+ → Mn2+ + Ir4+. The authors concluded that the role of oxygen vacancies was dominant in the OER and ORR, where the electrocatalytic activity was improved significantly with codoping.
5.1.1.4. Simultaneous cation and anion substitution. 
Hua et al.514 used solid-state synthesis to fabricate (La1−xBax/2Srx/2)Co(O3−yFy) (x = 0.5, y = 0.1) at 1050 °C in air. STEM-EDS data confirmed incorporation of all dopants and SEM-EDS data supported the conclusion that fluorine incorporation was successful. The experimental results revealed that F doping caused the following: (i) lattice contraction, from Rietveld refinement of the XRD data, (ii) a decrease in Eg from 3.50 to 3.22 eV, as shown by UV-Vis spectroscopy, and (iii) a decrease in the Co3+/Co4+ ratio, as suggested by XPS data. Concerning the last effect, F doping cannot explain the increase in Co valence. Consequently, the charge balance must derive from the acceptor doping of La, which, again, can be viewed as a synthesis of valence change/balance according to , although only single alkaline earth may be involved. As shown in Fig. 22a and b, fluorine doping substantially improved both hydrogen evolution reaction (HER) and OER, respectively. Fig. 22c shows that chronoamperometric test measurements indicated that F-doping in (La0.5Ba0.25Sr0.25)Co(O2.9F0.1) yielded much better durability than that of the control Pt/C(−)/RuO2(+) electrolyser. This study highlights the capacity to engineer defect types and concentrations through simultaneous substitution of cation sites (A-sites) and oxygen sites. That is, if only anion doping was implemented, then charge compensation would be likely to occur by cation vacancy formation and/or cation reduction. However, when anion doping is matched with cation doping, neither of these is required and a different mechanism result.
 Fig. 22  Linear sweep voltammetry (LSV) data for (La1−xBax/2Srx/2)Co(O3−yFy) and (Pr1−xBax/2Srx/2)Co(O3−yFy): (a) LSV HER curves, (b) LSV OER curves, and (c) chronoamperometric tests of LBSCOF (LBSCOF: x = 0.5, y = 0.1; LBSCO: x = 0.5, y = 0; PBSCF: x = 0.5, y = 0.2; LBSCF: x = 0.5, y = 0.2; BSCF: x = 1.0, y = 0.2; LSCO: (La0.5Sr0.5)CoO3−δ) (reproduced from ref. 514 with permission from the Elsevier). 

5.2. Residual and imposed strain effects
The correlation between strain and electrocatalytic activity was established theoretically by Hammer and Nørskov.560 Experimentally, strain effects on the electrochemical properties of noble metals, alloys, and complex oxides have been studied extensively.561 Residual strain can alter both the surface energy and the lattice energy of perovskite materials, which correlate with key electrocatalytic processes, such as the absorption/desorption of reaction intermediates and the charge transfer.562 Since strain can be imposed controllably through different techniques, as described briefly in Section 4.5, it is a parameter that can be used to engineer the relevant properties of perovskites.

5.2.1. Size-induced residual strain. 
For a spherical nanoparticle with a free surface, the lattice is slightly contracted owing to the large surface/volume ratio; this contraction increases with decreasing particle size.563 Both crystallite size (which is identical to the grain size for single-crystal grains) and strain can be calculated from XRD patterns using the Williamson–Hall equation, which is βcosθ = kλ/d + 4εsinθ, where β is the peak broadening of full width at half maximum (FWHM), θ is the diffraction angle, k is a shape factor (0.89 for spherical particles), λ is the X-ray wavelength, d is the crystallite size, and ε is the strain.
Kumar et al.564 synthesised (Ba1−xSrx)MnO3 (x = 0.25) and (La1−xSrx)MnO3 (x = 0.30) perovskites using a combustion method, controlling crystallite size in the range ∼20–200 nm through temperature variation. The respective calculated ε values were in the range 0.4–0.1%. These relatively small values did not contribute significantly to the electrocatalytic performance. However, this size range may have been too large to yield a detectable effect as metallic particles of size <5 nm have been observed to exhibit significant increases in strain.565


5.2.2. Substrate-induced imposed strain. 
Herklotz et al.566 summarised the effects of oxygen vacancies on the residual strain in perovskites. In this review, the authors generalised that the functionalities and physical properties of perovskites could be tailored through strain engineering using control of the formation and migration of oxygen vacancies.
Lattice mismatch in the range ±3% between epitaxial films and single-crystal substrates is well known, although values as high as 6.6% have been recorded.567 As shown in Fig. 23a, Schlom et al.567 calculated lattice mismatches for a range of perovskite films deposited on many common substrates. Petrie et al.568 have hypothesised that the reason for greatly enhanced OER and ORR over LaNiO3 was compressive strain-induced splitting of the eg orbitals and the orbital occupancy (surface polarisation) (Fig. 13). They were able to control the biaxial strain of LaNiO3 thin films (∼10 nm thickness) between +2.7% tension and −1.2% compression, which the authors considered allowed them to tune to oxygen chemisorption. Fig. 23b shows how the OER and ORR required bifunctional overpotential decreased as the compressive strain increased.
 Fig. 23  (a) Pseudotetragonal or pseudocubic a axis lattice constants of some perovskites and common single-crystal cubic substrates (reproduced from ref. 567 and 569 with permission from the Multidisciplinary Digital Publishing Institute), (b) effect of compressive strain on the required overpotential of LaNiO3 thin films (reproduced from ref. 568 with permission from the American Chemical Society), and (c) LaCoO3 thin films with moderate tensile strain (reproduced from ref. 570 with permission from the American Chemical Society). 
Stoerzinger et al.570 demonstrated that the OER and ORR over (001) LaCoO3 films were improved through modest tensile stresses and thinner films. The resultant strains changed the electronic structure and decreased the charge-transfer resistance to the electrolyte. These relations are shown schematically in Fig. 23c.
Liu et al.519 investigated the coupled effects of oxygen vacancy concentration and strain on the electrocatalytic activity of (La1−xSrx)CoO3−δ thin films on (100) SrTiO3 and (100) LaAlO3 substrates, with the  being controlled through vacuum annealing. In agreement with the preceding studies, the OER performance of films under compression was better than that of films under tension. However, these effects were reduced with increasing , which is consistent with the observation that tension enhanced the . DFT simulations indicated that the tension associated with increasing  caused greater occupancy of the eg states and an increase in the splitting energy Δ (Fig. 13).


5.2.3. Jahn–Teller distortion. 
Jahn–Teller distortion is based on the concept that if the ground state of a molecule is degenerate, it will distort in order to eliminate the degeneracy and make one energy level more stable,571 as shown in Fig. 24. The susceptibility of ions in octahedral or tetrahedral coordination is:
 Fig. 24  Distortion of coordination polyhedra and electron energy levels from Jahn–Teller distortion: (top) octahedral site elongated along the z axis and (bottom) octahedral site compressed along the z axis.571 
• Octahedral: d4, d9, and low-spin d7
• Tetrahedral: d3, d4, d8, d9
Tong et al.534 reported that with decreasing particle size to ∼33 nm, La2NiMnO6 double perovskite nanoparticles shifted from a static vibronic Ni2+–O–Mn4+ (Ni2+ = d8, Mn4+ = d3) to a vibronic superexchange interaction of Ni3+–O–Mn3+ (Ni3+ = d7, Mn3+ = d4), which resulted in strong Jahn–Teller distortion of the NiO6 and MnO6 octahedra, effectively lengthening the metal–oxygen bonds. The respective filling of the electron orbitals and the distortion are illustrated schematically in Fig. 25. The parallel between vibronic superexchange and IVCT is clear.
 Fig. 25  Schematic illustration of Jahn–Teller distortion of MnO6 and NiO6 octahedra in the La2NiMnO6 double perovskite (reproduced from ref. 534 with permission from the American Chemical Society). 
Guo et al.572 examined (Ca1−xYbx)MnO3−δ (x = 0, 0.1) and attributed improved OER to the effects of Jahn–Teller distortion. This study showed that Yb3+ substitution for Ca2+ caused (1) charge-compensating Mn4+ → Mn3+ reduction and (2) H2 reduction-induced oxygen vacancy formation. These two phenomena acted synergistically to alter the eg electron orbital filling, which elongated the Mn–O bonds. The key conclusion from this work was that this bond elongation facilitated the formation of oxygen vacancies during the H2 reduction that resulted without structural alteration otherwise. These outcomes increased both the electrical conductivity and the OER.
These two studies point to how Jahn–Teller distortion can be incorporated into a defect engineering strategy. That is, if B-site dopants are selected on the basis of the ability to adopt d4, d9, or low-spin d7 electronic configurations, it is possible that they can be induced to exhibit MO6 distortion, thereby increasing some of the M–O bond lengths. If a reduction system is then imposed, the removal of the more distant oxygen ions may be facilitated, thus generating oxygen vacancies more easily and above the concentration possible by charge compensation for acceptor doping. Depending on the how the degeneracy is broken by the stabilisation, the MO6 octahedron is elongated, increasing the M–O bond for two oxygen ligands, or compressed, increasing the M–O bond for twice as many oxygen ligands.

6. Defect engineering of perovskite photocatalysts
Photocatalysis involves the light-facilitated acceleration of a chemical reaction, where the catalyst typically is a solid semiconductor. In 1972, Fujishima and Honda573 were the first to report the ultraviolet (UV) irradiation of semiconducting TiO2 to generate electrons and holes with reducing and oxidising characteristics to decompose water into oxygen and hydrogen, respectively. The principles of the associated materials and processes were delineated comprehensively by Bak et al.574
Perovskites play a significant role in photocatalysis owing to their intrinsic capability to photogenerate electron–hole pairs (excitons). Perovskites have been utilised extensively as photocatalysts in many applications, including photocatalytic H2 generation, CO2 conversion, organic transformations, dye degradation, and pollutant photodegradation/oxidation.575 However, perovskites in the stoichiometric bulk form typically exhibit the disadvantages of being limited to selective UV absorption, large Eg, inappropriate band edge potential levels, inappropriate flat band potential, sluggish charge carrier separation mobility, and/or rapid charge carrier recombination.576 Consequently, perovskites have been subjected to modification by a range of methods, including generation of defects,577 heterojunction formation,530 and/or addition of a cocatalyst.576
Defect formation in perovskites has involved numerous strategies to modify and enhance photocatalytic activity. These may involve narrowing the band gap, aligning the band edges, shifting the flat band potential, and/or increasing charge carrier mobility, often with the aim of increasing radiation absorption into the visible range.578 Defects can transform an inactive perovskite into a photoactive semiconductor by enhancing the quantum efficiency (QE).579
In addition to this form of defect engineering, there is another approach, which is through the nanostructure. That is, these materials often are synthesised with different extents of crystallinity and grain size, where these variables are controlled by the heat treatment conditions of time, temperature, and atmosphere. That is, the effects of crystallinity can be viewed in terms of a continuum between crystalline and amorphous, where the latter involves 3D unformed bonds, which are not beneficial to photocatalysis. The effects of surface area, as exemplified typically through the grain size, also represent a continuum from a single crystal to individual precrystalline nuclei, where a grain boundary surface consists of an area of 2D dangling bonds that is rich in oxygen vacancies and dopant and lattice cation that may be beneficial (or detrimental) to photocatalysis. These considerations highlight the importance of suitable characterisation of the materials as a support to the interpretation of the causes of change in photocatalytic performance. Fig. 26 shows the relationships between the effect of doping on the grain size and photocatalytic performance.88 This schematic illustrates not only the significant impact that grain size and associated surface area (and hence active site concentration) have but also the phenomenon of quantum confinement (viz. quantum size effect), which is associated with a significant increase in performance but, paradoxically, a significant increase in the Eg.88 These data make it clear that the expectation of a linear relationship between grain size and photocatalytic performance would be misplaced. The schematic also reflects the common practice of doping studies to be limited to the effects between points C and D although the observation of a maximum in performance is also not uncommon.
 Fig. 26  Typical effects of doping on the grain size and resultant effects on photocatalytic performance for a full-scale doping study (reproduced from ref. 88 with permission from Elsevier). 
6.1. Defect-induced band-gap engineering
The broad intention of introducing defects is the alteration of the energy band structure, particularly to narrow the Eg by raising the VB and/or lowering the CB. However, charge carrier transport can also be facilitated by the introduction of midgap states associated with each type of intrinsic and introduced defect. Narrowing the band gap is effective in shifting the absorption edge, thereby increasing absorption of the light required for photocatalysis further into the visible range. The potential to achieve efficient activation of photocatalysts under sunlight would facilitate their application in renewable energy generation.
Narrowing the band gap through defect formation is a commonly used approach achieved by, as discussed previously, (1) substitution with cations and/or anions or (2) the use of reducing gases or solids. If the dopant is an electron acceptor, with p-type conductivity (e.g., Mg2+ for La3+), the CB may shift downward and, if the dopant is an electron donor, with n-type conductivity (e.g., N3− for O2−), the VB may shift upward; both cases narrow the Eg. Although a small Eg is desirable as this indicates that less energy is required to elevate an electron from the VB to the CB, the process is circumscribed by the requirement that the energy required for the target reaction must be located within the Eg and either the CB or VB should be in relatively close proximity to this energy.580 Consequently, in some cases, such as the oxynitride perovskites, band alignment requirements actually involve increasing the Eg.581Fig. 27 shows the band positions of a number of perovskite photocatalysts plus the redox potentials required for some of the most important reactions in renewable energy technologies.
 Fig. 27  Schematic of CB, VB, and band gap energy positions of some perovskites and redox potentials versus NHE (pH = 0) and vacuum relevant to renewable energy technologies.582–584 
6.2. Photolysis
The use of perovskite-based materials for solar water splitting is a key topic in contemporary research in the development of clean and efficient energy.585 Solar-driven water splitting falls into four general categories, which are (i) photoelectrocatalytic water splitting, (ii) thermophotocatalytic water splitting, (iii) solar-cell-powered electrochemical water splitting, and (iv) photocatalytic water splitting. As shown in Fig. 28, the required potential energy for photocatalytic water splitting is +1.23 eV,586 corresponding to a wavelength of ∼1000 nm in the infrared region of the solar energy, and so three criteria must be met: (i) the Eg should be larger than 1.23 eV, corresponding to a wavelength of ∼1000 nm in the infrared region of the solar energy, and so (ii) the CB should be above the H+/H2 redox potential for the 2H+ + 2e− → H2 (photo)cathodic reaction, and (iii) the VB should be below the H2O/O2 redox potential for the 2H2O + 4h+ → O2 + 4H+ photoanodic reaction.
 Fig. 28  Schematic of photocatalytic water splitting by a semiconductor loaded with co-catalysts for H2 and O2 evolution (reproduced from ref. 586 with permission from Royal Society of Chemistry). 
6.3. Titanate-based perovskites

6.3.1. La titanates. 
There has been considerable work on the substitution of perovskites for photocatalytic water splitting.582 In an early study, Kim et al.587 investigated the effect of Ni doping on the photocatalytic water splitting of layered La2Ti2O7. They found that metallic Ni-loaded La2Ti2O7 exhibited a 275-fold increase over the performance of Degussa P25 TiO2 (1376 μmol h−1 g−1for doped La2Ti2O7).587 They attributed this photocatalytic enhancement to the reduction of H+ by NiO and the oxidation of OH− by the perovskite. Kim et al.588 also studied the effect of alkaline earth metal dopants (viz., Ca, Sr, and Ba) on the photocatalytic water splitting activity of A-site-doped La2Ti2O7. Of the three dopants, Ba exhibited the greatest effect in enhancing the photocatalytic activity ∼2.75 times over that of undoped La2Ti2O7 (2010 μmol h−1 g−1). This was attributed to the larger ionic radius of Ba compared to that of Sr and Ca. The amount of Ba substitution was important as BaTi4O9 and BaTiO3 were photocatalytic reaction products that also absorbed light and reduced the overall effect.
Hwang et al.589 reported that the B-site substitution of La2Ti2O7 with Cr (30 μmol h−1 g−1) or Fe (20 μmol h−1 g−1) evolved H2 from an H2O/methanol solution under visible light irradiation (λ > 420 nm) while the undoped perovskite exhibited no photoactivity; a Pt cocatalyst was used for this work. Wang et al.590 reported that A-site substitution of La2Ti2O7 with Rh yielded a low H2 production rate of ∼4.9 μmol h−1 g−1. Other layered La titanates have also been investigated, including a comparative study of La2Ti3O9, La2TiO5, and La2Ti2O7, which revealed H2 production rates of 386, 442, and 478 μmol h−1 g−1, respectively. In contrast, bulk LaTiO3, with a three-dimensional orthorhombically distorted perovskite structure, exhibited a H2 production rate of 137 μmol h−1 g−1.


6.3.2. Sr titanates. 
SrTiO3 is another wide-band-gap semiconductor (3.18 eV or 390 nm591) with light absorption limited to the UV range (1.24–3.10 eV or 100–400 nm). However, the introduction of oxygen vacancies by acceptor substitution or reduction using NaBH4 has been found to be an effective strategy to enhance the photocatalytic activity by extending light absorption into the visible range.592 Konta et al.592 investigated the effect of Mn, Ru, Rh, or Ir substitution on the photocatalytic water splitting activity of SrTiO3. Mn- and Ru-doped SrTiO3 generated O2 with no H2 evolution in aqueous silver nitrate solution. In contrast, Rh-, Ir-, and Ru-doped SrTiO3 in the presence of Pt (as a cocatalyst) and methanol (as a sacrificial molecule) produced H2 under visible light irradiation (λ > 440 nm). They also observed that Rh-doped SrTiO3 with Pt cocatalyst gave the highest QE of 5.2% for H2 production, with a rate of 90 μmol h−1 g−1 under visible light irradiation. Under UV irradiation, the H2 production rate for Rh-doped SrTiO3 was as high as 330 μmol h−1 g−1 while undoped SrTiO3 exhibited a rate of 166 μmol h−1 g−1.592 They subsequently demonstrated that the cathodic photocurrent of Rh-doped SrTiO3 suggested its applicability as a photocathode for photoelectrochemical water splitting.593
Kumar et al.594 performed a comprehensive DFT study on the effects of band (CB and VB) edge alignment of SrTiO3 through substitutional cation (Mn) doping, anion (N) doping, and codoping (Mn and N) on photocatalytic water splitting. The effects of the dopants considered on the Eg and band positions are shown in Fig. 29. The authors determined that substitution with Mn or N lowered the Eg to the visible region midgap states, but these acted as deep trapping sites and charge-carrier recombination centres. The most stable defect configuration arose from codoping, with a  defect configuration, which also lowered the Eg to the visible region. However, in this case, the resultant localised states were passivated and effectively formed a continuous band through the formation of shallower states with suitable band-edge positions, which did not hinder electron mobility.
 Fig. 29  Electronic band structures of Mn and O substitutional monodoping and codoping of SrTiO3, showing defect configurations, Eg, CB and VB band edges positions, and redox reaction potential for H2 and O2 generation (reproduced from ref. 594 with permission from American Chemical Society). 
As stated previously, oxygen-vacancy engineering of perovskites is a common method of modifying the catalytic properties.595 The kinetics of O2 reduction at the surface of Sr(Ti1−xFex)O3−δ have been observed to be accelerated with increasing . In addition to the effects on the band energies, the secondary effects of oxygen vacancies are (i) their role in charge separation as surface trapping sites and (ii) the enhanced charge transfer from the surface to an adsorbate.596 Further when the oxygen vacancy acts as an electron trapping site, it can alter the coordination number and the electronic state of the surface adsorbate, which can accelerate the production of ROS, which are essential for catalytic environmental remedation.597
The regulation of oxygen defect concentration can be used to improve the photocatalytic efficiency. The reduction of SrTiO3 with NaBH4 under Ar has been used to generate disordered surfaces rich in oxygen vacancies through Ti4+ reduction.577 The outcomes were lowering of the band gaps and, as shown in Fig. 30, darkening of the perovskites and increasing H2 generation rates. However, the latter trend exhibited a maximum owing to the effective partial amorphisation of the reactive surfaces owing to excessive .205
 Fig. 30  (a) Crystal structure of SrTiO3 with an oxygen-deficient surface, (b) digital photography of reduced SrTiO3 at different temperatures, and (c) comparison of the photocatalytic H2 production rates of SrTiO3 reduced at different temperatures (reproduced from ref. 577 with permission from Royal Society of Chemistry). 
Layered Ruddlesden–Popper Sr2TiO4, with a crystal structure as shown in Fig. 31a, is a wide-band-gap semiconductor with an Eg of ∼3.5 eV, so it absorbs only in the UV region.598 B-Site Cr substitution enhanced electron transfer through the introduction of a Cr 3d midgap state, as shown in Fig. 31b, which the authors assumed to be a secondary VB. Substitutional codoping with La at the A sites and Cr at the B sites was also used to improve the photoactivity. Fig. 31c shows the hydrogen production kinetics data for these materials under UV + visible light, and Fig. 31d shows H2 production rates under both UV + visible light and visible light. The optimal Cr-substituted (5 mol%) sample showed the optimal H2 production rate, which was ∼98 μmol h−1 g−1, and a QE of 0.16% under UV + visible light for H2 production, which was attributed to a lowered Eg (indeterminate) and consequent visible light absorption. The optimal codoped sample (10 mol% La, 10 mol% Cr) showed a maximal H2 production rate of ∼39 μmol h−1 g−1 under UV + visible light, which was linked to a lowered Eg, in this case to ∼2.9 eV.
 Fig. 31  Photocatalytic Sr(Ti1−xCrx)O4−δ and (Sr1−xLax)(Ti1−xCrx)O4−δ: (a) crystal structure, (b) schematic band energies, (c) kinetics of photocatalytic H2 evolution from water splitting, and (d) comparison of photocatalytic H2 production rates (full range = UV + visible light) (reproduced from ref. 598 with permission from American Chemical Society). 


6.3.3. Ba titanates. 
A-Site substitutional doping of BaTiO3 with Rh has been found to be another practical pathway for enhancing the photocatalytic activity under visible-range irradiation. Nishioka and Maeda599 demonstrated that doping Rh in BiTiO3 leads to the uplifting of the VB by generating donor bands near the VB edge. This band gap narrowing has been found to be effective in enhancing the photoactivity in H2 evolution. The synthesis was simply achieved by hydrothermal treatment of Ba(OH)2, TiO2 and Rh3+ salt in an autoclave at 433 K for 24 h and subsequent calcination at higher temperature. Among the different calcined temperatures, 1127 K exhibited a higher H2 production rate (18 μmol h−1 g−1) which was ∼5-fold more elevated compared to that of the uncalcined sample. Furthermore, the utilization of TiO2 as a precursor for the synthesis of Rh-doped BiTiO3 had a crucial impact on the photoactivity, in which the P25 was better than others.599 It is noteworthy that the cocatalyst in this reaction was 0.5 wt% Pt.


6.3.4. Bi titanates. 
There are a number of Bi titanates, including Bi4Ti3O12, Bi20TiO32, Bi12TiO20, Bi2Ti2O7, and Bi2Ti4O11, that have been investigated for photocatalytic H2 evolution.600 Bi4Ti3O12 in the presence of a cocatalyst under UV light irradiation has been investigated601 as has B-site substitution of Bi4Ti3O12 with Cr.602 In the latter study,602 nanosheets of 20–30 nm thickness and 100–300 nm width were hydrothermally synthesised with NiO as the cocatalyst. Both dimensions decreased significantly with increasing Cr substitution, as shown in Fig. 32a and b. Fig. 32c and d show the effects of the levels of Cr substitution and NiO cocatalyst, with both showing maxima (∼98 μmol h−1 g−1 without cocatalyst; ∼148 μmol h−1 g−1 with cocatalyst) in terms of H2 production rates under visible light. Although the authors did not speculate, it is likely that the observed maxima resulted from blockage of active sites deriving from, in the first case, exsolution of Cr upon exceeding the solubility limit and, in the second case, reversal of the balance between the beneficial effect of the NiO cocatalysis and active site occlusion. The former could be examined by lattice parameter determination and the latter could be assessed by electrochemically active surface area (ECSA). The photocatalytic performance can be interpreted by photoluminescence through the determination of electron–hole recombination.
 Fig. 32  (a) SEM image of undoped Bi4Ti3O12, (b) SEM image of Cr-doped Bi4Ti3O12 (Bi4Ti2.6Cr0.4O12), (c) photocatalytic H2 production rates of Bi4Ti3O12 as a function of Cr substitution, and (d) photocatalytic H2 production rates of Bi4Ti3O12 as a function of NiO cocatalyst addition (reproduced from ref. 602 with permission from Royal Society of Chemistry). 

6.4. Tantalate-based perovskites
Sr2InTaO6 and Ba2InTaO6 are tantalate perovskites that have been investigated for H2 production from photocatalytic water splitting.603 Lv et al.603 found that 10 mol% B-site substitution by Ni increased the H2 production rate under UV + visible light from ∼120 to ∼210 μmol h−1 g−1 for Sr2InTaO6 and ∼220 to ∼394 μmol h−1 g−1 for Ba2InTaO6 (QE = 2.75% for H2 production); these improvements were attributed to lowered Eg values. DFT simulations indicated that these results derived from the introduction of additional spin-polarised bands that reduced the threshold for light absorption.
Anion substitution has also been found to be a promising approach to enhance the photocatalytic activity under visible light for H2 production.604 Substitution of O by N in Dion–Jacobson layered La2Ta2O7 nanosheets (LaTa2O6.77N0.15), which had been exfoliated from bulk RbLaTa2O6.77N0.15, exhibited strong visible light absorption over the range 400–600 nm owing to hybridisation between the O 2p and N 2p orbitals, effectively raising the VB. The QE values under visible light of the nanosheets were 1.29% and 3.27% for photocatalytic H2 and O2 production, respectively, which were 300% and 700%, respectively, greater than that of their bulk counterparts. These performances were also attributed to the nature of the 2D nanosheets, which involved low thicknesses (∼2 nm), high surface areas, instantaneous charge generation at the surfaces, and minimal charge carrier diffusion distances.
6.5. Oxynitride-based perovskites
Oxynitrides have emerged as perovskites of considerable interest for photocatalytic oxygen evolution owing to their narrow Eg values, which generally are appropriate for visible light absorption.605 However, in most cases, particularly for water splitting, they require post-modification or the formation of heterojunctions because the potential energy levels of the CB and VB are not aligned with relevant redox potentials for H2 and O2 production, respectively.606 The low Eg values derive from the VB levels, which are comprised of hybridised O 2p and N 2p orbitals, which effectively raise the VB to a level higher than that of their oxide analogues, which have only O 2p orbitals.607 Wu et al.608 investigated A-site substitution of the Ruddlesden–Popper phase LaTiO2N with Ca since the undoped material exhibited no photocatalytic activity for H2 and O2 production. This study revealed that the composition (Ca0.7La0.3)Ti(O2.7N0.3) yielded a maximal H2 production rate of ∼67 μmol h−1 g−1 and a QE of 3.13% under visible light for O2 production. As suggested in Fig. 33a and b, Ca substitution resulted in significant alteration of the unit cell, which underwent considerable shrinkage. This substitution reflected the ability to tune the associated light absorption, [N], and [Ti3+]. Photoelectrochemical analyses revealed increased photocurrents, fewer defect states, lower electrical resistance, and longer electron lifetime. The improved photocatalytic performance, which is shown in Fig. 33c, was attributed to reduced surface [Ti3+], high charge mobility, and suitable surface texture.
 Fig. 33  Refined crystal structures of (a) La0.5Ca0.5TiO2.5N0.5 and (b) La0.3Ca0.7TiO2.7N0.3 (c) O2 production rates as a function of x in (La1−xCax)Ti(O2+xN1−x) (reproduced from ref. 608 with permission from Elsevier). 
Wang et al.609 codoped SrTiO2N with La (A-site) and Zr (B-site), calcined and equilibrated the samples for 10 h at 600 °C, observing an apparent solubility limit and maximal unit cell expansion at the composition (La0.6Sr0.4)(Zr0.6Ta0.4)O2N (x = 0.6). Fig. 34a illustrates the schematics of the crystal structures of undoped SrTiO2N and maximally doped (La0.6Sr0.4)(Zr0.6Ta0.4)O2N. The latter structure suggested that codoping increased the antiphase [001] tilting of the Ta(O,N)6 octahedra. It is difficult to rationalise the observations that there was no change in the Eg from ∼2.1 eV while the CB was lowered, but the VB remained unchanged, although the authors offered the comment that the structural changes from La and Zr codoping modified the electronic band structure; improved charge separation also resulted from codoping. Regardless, the data in Fig. 34b–e reveal significant improvements in photocatalytic performance as a function of the codoping level and Pt cocatalyst loading. Again, the detection of a maximum in the latter suggests the competition between cocatalysis and active site occlusion. The authors also commented that the correspondence between visible light absorbance and QE (maximum at 0.84% at 420 nm) as a function of excitation wavelength suggested that the H2 production is photon-driven. The aqueous durability of the material is suggested by the maintenance of the H2 production over three cycles under visible light.
 Fig. 34  (a) Refined crystal structures of SrTaO2N (top) and codoped (La0.6Sr0.4)(Zr0.6Ta0.4)O2N (bottom); kinetics of photocatalytic H2 evolution from water splitting as a function of (b) La and Zr codoping and (c) Pt cocatalyst loading of (La0.6Sr0.4)(Zr0.6Ta0.4)O2N. (d) Visible light absorbance and QE as a function of excitation wavelength (i.e., action spectrum) of (La0.6Sr0.4)(Zr0.6Ta0.4)O2N for H2 production (0.5 M Na2SO3 aqueous solution as a sacrificial agent, 0.5 wt% Pt as a cocatalyst), and (e) cyclic H2 production for (La0.6Sr0.4)(Zr0.6Ta0.4)O2N under visible light (reproduced from ref. 609 with permission from Royal Society of Chemistry). 
6.6. Mutually soluble perovskites
Ueda et al.606,607 observed that complete LaTaON2–NaTaO3 solid solutions exhibited gradually decreasing photoactivity proportional to the extent of decreasing nitrogen content. The authors tuned the Eg resulted principally from the more positive VB, which shifted sufficiently far enough from the potential energy level of OER (H2O → O2). However, in the case of NaTaO3, oxygen evolution did not occur since its VB is significantly far from the required potential energy level, making NaTaO3 inapplicable for OER. Similar work by Kato et al.606 on the synthesis of complete LaTaON2–SrTiO3 solid solutions also revealed the capacity to tune the Eg. As shown in Fig. 35, decreasing LaTaON2 content blue-shifted the absorption edge and increased the Eg, with H2 production increasing with LaTaON2 content, minimal O2 production for LaTaON2, and maximal O2 production for 50LaTaON2–50SrTiO3. At the optimal composition of 50LaTaON2–50SrTiO3, the H2 produced was ∼7 μmol h−1 while the O2 produced was 104 μmol h−1; water splitting with pure LaTaON2 did not occur.
 Fig. 35  (a) UV-visible absorption spectra of complete LaTaON2–SrTiO3 solid solutions and (b) tuning of the Eg and band edge modification through variation in the LaTaON2/SrTiO3 ratio (Sr25 = 75LaTaON2−25SrTiO3) (reproduced from ref. 606 with permission from Royal Society of Chemistry). 

6.6.1. Water oxidation. 
6.6.1.1. Tantalum-based perovskites. 
The photocatalytic activity of SrTaO2N can be enhanced by substitution at both A sites and B sites.610 Na+ acceptor substitution for Sr2+ in this oxynitride perovskite, as shown in Fig. 36a, has been observed to improve the photocatalytic activity under visible light. The O2 produced with (Sr0.8Na0.2)TaO2.8N0.2 was found to be ∼220 μmol h−1 g−1 while that of undoped SrTaO2N was nil; both included 1 wt% Rh2O3 cocatalyst. Despite the strong absorption band in the visible range, with an Eg of 2.16 eV for the substituted sample (increased from 2.03 eV for the undoped sample; Fig. 36b), the QE was only 0.2%.
 Fig. 36  (a) Refined crystal structure and (b) energy band diagram for undoped and Na-doped SrTaO2N (reproduced from ref. 610 with permission from Royal Society of Chemistry), (c) energy band diagram with secondary VBs for N-doped Sr4Nb2O9 calcined at different temperatures, (d) photocatalytic O2 production under visible light of N-doped Sr4Nb2O9 (reproduced from ref. 605 with permission from John Wiley & Sons), (e) energy band diagram for undoped and doped SrTaO2N, i.e., Sr(Nb1−xMgx)O2+yN1−y (x = 0.2), and (f) visible light absorbance and QE as a function of excitation wavelength (i.e., action spectrum) of Mg-doped Sr(Nb1−xMgx)O2+yN1−y (x = 0.2) (reproduced from ref. 611 with permission from Royal Society of Chemistry). 
6.6.1.2. Niobate-based perovskites. 
Wu et al.605 studied the effect of nitrogen doping on the water oxidation of Sr4Nb2O9 with 3 wt% Rh2O3 cocatalyst under visible light. It was observed that nitrogen incorporation resulted in the formation of oxygen vacancies. These authors commented that the nitrogen caused the formation of a secondary VB, associated with lowering of the Eg (from ∼3.9 eV to ∼1.9 eV) and absorption in the visible range. The Eg depended on the calcination temperature, with the minimum at 900 °C, as shown in Fig. 36c. However, the greatest O2 production rate, which was ∼345 μmol h−1 g−1, as shown in Fig. 36d, was for the sample calcined at 1100 °C; the QE was ∼0.31%.
Although SrNbO2N has a low Eg of 1.8 eV, high defect concentrations result in poor photocatalytic activities.611 B-Site substitution by Mg allowed Eg tuning and blue shifting into the visible range, resulting in an Eg of 1.97 eV (as shown in Fig. 36e), a maximal O2 production rate of 65.19 μmol h−1 g−1, and a QE of 5.31% (as shown in Fig. 36f), for Sr(Nb0.8Mg0.2)O2+yN1−y with 1 wt% CoOx cocatalyst.
Wan et al.612 improved the water oxidation by LaNbON2 (with CoOx cocatalyst), which has a low Eg of 1.65 eV, by generating high levels of anion (both nitrogen and oxygen) vacancies by annealing in Ar at 700 °C. The increase in the O2 production rate from nil to 108 μmol h−1 g−1 was attributed largely to the increase of the Fermi level (EF) owing to the increase in donors in the form of anion vacancies.


6.6.2. Photoreduction of CO2 to fuel. 
Although CO2 is perceived as a deleterious greenhouse gas, it has the advantage that it can also be converted into fuels, including CO, CH4, HCHO, HCOOH, and CH3OH.613,614 However, current materials used for CO2 fixation and conversion suffer from low conversion rates and selectivity. Solar-driven photoreduction represents a promising solution for the challenge of CO2 remediation.615 This homogeneous catalytic process, using GaP, was reported initially by Halmann616 in 1978 and, since then, has been used with WO3,617 graphitic carbon nitride (g-C3N4),618 and perovskites.203,619 With perovskites, the rate of photoreduction depends not only on the capacity to photogenerate electrons but also on the extent of CO2 surface adsorption, both of which have been observed to be improved through the introduction of defects in perovskites.620 In contrast, perovskites of low defect concentrations exhibit little or no photoreduction of CO2.621 Consequently, various strategies have been developed in order to enhance the photocatalytic activity, particularly towards visible-range photoreduction. These strategies include the use of cocatalysts,622 formation of heterojunctions,623 and the introduction of defects.624 Although photoreduction can be done in both gaseous624 and liquid states, liquid-based photocatalysis has the limitation of the requirement of CO2 solubility across the gas–liquid interface.625 The principle of photoreduction for CO2 dissolved in aqueous solutions is shown in Fig. 37, which shows band energy conditions suitable for water oxidation (E vs. NHE = +0.82 V) and the conversion of CO2 into some fuels (E vs. NHE <−0.6 V).
 Fig. 37  Schematic representation of the band energy diagram for CO2 photoreduction in an aqueous medium (reproduced from ref. 614 with permission from Royal Society of Chemistry). 
6.6.2.1. Titanate-based perovskites. 
Titanate-based perovskites, such as SrTiO3, have also been applied for photocatalytic CO2 reduction.620 SrTiO3 is a wide-band-gap semiconductor, with an Eg of 3.4 eV, which is considerably larger than the minimal required potential of ∼1.4 eV. As shown in Fig. 37, this minimum is between the limiting standard potential for CO2 reduction of −0.60 eV and the standard potential for water oxidation of +0.82 eV. Since the large Eg of SrTiO3 results in the absorption being limited to the UV range, its Eg must be reduced while maintaining suitable energy band positions. This strategy has been applied through the introduction of oxygen vacancies by high-temperature reduction under Ar and the use of 0.3 wt% Pt cocatalyst.626 A secondary benefit was that the reduced surfaces significantly enhanced the chemisorption of CO2, and so increased the photoactivity. An artificial photosynthesis technique involved a 2 vol% H2O/98 vol% Ar atmosphere to generate CH4 at a maximal QE of 0.21%. However, a challenge for such approaches is the effect of the chemisorption of CO2, which serves to annihilate oxygen vacancies, thereby reducing and eventually eliminating the active sites.621
6.6.2.2. Tantalate-based perovskites. 
Hou et al.203 synthesised NaTa(O3−xNx) by reductive hydrothermal synthesis using Ta3N5 in NaOH + NaBH4 aqueous solution, introducing charge-compensating oxygen vacancies, the structure of which is shown in Fig. 38a and the nanocubic morphology of which is shown in Fig. 38b. Fig. 38c shows that nitrogen substitution significantly lowered the Eg from 3.78 eV to 2.18 eV; DFT was used to determine the shifts in the band edges. While there was nil CO2 conversion for NaTaO3, the resultant CO and CH4 evolution rates under Xe light were 15 μmol h−1 g−1 and 5.2 μmol h−1 g−1, respectively, for NaTa(O3−xNx). These materials were improved yet more significantly with the use of graphene quantum dots (QDs) and nitrogen-doped graphene quantum dots (NQDs) for heterojunction formation. Comparative testing under Xe light with a 420 cut-off filter, the results of which are shown in Fig. 38d, revealed that nitrogen substitution in NaTaO3 doubled the CO and CH4 evolution rates, heterojunction formation with QDs tripled the rates, and heterojunction formation with NQDs quintupled the rates.
 Fig. 38  (a) Refined crystal structure of NaTa(O3−xNx; unstated stoichiometry) (colour: purple = Na, cyan (inside grey octahedra) = Ta, blue = N, red = oxygen, white = oxygen vacancy), (b) SEM image of NaTa(O3−xNx) nanocubes, (c) band edge energy diagram for NaTaO3 and NaTa(O3−xNx), and (d) comparative CO and CH4 evolution rates for perovskites and heterojunctions (reproduced from ref. 203 with permission from Elsevier). 


6.6.3. Photocatalytic degradation of dyes. 
6.6.3.1. Cation site substitution. 
Ilanchezhiyan et al.627 hydrothermally synthesised B-site-substituted SmFeO3 with Er3+. Although the [Er3+] was not disclosed, EDS semiquantitative surface scans of the undoped and doped perovskite suggested that the [Er] was ∼1 at% and the [Fe] decreased by a similar amount upon doping, which imply that substitution occurred. Consideration of Hume-Rothery's rules indicates that substantial solid solubility is unlikely since the ionic radii of Fe3+ and Er3+ are 0.0785 nm (high spin, which is likely197) and 0.1004 nm, respectively (28% difference). In contrast, substantial interstitial solid solubility is favoured as the size rule is satisfied and the electronegativities are reasonably close, with values of Fe3+ = 1.9 and Er3+ = 1.3. Thus, only limited or no substitutional solid solubility would be anticipated while substantial interstitial solid solubility is more likely. Comparison of the indicative EDS data clarifies the matter as they show that the [O] increased significantly by 5–6 at%. Since all three cations have valence 3+, any substitutions are neutral and so cannot result in charge compensation through oxygen. The only way that this increase in [O] can be explained is by oxygen interstitial solid solubility. Consequently, it is concluded that the solid solubility involves interstitial O that charge compensates the interstitial Er3+. The analytical methods applied included XRD, Raman, SEM, XPS, PL, and UV-Vis; testing was by rhodamine-B (RhB) dye degradation under visible light. Both the undoped and doped materials were well crystallised, with an indication that the latter materials were less well crystallised (although it was not indicated if the XRD intensity ratios were identical) and the Raman data suggested that doping essentially did not alter the structure. The SEM images revealed that doping caused substantial coarsening. Most importantly, the XPS data revealed that doping caused Fe3+ → Fe2+ reduction. Since there is no driving force for this alteration, this is attributed to the effects of electronegativity, which is higher for Fe3+ relative to Er3+ and Sm3+ (this is not included in ref. 628 but the range of values for the rare earths is 1.1–1.3). Consequently, the electron transfer from the two rare earth ions to Fe3+ can explain the reduction. The PL intensity data suggested that Er suppressed the recombination of charge carriers through the formation of midgap states. The UV-vis data revealed that doping resulted in a decrease in the Eg from ∼2.25 eV to ∼1.88 eV. The dye degradation data showed a modest increase in photocatalytic activity of ∼10% after 3 h exposure. This was attributed to the role of the Eg in increasing the electrical conductivity, which enhanced charge transfer and hence reduced recombination.
Sun et al.629 used hydrothermal synthesis to fabricate PbTiO3, to which Li was added as a dopant without stoichiometric compensation. XRD peak shifts showing lattice contraction suggested substitution, although Li is a small ion with high field strength, so it is quite possible that it dissolved interstitially and caused lattice contraction. The authors considered that low [Li] involved A-site substitution for Pb and high [Li] involved interstitial solid solubility. If substitutional, the Li+ would act as an acceptor, but if interstitial, it would act as a donor. Assuming ionic charge compensation, the former would result in oxygen vacancies and the latter would result in cation vacancies, both of which would also be expected to cause lattice contraction. The ionic radius of Li+ in eightfold coordination (for comparison) is 0.106 nm, and that of eightfold Pb2+ is 0.143 nm.287 Also, the electronegativities of Li+ and Pb2+ are 1.0 and 1.7, respectively.628 According to Hume-Rothery's rules, substantial substitutional solid solubility is not favoured in terms of size (∼20% difference) or valence (∼41% difference); substantial interstitial solid solubility is not supported by the electronegativity rule. The XPS data revealed the formation of Ti3+, which was assumed to be linked to the formation of charge compensating oxygen vacancies. However, an alternative interpretation is based on redox, where interstitial Li+ is charge compensated by Ti4+ → Ti3+ reduction, without the formation of oxygen vacancies. Methylene blue (MB) dye testing under visible light revealed that an increase in [Li+] resulted in increased degradation, which was attributed to Ti3+ and . However, examination of the SEM images (and XRD data) reveals that, while all of the materials were well crystallised, increasing [Li+] resulted in significant particle size reduction, so increased surface area may have been responsible for the improved photocatalytic performance. The preceding analysis shows the relevance of consideration of alternative scenarios for solubility and charge compensation as well as the usefulness of applying a structural and/or microstructural approach to the interpretation of the data as an alternative to semiconducting concepts alone.
Abirami et al.630 used hydrothermal synthesis to fabricate PbTiO3, to which Ag was added as a dopant without stoichiometric compensation. XRD peak shifts showing lattice expansion led the authors to conclude that Ag+ substituted for Pb2+ suggested substitution. Insofar as the respective ionic radii (0.142 nm vs. 0.163 nm)287 and electronegativities (1.9 vs. 1.7628) are close, this assumption appears to be justified. Field emission scanning electron microscopy (FESEM) images revealed that undoped PbTiO3 consisted of smooth but agglomerated grains, while all of the Ag-doped samples exhibited high densities of fine-scale surface pores. The XRD data indicated that the crystallite size increased with increasing [Ag+] (0.01–0.03 wt%). More importantly, these data indicated that Ag precipitated at the two highest (of three) dopant levels, suggesting its deposition on the grain surfaces. The UV-vis data showed that the Eg decreased with increasing [Ag+]. MB testing revealed that, at pH 6, the dye degradation under UV increased in proportion to the [Ag+], although only the highest concentration (0.03 wt%) was superior to the undoped PbTiO3. The authors interpreted the trend in performance in terms of the decoration of the surfaces by Ag, which assisted charge separation and hence reduced the recombination rate.
6.6.3.2. Anion site substitution. 
Huang et al.631 used solid-state synthesis to fabricate the Ruddlesden–Popper phase K2La2Ti3O10 and nitrogen-doped it by (i) protonation with NH4+ by soaking in HNO3, (ii) doping by soaking in an aqueous NH3 solution, and (iii) annealing in N2 at 400 °C for 1 h. Their designation of the resultant product as K2La2Ti3O10−xNx indicates the authors’ assumption of substitutional solid solubility, which was supported by a UV-vis red shift and Ti–O–N bonding in FTIR. Comparison of the undoped and N-doped materials revealed a significant decrease in crystallinity, a decrease in the Eg from 3.69 to 3.44 eV and an increase in surface area from 4.1 to 5.7 m2 g−1. The photocatalytic performance was assessed using the dyes methyl orange, Cibacron yellow, and erionyl red under visible light and an Hg lamp. These data revealed consistently improved photocatalytic activity with N doping, regardless of the light source or dye. These data highlight the contrasting contributory factors that the decreasing Eg and increasing surface area would be expected to increase the photocatalytic performance but that the decreasing crystallinity is associated with decreasing photocatalytic performance.88,288,632,633 However, when considering XRD data, peak broadening from increasing amorphisation also decreases the crystallite size, which is calculated from the Scherrer equation.634 Further, increasing amorphisation and decreasing crystallite size are associated with increasing defect densities associated with grain boundaries, which are sinks for structural defects,308 which have an effect opposite to that of amorphisation.
Cao et al.635 fabricated single-phase BaTiO3 by solid-state synthesis, and doped it with N by addition of urea, and considered the effects of increasing [N] (two levels of urea addition (20 and 40 wt%) to BaTiO3 (1 wt%)) and increasing calcining temperature (500°, 550°, 600 °C). The authors assumed N dissolution in light of (110) XRD peak shifts, which indicated lattice expansion. These shifts were supported generally by lattice parameter data, which also showed expansion. This was rationalised by consideration of the ionic radii, which, in fourfold coordination (for comparison), are O2− = 0.124 nm and N3− = 0.132 nm.287 However, the difference in electronegativity (O2− = 3.65 vs. N3− = 3.00628) provides a more significant influence on the size. UV-Vis data showed that the Eg decreased significantly with both increasing [N] and increasing calcining temperature, which was reflected in the RhB degradation data as a function of increasing [N]. However, these data were not consistent for the effect of increasing calcining temperature, which revealed a maximum in RhB degradation for calcination at 550 °C. Although the XRD patterns, which did not indicate that they were scaled to the same intensity ranges, revealed powders that were well crystallised approximately to the same extent, no data on the particle sizes or surface areas were provided, and the SEM images were not comprehensive, albeit illustrating the undoped and optimal (550 °C) powders. Comparison of these images revealed similar particle sizes but that the undoped powder sintered at 500 °C was sintered to a considerably greater extent than the powder calcined at 550 °C. This is likely to have resulted from microstructural disturbance from gas exudation from urea during calcination (absent for undoped powder) followed by densification, which was greater at 600 °C than at 550 °C. This may explain the non-trending RhB degradation data as a function of calcining temperature. If this is the case, then, again, the importance of simultaneously considering the effects of structure, microstructure, and semiconducting properties is highlighted.
6.6.3.3. Cation and anion codoping. 
Saai Harini et al.636 codoped (Sn + C) nanowire Bi4Ti3O12 (with secondary phases Bi12TiO20 and Bi2Ti2O7) with 0.025, 0.050, and 0.100 mol% of SnCl2 and urea (CH4N2O) using the Pechini method. The intention appears to have been to B-site substitute Sn4+ for Ti4+ and to dissolve C4+ interstitially. However, in light of the preceding discussion, it is possible that N substitution for O was another possible outcome. The materials were characterised by XRD, Raman, HRTEM, XPS, PL, UV-vis, surface area, and average pore size; testing involved malachite green dye degradation under sunlight and an Hg lamp. The PL data revealed that the codoped Bi4Ti3O12 was less defective than the undoped material, which the authors suggested reflected reduced , presumably from C (and possibly N) substitution for O. The XPS data did not indicate an associated Ti4+ → Ti3+ reduction, but the observed Sn4+ → Sn2+ reduction represents a driving force for oxygen vacancy formation. However, reduction by carbon is a probable cause of both Sn reduction and direct oxygen vacancy formation. The authors attributed an XPS peak at 285.6 eV to C incorporation in the Bi4Ti3O12 lattice (without reference). However, the XPS peak for adventitious surface C in this range is well known.637 Both the surface area and average pore size increased, and the Eg decreased from 3.05 eV for undoped Bi4Ti3O12 progressively to 1.8 eV with the increasing codopant level as well. The photocatalytic data revealed that undoped Bi4Ti3O12 was photocatalytically inactive and that the increasing codopant level enhanced the dye degradation under both light sources. The authors attributed these outcomes to the reduced Eg and assumed decreased electron–hole recombination rate, presumably owing to the short diffusion length from the nanowire morphology. The mesoporous nature of the high-surface-area nanostructure was also considered to contribute to the enhanced photocatalytic activity of the codoped Bi4Ti3O12 compared to the undoped analogue.


6.6.4. Photodegradation of pollutants and VOCs. 
6.6.4.1. Cation site substitution. 
VOCs have been identified as some of the most critical agents of air pollution,88 which has resulted in considerable research in their oxidation. The achievement of this goal has proved elusive owing to the shortcomings in catalysts of low quantum efficiencies and difficulties in achieving complete degradation of these contaminants.638
Liu et al.639 used an NaCl–KCl molten-salt material to synthesise Au-loaded Fe3+-doped Bi4(Ti3−xFex)O12 (x = 0.2, 0.4, 0.6). Characterisation included XRD-EDS, SEM, TEM, HRTEM, XPS, PL, and UV-Vis; testing involved the degradation of aqueous solutions of phenol (C6H6O) and bisphenol A (C15H16O2) under visible light produced by a halogen source as well as transient photocurrent (i–V) measurements using chopped light under a Xe light. The XRD and SEM data revealed single-phase Bi4Ti3FeO12 of nanosheet shape, respectively. EDS, TEM, and HRTEM confirmed the successful loading of the Au nanoparticles. The XPS data are of limited use because the authors did not contrast the undoped with the doped materials. Consequently, the commonly observed Ti4+ → Ti3+ and Fe3+ → Fe2+ valence shifts cannot be examined, the generation of charge-compensating oxygen vacancies cannot be confirmed, and electron exchanges between dopant and matrix ions cannot be considered. These are relevant to the previously observed common dual charge-compensation mechanism involving (i) oxygen vacancy formation (ionic charge compensation) and (ii) IVCT. The UV-vis data revealed that Fe-doping resulted in a progressive decrease in Eg from 2.92 (undoped) to 2.28 eV (x = 0.06). The i–V and PL data revealed that the photocurrent density and charge-carrier separation, respectively, increased in the order undoped < doped < Au-loaded doped perovskites. Unsurprisingly, the extent of photodegradation of both phenol and bisphenol A followed the same order, with greater degradation for the latter. The identities of the reactive species were examined with the use of ammonium oxalate (hole (h˙) scavenger), isopropyl alcohol (˙OH hydroxyl radical scavenger), and benzoquinone (˙O2− superoxide anion scavenger). These data revealed that the contribution of the reactive species to the degradation was in the order ˙OH < ˙O2− < h˙. The authors speculate that there were five mechanisms behind these photocatalytic performances. First, the exposed (001) planes of the nanosheets enhanced charge-carrier separation through surface-charge anisotropy. Second, the anisotropic polarity of the nanosheets also enhanced charge-carrier separation. Third, Fe3+ can be oxidised by h˙ to Fe4+, which can trap h˙s, but Fe3+ can also be reduced by electrons (e−) to Fe2+, which can trap e−s; these processes enhance charge carrier separation by trapping the respective charge carriers. Fourth, the presence of defects introduced midgap states, which were shown to reduce the Eg. This is a misconception as midgap states do not reduce the Eg, which is established by the gap between the CB and VB. When shallow, midgap states may facilitate charge-carrier transition across the Eg. Fifth, light absorption was enhanced by surface plasmon resonance, which was affected by the Au loading. Other benefits from this phenomenon are promotion of the charge-carrier formation rate and the establishment of a Schottky barrier, which can enhance interfacial e− transfer. The mechanism at an atomistic level would have benefitted from some basic considerations of the nature of solid solubility and closer analysis of the data. The sixfold-coordinated ionic radii (for comparison) are Ti4+ = 0.0745 nm and Fe3+ (HS) = 0.0785.287 Hence, substitution might result in slight lattice expansion. However, if oxygen vacancies form by charge compensation, then this would be likely to provide the dominant effect of lattice contraction. If Fe3+ interstitials formed, then lattice constriction would be likely to cause expansion. However, an alternative is charge compensation by redox. If this were to occur, substitutional solid solubility would require Ti3+ (0.081 nm) → Ti4+ (0.0745 nm) oxidation and minor lattice expansion would be expected. In contrast, interstitial solid solubility charge-compensated by redox would require three Ti4+ → Ti3+ reduction reactions, which would facilitate more substantial lattice expansion. An examination of the lattice parameters might have provided a basis for more grounded speculation of the solid solubility mechanism. This could have been confirmed through closer analysis of the XPS data, which would allow consideration of the redox reactions involving Ti and Fe through IVCT according to Ti3+ + Fe3+ ↔ Ti4+ + Fe2+ and/or Ti4+ + Fe3+ ↔ Ti3+ + Fe4+. This speculation is an alternative to the authors’ proposal of redox through e−s and h˙s. These comments also highlight the risks of assuming that  as it is clear that charge compensation by redox negates this proportionality.
6.6.4.2. Anion site substitution. 
In addition to cation site substitution with the aim of lowering the Eg in order to absorb visible light, an alternative strategy is modification of the electronic structure through anion site doping. There do not appear to be any reports of the experimental investigation of VOC degradation using perovskites doped solely by anions. However, more generally, although partial substitution of oxygen by carbon, fluorine, or sulphur has been reported to lower the Eg values of perovskites,640 more promising results have been reported for partial substitution by nitrogen,641 as indicated in Fig. 39. For example, Mukherji et al.642 synthesised layered Ba5Ta4(O15−xNx) by annealing at 900 °C under NH3, which resulted in nitrogen intercalation between the (111) planes. This resulted in a significant increase in visible light absorbance owing to a change in Eg from ∼4.06 to 1.76 eV. DFT simulations led the authors to attribute this to hybridisation of the N 2p and O 2p orbitals. The photocatalytic hydrogen generation under Xe light increased significantly, from ∼310 to ∼495 μM h−1.
 Fig. 39  (a) Eg values of various oxide (white) and oxynitride (coloured) perovskites having B-site occupation by Ti4+, Nb5+, or Ta5+ and (b) examples of colours displayed by a range of oxynitride perovskites (reproduced from ref. 203 and 641 with permission from Elsevier). 
Ji et al.643 reported visible-light-induced thermocatalytic, photocatalytic, and photothermocatalytic degradation of aromatic VOCs using 3 wt% Ag-loaded SrTi(O3−xFx) (F content not reported but batched F/(F + O) = 25 at%). The effect of F doping on the electronic structure was minimal, with an Eg change from ∼3.29 to ∼3.26 eV, which was attributed to increased Ti4+ → Ti3+ reduction , which was charge-compensated by O2− substitution by F−. The degradation of gaseous toluene, benzene, and xylene at ≤90 °C using Xe light with a visible-light reflector revealed similar conversion trends, but the effectiveness of degradation was in the order thermocatalytic < photocatalytic < photothermocatalytic. The last process exhibited a monotonic increase in conversion efficiency over the increasing temperature range 45–90 °C. The results suggest that F doping had little effect on the performance and that the Ag cocatalyst provided the dominant effect.
6.6.4.3. A- and B-site substitution. 
Ramana et al.644 used solid-state reaction to synthesise the codoped Aurivillius phase (Bi4−xSmx)(Ti3−yVy)O12 (x = 0.75, y = 0.0375) for the purpose of photocatalytic degradation of NOx and improvement in ferroelectric properties, the latter of which required minimisation of the . Since it is unlikely that homogeneous doping at the very low level of y = 0.0375 could be achieved by solid-state reaction, the decreased lattice parameters could be explained by Sm substitution for Bi since the eightfold ionic radii (for comparison) are Sm3+ = 0.1219 nm and Bi3+ = 0.131 nm.287 However, since V5+ is a donor dopant, the formation of charge-compensating oxygen interstitials could cause lattice expansion, in contradiction to the observed lattice contraction. Even so, its substitution level probably is too low for this to have a significant effect. Also, the relatively high vapour pressure of Bi645 and its consequent volatilisation would result in the formation of charge-compensating oxygen vacancies, also contributing to lattice contraction. The latter possibility could be critical because oxygen vacancy formation would be very likely to be charge compensated by Ti4+ → Ti3+ reduction and the associated expansion from sixfold coordinated ionic radii (for comparison) increase (0.0745 to 0.081 nm). As shown in Fig. 40a, the authors presented a band energy diagram, which included measured Eg and a calculated CB, which illustrated the Eg change from 2.88 to 2.02 eV upon codoping. Photocatalytic degradation of NOx using a solar simulator (Osram Ultra-Vitalux, 300 W) revealed that the codoped perovskite caused linear degradation of NOx (at the ppb concentration level) to a level of 12.5% over 60 min, as shown in Fig. 40b.
 Fig. 40  (Bi4−xSmx)(Ti3−yVy)O12 (x = 0.75, y = 0.0375): (a) schematic of the energy band diagram, incorporating a calculated CB and measured Eg, and (b) NOx photodegradation (reproduced from ref. 644 with permission from Royal Society of Chemistry). 

7. Defective perovskites in energy storage
Owing to the imperatives to direct energy conversion from fossil fuels to renewables, the limitations of periodic solar, wind, and tidal energy sources have highlighted the importance of developing the means of energy storage that overcome the constraints of cost, energy and power densities, lifetime, safety, and environmental impact. To these ends, batteries and supercapacitors that contain perovskites as components are perceived widely as the most promising trajectories.
The current status of perovskite-containing energy-storage systems focuses on three key technical parameters: (i) energy density (specific capacity), (ii) power density (rate capability), and (iii) discharge time (C-rate). These data show that supercapacitors exhibit relatively low energy densities but very high power densities, which makes them applicable for applications requiring rapid charge/discharge. Also, metal-ion and metal–air batteries exhibit a relatively narrow range of high energy densities but relatively low power densities, which makes them applicable for longer operational times.
The lifetimes of batteries are determined by the impact of charge-carrier diffusion into the volumetric structure of the electrodes, ultimately resulting in failure owing to crystallographic phase transformation, chemical reaction, and/or microstructural alteration.646 In contrast, the lifetimes of supercapacitors are considerably longer than those of batteries because the relevant reversible chemical reactions occur at the electrode surface, which minimises long-term alteration.647
Concerning safety and environmental impact, each battery and supercapacitor type has its relative positives and negatives, depending largely on the nature of the cation chemistries. Further, the costs of these energy storage devices are quite variable and depend principally on the costs of raw materials and the synthesis methods.
7.1. Batteries

7.1.1. Electrodes. 
Metal-ion batteries, which generally have high energy densities, low self-discharge degrees, and high cyclability, attract considerable attention owing to the great potential for their use in mobile electronics and large energy storage systems.648 Further, new markets, such as electric vehicles, are driving research in higher power densities that may not be possible with metal-ion batteries. Consequently, the alternative metal–air batteries are subject to intensive development with the aim of meeting the requirement of existing, emerging, and future markets.649 Such batteries are likely to play key roles in the future integration of transient renewable energy systems with complementary energy storage systems. Hence, the development and optimisation of the key elements in a battery system, namely, the electrodes, electrolyte, and membrane, are essential to technical and commercial implementation.
A range of strategies have been applied to the goal of improving the performance of the electrodes and electrolytes in batteries. For example, the synthesis of electrodes of controlled structures and nanoscale morphologies, including nanowires, nanotubes, nanosheets, and complex hierarchical architectures, aims at decreasing charge-carrier diffusion lengths, accelerating charge-carrier transport, and increasing the density of accessible active sites.650 As the present work has emphasised, defect engineering to overcome performance limitations has emerged as the key strategy to this end. Table 14 summarises the state-of-the-art of the principal metal-ion and metal–air batteries that incorporate perovskites as elements in the battery design.
