7.1.1.1. Lithium-ion batteries. 
Lithium-ion batteries are the current state-of-the-art technology for high-energy-density electrochemical storage.657–659 Commercial Li-ion batteries employ intercalating materials, as suggested in Section 2.2.2.1,660–662 which host Li-ions during battery charge and discharge, so charge transfer takes place through ionic conductivity. The energy and power densities are determined by the amount and kinetics of intercalation and deintercalation for these layered structures.
One of the most topical areas of perovskites used as anodes for Li-ion batteries is high-entropy perovskite oxides (HEPOs).663 Yan et al.664 used solid-state synthesis to fabricate [(Bi,Na)0.2(La,Li)0.2(Ce,K)0.2Ca0.2Sr0.2]TiO3 at 1000–1250 °C in air. The EDS data suggest that the Bi/Na, La/Li, and Ce/K molar ratios were 50/50. Excess Bi2O3 was added in order to attempt to compensate Bi loss by volatilisation. However, the A sites were intended to be populated with eight cations, of which four are known to exhibit high vapour pressures and consequent tendencies to volatilize. Although the stoichiometry appears to have remained relatively constant, any volatilisation would have resulted in A-site deficiency and probable consequent charge compensation by oxygen vacancy formation. It is clear that such a composition aims at the generation of oxygen vacancies as the eight A-site cations include one donor, two neutral, and five acceptor dopants. Further, the use of such a high number of cations is likely to impede recrystallisation and consequently enhance oxygen vacancy formation through structural disorder. XPS and EPR data revealed that this strategy was successful and that what were considered to be isolated oxygen vacancies were formed. It is probable that the  was relatively high as half-cell battery testing revealed a high initial discharge capacity of ∼125.9 mA h g−1, with excellent cycling stability; other attributes were an outstanding reversible capacity of 120.4 mA h g−1 and a capacity retention of ∼100% at 1000 mA g−1 after 300 cycles. Even at a high current density of 3000 mA g−1, a reversible capacity of 70 mA h g−1 was retained, indicating excellent rate performance. These outstanding capacity retention and rate performances were attributed to the complex mixed heterovalent A-site cations and the charge compensation mechanism in HEPO, respectively. This work highlights the considerable potential of perovskite-based high-entropy oxides as anode materials for Li-ion batteries.
As stated previously, solid solutions exhibit a specific saturation solubility for each temperature. At very low dopant concentrations, the dopants are inhomogeneously and distantly separated, which hinders recrystallisation and effectively destabilises the structure.665 As the dopant concentration increases, the dopant distribution becomes more homogeneous, thereby assisting recrystallisation and enhancing crystallinity. When the saturation solubility is exceeded, the solid solution can become supersaturated, which again destabilises the structure. The alternative is that the dopant precipitates, which results in surface decoration by the dopant but retention of the saturated solution. As the defects associated with solid solution formation can enhance the electrical conductivity,666 a homogeneous distribution of these is facilitated by a homogeneous distribution of the dopant.
Although conventional cation-disordered perovskites have been considered for use in Li-ion batteries to control Li ionic conductivity,667 cation disorder appears to be deleterious to cathodes in Li-ion batteries since the octahedral site hopping mechanism of diffusional Li transport is limited by it.668 Consequently, the alternative strategies of cation ordering, anion ordering, vacancy ordering, and structural anisotropy potentially can be applied to increase the ionic conductivity.
Lee et al.668 circumvented the disadvantage of cation disorder by synthesising a disordered Li2MoO3 perovskite with excess Li (Li1.211Mo0.467Cr0.3O2). The formation of percolated diffusion channels facilitated Li diffusion that were modelled for both layered and fully disordered perovskites. These materials exhibited excellent cyclability, revealing a decrease in energy density of ∼7% from 280 mA h g−1 after 10 cycles.
Santibáñez-Mendieta et al.669 used solid-state synthesis to fabricate the (La1−xLix)WLiO6 (x = 0.5) double perovskite (A2BB′O6) with a rock salt structure. This material exhibited site ordering of both W6+ and Li+. DFT simulations also indicated A-site ordering of La3+ and Li+. This study suggested that the Li+ hopping diffusion mechanism was facilitated by localised Li+ hops between neighbouring A sites and exchange between the A and B sites. The testing results revealed that the activation energy barrier to Li+ hopping was comparable to that of the best Li+ ion conductors.
Anion ordering appears to be much less common than cation ordering. One of a few such studies is that of Gamon et al.,670 who used solid-state synthesis to produce the Na2Fe2OS2 antiperovskite (or inverse perovskite) in which the cations and anions are in reversed positions. This material exhibits the anti-K2NiF4 structure, and it corresponds to an anti-Ruddlesden–Popper phase of n = 1, with anion ordering of O2− by S2−. The authors reported a decrease in power density of 25% from 225 mA h g−1 after 20 cycles.
Ma et al.217 attempted to increase the electronic conductivity while maintaining the ionic conductivity of cation-deficient (La1−xLix−y)MO3 (M = Ti, Cr, Mn, Fe, Co; x = 0.45, y = 0.09), which was fabricated by solid-state synthesis at 1100° or 1200 °C in air. The results suggested that ordered La vacancies enhanced the Li percolation pathways and hence the Li+ diffusion in this anode material. The electronic and ionic conductivities were relatively high at 2.04 × 10−3 S cm−1 and 8.53 × 10−5 S cm−1, respectively. The cyclability was excellent, revealing good energy density for the anode of 0.21 mA h cm−1 after 100 cycles.
Stratified perovskites, consisting of stacked layers of BO6 octahedra linked by Li substituted at the A-site, have been reported to enhance ionic conductivity. Zhang et al.136 reported A-site substitution in bulk layered (La1−xLix)TiO3 (x = 0.5) as a replacement for the widely used carbon-based anodes. This material was synthesised at 1250 °C in air, with 6.7 at% excess Li to compensate for volatilisation losses. Fig. 41a shows a schematic of the crystal structure, in which the A sites are shared between Li and La and Fig. 41b shows the results of DFT simulations, which indicated that Li+ diffusion was preferential in the x axis direction. The synthesised battery exhibited an average potential of ∼1.0V vs. Li+/Li and a high specific capacity of 225mA h g−1, sustaining 3000 cycles that involved a reversible phase transition. The latter observation is in contrast to benchmark layered materials, such as Li2TiSiO5, which exhibit irreversible phase separation and grain boundary movement during the charge/discharge process. These result in lattice mismatch and consequent cracking.
 Fig. 41  (a) Schematic of the crystal structure of stratified (La0.5Li0.5)TiO3 and (b) DFT simulation of the preferential Li+ diffusion path (yellow) in the x axis direction (reproduced from ref. 136 with permission from the Nature Publishing Group). 
7.1.1.2. Sodium-ion batteries. 
The implementation in Na in batteries is attractive as Na is abundant, easily extracted, and inexpensive.671 An advantage of its use in Na-ion batteries is that aluminium can be used as the anodic current collector because, unlike Li, Na does not react with or exhibit solid solubility with Al.672 Despite these advantages, challenges in developing Na intercalation materials capable of achieving high energy densities and cycling stability remain. However, the main concerns are the low extent of Na intercalation with the graphite cathode and thermodynamic instability of Na–graphite intercalation compounds.673Fig. 42 shows the energy density (capacity) of the materials that have been used in Na-ion batteries as both cathodes and anodes, although no perovskites are shown.
 Fig. 42  Plot of working potential against capacity (energy density) for electrode materials used in Na-ion batteries (reproduced from ref. 674 with permission from the American Chemical Society). 
Ti-Based perovskites, particularly Na2Ti3O7, with intercalated Na, low-voltage operation, and high capacity approaching ∼180 mA h g−1 have been reported.674,675 The high capacity are mainly due to the inherent ability to take up two Na+ ions per Ti3+ ↔ Ti4+ redox couple reaction.676 Other promising materials are trigonal Na4Ti5O12,677 monoclinic Na2Ti3O7,678 monoclinic Na2Ti6O13,679 monoclinic Li4Ti5O12,680 and spinel-type Na3LiTi5O12.681
The perovskite FeTiO3 (ilmenite) is significant because IVCT can take place between A site (Fe2+, Fe3+) and B site (Ti3+, Ti4+) cations, which is in contrast to the large majority of perovskites in which the A sites are occupied by univalent cations. Brugnetti et al.682 reported that FeTiO3 as the anode has a theoretical capacity of 530 mA g−1 and that the low electrical conductivity of this material could be modified by decorating it with carbon nanotubes. This resulted in a high energy density of 358.8 mA h−1 after 200 cycles at 100 mA g−1, an outstanding energy density of 201.8 mA h g−1 at 5000 mA g−1, and a high coulombic efficiency of ∼99%. This material can also be coupled with other perovskite cathodes, such as Na3V2(PO4)3, to make a full cell battery.683
Another example of a Ti-based perovskite with anodic properties is (Na1−xBix)TiO3 (x = 0.5), which exhibited rapid Na charge/discharge, a high capacity of ∼470 mA h g−1 at 100 mA g−1, and a 100% energy density of ∼215 mA h g−1 after 50 cycles at 100 mA g−1.684 These properties derived from 50 at% A-site substitution of Bi3+ by Na+, which was concluded to have resulted from the dual redox and ionic charge compensation mechanisms according to 2(Ti3+ → Ti4+) redox  and oxygen vacancy formation , as given in Table 7.
7.1.1.3. Lithium–air batteries. 
A schematic of the design of a typical Li–air battery is shown in Fig. 43. Such Li–air batteries possess a practical specific energy of ∼900 W h kg−1, which is an order of magnitude higher than that of Li-ion batteries.685,686 However, Li–air batteries currently face challenges in terms of low cyclability owing to parasitic reactions and poor coulomb efficiencies, which derive from sluggish OER and ORR on the cathode catalysts.
 Fig. 43  Schematic of the principle of an Li–air battery, with perovskite as the OER catalyst. 
The discharge process in Li–air batteries involves a half-cell oxidation reaction of Li, at the Li anode, to form Li+, and a half-cell reduction of O2 to form the superoxide radical ˙O2− by the reaction O2 + 2e− → ˙O2−, at the catalyst cathode/electrolyte interface.687 The cathodic reaction during the discharge process occurs at the surface of the cathode, between O2 and free Li+, resulting in the formation of LiO2, as encapsulated in Table 15 (reaction (1)). The formation of LiO2 by reduction of Li+ and O2 (reaction (1)) is a kinetically irreversible or quasi-reversible electrochemical process, resulting in high polarisation (thus requiring high overpotential) for the ORR. Subsequently, the LiO2 converts to Li2O2 through either chemical (reaction (2a)) or faradaic (reaction (2b)) reactions, which are competing alternatives. reaction (2a) involves a solution mechanism in which there is equilibrium between LiO2 solubility and adsorption on the cathode surface. In the latter case, Li2O2 grows in the solution and not on the surface. reaction (2b) involves a surface mechanism in which two soluble LiO2 molecules undergo a one-electron faradaic reaction at the surface. This results in the formation of a partial or continuous layer on the cathode surface, which results in low energy density, low power density, and short lifetime. However, individual Li2O2 particles for mass transfer are essential for high performance of a battery.688 In the ideal case, during the charging process, as-formed Li2O2 is reduced back to Li+ and superoxide. For a rechargeable battery, the OER involving oxidation of Li2O2 into Li+ and O2 is required. The two possible reactions are shown in reactions (3) and (4). Li–air batteries with aqueous electrolytes are encumbered by safety concerns owing to the generation of hydrogen gas as a result of the reaction between Li metal and water. However, the presence of oxygen can mitigate H2 generation. The corresponding ORR and OER reactions for Li–air batteries with an aqueous electrolyte are given in reactions (5)–(7).
Table 15 Charge–discharge reactions in Li–air batteries689





a Competitive alternative reaction pairs.
b Competitive alternative reaction pairs.





Nonaqueous (aprotic) electrolyte
Oxygen reduction reaction, discharge at cathode (ORR)
Li+ + e− + O2 → LiO2 (E0 = 3.0 V vs. Li/Li+)

2LiO2 → Li2O2 + O2a (chemical reaction)
Reaction (2a)


LiO2 + Li+ + e− → Li2O2a (E0 = 2.96 V vs. Li/Li+)
Reaction (2b)


Oxygen evolution reaction, charge at cathode (OER)
Li2O2(s) → 2Li+ + 2e− + O2b (two-electron process)
Reaction (3)


Li2O2(s) → Li+ + e− + LiO2b (one-electron process)
Reaction (4)


Aqueous electrolyte
Oxygen reduction reaction, discharge at cathode (ORR)
4Li + O2 + 2H2O → 4LiOH (base electrolyte)
Reaction (5)


4Li + O2 + 4H+ → 4LiOH + 2H2O (acid electrolyte)
Reaction (6)


Oxygen evolution reaction, charge at cathode (OER)
4(LiOH + H2O) → 4Li + 6H2O + O2
Reaction (7)




Although noble metal oxide catalysts, such as IrO2 and RuO2, accelerate the kinetics and reduce the overpotential, their high costs limit their applicability. The most promising alternatives have been shown to be perovskites since they exhibit stoichiometric, structural, and electrical flexibility, low costs, and high surface-to-volume ratios.690 The role of the perovskite catalyst in Li–air batteries is to form O2 at the cathode surface through the OER, which is critical to the battery performance. The perovskite addresses the well-known shortcoming of the OER, which is the slow kinetics owing to the four-electron oxidation process, which necessitates a high overpotential.691 While regular shortcomings of Li–air batteries are low capacity retention during cycling and high required overpotentials, Mn-based perovskites as heterogeneous electrocatalysts have been shown to ameliorate these problems considerably.692 The A site can be substituted partially or fully in AMnO3 perovskites, where A generally is a rare-earth element. These perovskites have the advantage of being able to accommodate high concentrations of oxygen vacancies without structural change, which enhances the mobility of lattice oxygen and thus results in increased cathode conductivity and cycling stability.693
Cong et al.694 used solid-state synthesis to fabricate (separately) cation-deficient (La1−x)FeO3−δ (x = 0.15) and Fe2O3 by sol–gel, followed by calcining at 600° or 900 °C. Apparently, the authors also fabricated a mechanical mixture of LaFeO3 and Fe2O3. It appears that the latter was intended to replicate the phase assemblage of LaFeO3 and Fe2O3 that formed following calcination, although it is inexplicable why sintering was not done for the mechanical mixture. The authors proposed a mechanism of segregation based on oxygen vacancy formation, migration, and aggregation, followed by interaction with the Fe2O3 secondary phase and its reconstruction, as shown in the upper part of Fig. 44a. However, this process cannot involve segregation as this phenomenon involves cation diffusion driven by lattice stress, so it is not possible to segregate (deformable) oxygen vacancies. Consequently, the performance of this material is likely to derive from the formation of heterojunctions by the Fe2O3 reaction product formed during calcining. This material exhibited a discharge energy density of 500 mA h g−1 at a current density of 0.1 A g−1 and a capacity retention for α-Fe2O3–LaFeO3−δ of 108 cycles, which can be compared to those for α-Fe2O3 of 21 cycles and (La1−x)FeO3−δ of 51 cycles. This heterojunction structure also exhibited a low required overpotential, enhanced power density, and long cycling stability, as shown in the lower part of Fig. 44a.
 Fig. 44  (a) Proposed mechanism of Fe2O3 surface reconstruction and polarisation curves of La0.85FeO3−δ electrodes at a current density of 100 mA g−1 with an energy density of 500 mA h g−1 (reproduced from ref. 694 with permission from American Chemical Society) and (b) charge/discharge resulting in decomposition/formation of Li2O2 for LaNi(O3−xNx) electrodes (reproduced from ref. 695 with permission from American Chemical Society). 
Zhang et al.695 synthesised N-doped LaNi(O3−xNx) by sol–gel synthesis, sintering at 750 °C in oxygen and nitriding at 450 °C under NH3. The authors concluded that N substitution resulted in an increase in the concentrations of Ni3+ and oxygen vacancies at the surface. Although this cannot be explained by ionic, electronic, or redox charge compensation, the nitridation process is likely to have caused Ni3+ → Ni2+ reduction, with Ni2+ being identified by XPS. As this would involve an ionic radius size increase from 0.70 nm (d8 – low spin) to 0.83 nm, this would provide the driving force for segregation. A high concentration of surface oxygen vacancies derived from the enhanced nitridation at the surface would be expected. The high concentration of surface oxygen vacancies facilitated the decomposition of Li2O2, as shown by the schematic of the mechanism in Fig. 44b, which improved the OER/ORR catalytic activity, discharge capacity, and cyclability.
Hegde et al.696 reported the synthesis of the defect-rich CaMnO3 perovskite using sol–gel, calcining at 900 °C, and oxygen vacancy formation by heating at 300 °C under H2. The observation of Mn3+ in the XPS data indicates that Mn4+ → Mn3+ reduction  occurred, but H2 reduction could also have resulted in the formation of oxygen vacancies , as indicated by TGA, XRD, and TEM. As these are charge compensating defects, one caused the other. This dual charge compensation is shown by the defect equilibria . In addition, although not considered, it is likely that IVCT plays a key role in the performance of this perovskite as a cathode catalyst for Li–air batteries through the reversible electron exchange reaction Mn3+ + Li+ ↔ Mn4+ + Li0. This material exhibited improved OER/ORR activity, a high power density of 500 mA g−1, a high energy density of 500 mA h g−1, a low required overpotential, and improved cyclability.
7.1.1.4. Zinc–air batteries. 
Zn–air batteries are promising largely owing to several advantages. First, they have a theoretical energy density of 1086 W h kg−1, which is approximately five times that of Li-ion batteries owing to the fact that they can be operated in air.697 Second, the appropriate operating voltage (∼1.65 V) of Zn–air batteries is insufficient to induce the electrolysis of the water of the aqueous electrolyte. Third, the crustal abundance of Zn and the free supply of oxygen for the air cathode allow Zn–air batteries to be manufactured very economically (<US$10 kW−1 h−1 for Zn–air batteries vs. US$400–800 kW−1 h−1 for Li-ion batteries). The electrochemical reactions that occur at the anode and cathode sides in Zn–air batteries are given in Table 16.
Table 16 Charge–discharge reactions in Zn–air batteries698




Zn–air batteries
Electrochemical reaction




Anode (Zn)
Zn + 4OH− → Zn(OH)42− + 2e−


Zn(OH)42− → ZnO + H2O + 2OH−


Cathode (perovskite + oxide)
O2 + 4e− + 2H2O → 4OH−


Full-cell reaction
2Zn + O2 → 2ZnO


Parasitic reaction
Zn + 2H2O → Zn(OH)2 + H2




At the perovskite cathode, penetration of the surrounding air into the porous electrode results in the electrochemical reduction of oxygen at the interface between the cathode and the electrolyte.699 The ORR that takes place at the air electrode is similar to that in alkaline hydrogen fuel cells, where hydroxide ions are the primary product.700 Consequently, these two energy-conversion systems share correspondingly similar electrode architectures and catalyst material design principles. Thus, many ORR catalysts applicable to fuel cells are promising candidates for Zn–air batteries. However, Zn–air batteries have the disadvantage that, during the discharge cycle, an undesired parasitic reaction between Zn and H2O results in H2 evolution, which is responsible for gradual self-corrosion at the Zn anode surface.701 This has the effect of scavenging active sites and reducing efficiency.
Qian et al.702 used sol–gel and calcination at 600 °C to synthesise single-phase Ce-doped LaCoO3 (4.2–5.6 at% Ce-doped but without stoichiometric compensation), as shown in Fig. 45a. The refined crystal structure, shown in Fig. 45b, indicated that the solid solubility mechanism was Ce substitution for La, thus giving the stoichiometry (La1−xCex)CoO3. Hence, since Ce is a donor dopant, charge compensation cannot be by oxygen vacancy formation. Therefore, it is by redox, giving the defect equilibria . These equilibria were confirmed by the identification of Co2+ by XPS and EELS, as shown in Fig. 45c. This perovskite exhibited enhanced OER/ORR activity, which was attributed to the strengthening of the binding of oxygen-related groups on the Co sites owing to the existence of an intermediate spin state, which can occur in 3d4 to 3d6 transition metal ions.703 The conversion from low spin to intermediate spin causes increased overlap from Co d and O p orbital hybridisation and occupied states near the Fermi level, as suggested in Fig. 45d and e, which leads to redistribution of some electrons from the t2g to the eg orbitals, as indicated in Fig. 45f. The (La0.994Ce0.056)CoO3 perovskite exhibited the most improved properties, including outstanding OER/ORR activity, a low required overpotential of 0.96 V, an ultrahigh specific capacity of 783 mA h g−1, an excellent durability of ∼100% capacity retention after 160 h, and a good discharge rate.
 Fig. 45  Ce-Doped LaCoO3: (a) XRD data, (b) refined crystal structure, (c) EELS analysis for Co L-edge, (d) density of states (DOS) for undoped and Ce-doped LaCoO3, (e) orbital overlap of low-spin (LS) and intermediate-spin (IS) states of Co 3d with that of O 2p, and (f) EELS analysis for O K-edge (reproduced from ref. 702 with permission from John Wiley & Sons).

7.1.2. Solid-state electrolytes. 
The flammable liquid electrolyte in Li-ion batteries and the associated safety concern has led to the development of alternatives, particularly all-solid-state lithium batteries (ASSLBs), which exhibit a discharge capacity comparable to that of traditional Li-ion batteries.723 These devices incorporate solid-state electrolytes for batteries, which must be designed to exhibit high ionic conductivity at room temperature and to operate safely.724,725 (La1−xLax)TiO3 perovskites have emerged as outstanding solid-state electrolytes owing to their superior ionic conductivity, wide and stable potential windows, low interfacial resistance, excellent thermal stability, high elastic moduli, low costs, and environmental friendliness. A disadvantage of these and similar perovskites is their greater bulk densities (typically >5000 kg m−3) relative to that of the competitor polyethylene oxide (PEO) electrolyte (typically <1000 kg m−3). Ideally, the mass of a perovskite electrolyte should be equivalent to that of the sum of the masses of the competitor electrolyte and the membrane separator that isolates the cathode from the anode.
The energy densities of ASSLBs depend largely on microstructure (grain size), macrostructure (thickness), and electrical properties (conductivity, impedance, band gap, etc.), and defect equilibria of their components.726–728 Outside of the intrinsic properties, the preceding characteristics depend to a large extent on the extrinsic effects of processing. Solid oxide electrolytes generally are densified by pressureless sintering or hot pressing to yield thicknesses typically in the range of 200–1000 μm.729,730 In order to avoid limited ionic diffusion through the membrane separator in thick solid-state electrolytes, the technique of phase inversion has been developed.731 In this method, a mixture of perovskite and a solution of dissolved polymer is immersed in another solution that is not a solvent for the polymer. This results in the precipitation of the polymer in the perovskite matrix. Subsequent oxidation removes the polymer, leaving behind a continuous fine-scale pore network.
Tape casting recently has been shown to be capable of large-scale production of freestanding solid electrolyte films with minimal thicknesses.732 Even more recently, Jiang et al.733 reported the synthesis of (La0.56Li0.34)TiO3 films as thin as ∼29 μm using tape casting. In this method, a slurry containing the perovskite powder, triethanolamine dispersant, benzyl butyl phthalate plasticiser, polyvinyl butyral binder, and ethanol solvent was tape cast onto a polyethylene terephthalate (PET) release film. Two layers of the tape-cast films were joined by hot pressing at 100 °C and 10 MPa, followed by organic oxidation at 500 °C, heating at 1050 °C to prevent reformation of Li2CO3, and final sintering at 1260 °C. SEM images of the ultrathin solid oxide electrolyte are shown in Fig. 46a–c. Although the Li ionic conductivity of this electrolyte was shown to be mid-range at 2.0 × 10−5 S cm−1, the discharge capacity was relatively high at 145 mA h g−1 and the capacity retention ratio was high at 86.2% after 50 cycles, as shown in Fig. 46d, while exhibiting the galvanostatic charge–discharge behaviour for the full cell battery shown in Fig. 46e. Finally, the flexural strength was high at 264 MPa.
 Fig. 46  (La0.56Li0.34)TiO3 tape-cast films of ∼29 μm thickness: (a) cross sectional SEM image before sintering, (b) cross-sectional SEM image after sintering at 1260 °C, (c) SEM image of the surface after sintering at 1050 °C, (d) cycling performance at 0.1C (inset: images of pasted cathode and anode), and (e) galvanostatic charge/discharge curves of the Li/PEO/(Li0.34La0.56)TiO3/LiFePO4 full cell battery at 65 °C (reproduced from ref. 733 with permission from John Wiley & Sons). 

7.2. Supercapacitors
Although rechargeable batteries usually possess high energy densities, the power densities and long-term stability are often insufficient for many applications.734 Supercapacitors are alternative energy-storage devices that have been used extensively in a wide range of portable and size-limited devices.215,735 Supercapacitors are categorised into three mechanistic areas, which area summarised in Table 18. They differ in their operational mechanisms in that electrochemical double layer capacitors (EDLCs) store energy through electrostatic adsorption over the electrode surface. Pseudocapacitors utilise electrochemical redox reactions of the electrode materials during charging and discharging, and intercalation pseudocapacitors store energy by rapid intercalation and deintercalation of ionic charge carriers through atomic channels.
Table 18 Summary of types of supercapacitors




Type
Advantages
Disadvantages
Typical electrode materials




Electrochemical double layer capacitors (EDLC)
Ultrahigh power densities
Low energy densities
Carbon-based materials


Pseudocapacitors
Higher energy densities
Low capacitance retention
Metal oxides


Lower power densities
Conductive polymers


Intercalation pseudocapacitors
High power densities
Limited to specific material structures
Perovskites


Highest energy densities
Nb, Ru, W oxides




Although the applicability of perovskites as anodes for supercapacitors emerged only in 2014,736 this work has generated considerable subsequent investigations using other perovskites. Defective perovskite pseudocapacitors with high concentrations of oxygen vacancies have exhibited improved electrical conductivity and energy-storage capacity relative to those of stoichiometric perovskites owing to bulk intercalation/deintercalation of oxygen species at the vacancy sites.736–738 In contrast to metal–oxide pseudocapacitors, high capacitance is determined not principally by the surface area but largely by the rate and depth of intercalation. However, the large radii of oxygen species relative to those of other charge carriers reduce their diffusivity and have a greater impact on the structural stability of perovskites. In effect, the diffusivity of the oxygen species in intercalation pseudocapacitor electrodes is determined by the perovskite architectures and the densities of oxygen vacancies.
Mefford et al.736 reported work on LaMnO3±x (x = 0.09) and showed for the first time that the oxygen-deficient LaMnO2.91 provided a specific structural architecture of cations that facilitated a charge storage mechanism consisting of intercalation of oxygen species under electrical polarisation, occupation of oxygen vacancies, and oxygen mobility by hopping.
Owing to the fact that intercalation perovskites generally are immersed in a strong alkaline electrolyte (KOH or NaOH) during charge/discharge, the resultant chemical reactions may alter the bulk phase structures, stoichiometries, defect concentrations, surface areas, and surface compositions of the perovskites. Further, the use of such strong electrolytes suggests the advisability of being aware of the susceptibility to leaching of both lattice ions and substitutional solutes and its effect on the structural and performance stability.
Liu et al.739 reported a comparative study of the anion-intercalation supercapacitors SrCoO3−δ and (Sr1−xBax)(Co1−yFey)O3−δ (x = 0.5, y = 0.2); data for Co3O4 were also included. Although the rationale for Ba substitution for Sr was not stated, the intention may have been to cause structural destabilisation and associated defect formation owing to ionic radii difference. In order for charge-compensating oxygen vacancies to result from Fe substitution for Co, the latter ion must be an acceptor. Although XPS data for Co revealed the presence of Co2+, Co3+ (principal valence), and Co4+, the absence of XPS data for Fe precludes assessment of whether Fe acted as an acceptor dopant. As shown in Fig. 47, the results for gravimetric capacitance showed that SrCoO3−δ exhibited a value ∼56% of that of (Sr1−xBax)(Co1−yFey)O3−δ after 70 cycles. However, with increasing number of cycles, the former increased to a maximum at 3000 cycles, followed by slow deterioration. The latter rapidly increased over 70 cycles, deteriorated by ∼15% over the immediately following cycles, and then deteriorated approximately linearly over 5000 cycles. The significant increase in the electrochemical performance for SrCoO3−δ is attributed to the increased surface area based on data, provided in Fig. 47, for the direct leaching in KOH of Sr. It is also likely that the increasing performance of the more stable SrCoO3−δ derived from the preferential leaching of Sr from the A sites with minimal structural deterioration and the resultant generation of charge-compensating oxygen vacancies. The subsequent decrease in performance could be interpreted in terms of structural deterioration following long-term testing. In contrast, although the data for less stable (Sr0.5Ba0.5)(Co0.8Fe0.2)O3−δ during the first 70 cycles were not explained, the relatively low surface areas of these samples suggest that the rapid increase in performance derived not from the achievement of surface wetting and saturation by KOH but from increasing intercalation. The rapid subsequent decrease could be interpreted in terms of reduction in surface area due to agglomeration740 or structural destabilisation through the B site. The subsequent linear decrease is likely to have resulted from the gradual leaching of Sr and Ba at the A site.
 Fig. 47  Effect of cycling on the gravimetric capacitance of SrCoO3−δ and (Sr0.5Ba0.5)(Co0.8Fe0.2)O3−δ (reproduced from ref. 739 with permission from the American Chemical Society). 
Forslund et al.741 reported a comparative study between stoichiometric and oxygen-deficient variants of the perovskite CaMnO3−δ and the Ruddlesden–Popper structure Ca2MnO4−δ. The authors used an overpressure of H2 at 325 °C to generate mutually charge-compensating Mn reduction and oxygen vacancy formation. This work revealed that the main charge/discharge mechanism is affected by the crystal structures of these two perovskites, which were differentiated by rock-salt layers in Ca2MnO4−δ. While oxygen transport occurred through oxygen lattice diffusivity via the oxygen vacancies for both perovskites, the oxygen transport also took place via oxygen ions intercalated in the interstitial sites of the rock-salt layers of Ca2MnO4−δ. Fig. 48a and b show the peak current variations against scan rate (v1/2 and v) during charge/discharge through cyclic voltammetry analyses for anion intercalation/deintercalation. For CaMnO3−δ, the peak current varied linearly against v (R2 = 1.00) indicating that the dominant mechanism for such rapid charge/discharge processes is capacitive and so the redox reaction occurred mainly on the surface or near-surface of the perovskite. However, for Ca2MnO4−δ, the peak currents were proportional to v1/2 (R2 ≥ 0.99), indicating that the charge/discharge process is governed principally by diffusion rather than a capacitive mechanism. Fig. 48c shows oxygen intercalation in the perovskites, and Fig. 48d shows the deintercalation. These data reveal that the intercalation diffusion rates were superior for (i) the reduced variants relative to the oxidised variants of both perovskites and (ii) both types of CaMnO3−δ relative to the Ruddlesden–Popper Ca2MnO4−δ. This counter-intuitive outcome was explained in terms of the scan rates during charge/discharge. That is, with slow scan rates, interstitial intercalation of oxygen contributes significantly to the increased capacitance of the Ruddlesden–Popper Ca2MnO4−δ. With fast scan rates, there is insufficient time for this mechanism to be effected.
 Fig. 48  Dependence of the peak current density on the scan rate for (a) CaMnO3−δ, (b) Ca2MnO4−δ (measured at potentials of −0.10 V (black lines) and −0.25 V (red lines)), (c) intercalation rates of oxygen for all four perovskite variants, and (d) deintercalation rates of oxygen for all four perovskite variants (diffusion rates measured using a bounded diffusion model) (reproduced from ref. 741 with permission from the Royal Society of Chemistry). 
As another example of the pseudocapacitors, Guo et al.742 used plasma etching with and without H2 + Ar in order to generate high oxygen vacancy concentrations (∼8–10%) in LaCoO3−δ. An asymmetric supercapacitor was fabricated using the highly defective LaCoO3−δ as the cathode and graphene oxide as the anode. The device produced an energy density of 47.64 W h kg−1 at a power density of 804.4 W kg−1 and a capacitance retention of 96% after 4000 cycles at 5 A g−1.
Liu et al.207 used sol–gel and sintering in air at 950 °C to prepare the A-site-substituted (Pr1−xBax)MnO3−δ (x = 0.5) single perovskite; heating under H2 at 800 °C was used to convert (Pr0.5Ba0.5)MnO3−δ to the A-site-substituted double perovskite (Pr1−xBax)2Mn2O6−δ (x = 0.5). The layered structure resulted in both high oxygen diffusion rates and oxygen vacancy concentration, resulting in a very high gravimetric capacitance of 1034.8 F g−1 and an excellent volumetric capacitance of ∼2535.3 F cm−3 at a current density of 1 A g−1. The mechanism for the intercalation of oxygen was investigated using ex situ near-edge X-ray adsorption fine structure (NEXAFS) for both charged and discharged double perovskite, as shown in Fig. 49a. The Mn L-edge spectra are differentiated into two multiplets of L3-edge positioned at 640–645 eV and L2-edge positioned at 650–655 eV. For the L3-edge, the predominance of Mn4+ and Mn3+ during charging is clear, although the L2-edge is non-indicative. During discharging, the L3- and L2-edges shifted to lower energies, suggesting a general decrease in the Mn valence. Accordingly, the increased Mn3+ peak intensity, the decreased Mn4+ peak intensity, and the unchanged Mn2+ peak intensity show that the dominant redox reactions are Mn3+ → Mn4+ upon charging and Mn4+ → Mn3+ upon discharging. Fig. 49b shows a schematic of oxygen ion diffusion (i) along the interlayer spaces and (ii) through the vacancies. Fig. 49c and d show DOS results obtained by DFT simulations of the cubic and hexagonal polymorphs that were identified by XRD, where the single perovskite consisted of both cubic and hexagonal polymorphs, but the double perovskite consisted solely of the cubic polymorph. These data show that the cubic polymorph exhibited no Eg but that the hexagonal polymorph had an Eg of 1.39 eV. The authors attributed the excellent electrochemical performance to the high electrical conductivity implied by the absent Eg for the single crystal structure of the double perovskite.
 Fig. 49  (a) NEXAFS spectra obtained at the Mn 2p adsorption edge, (b) refined crystal structure of (PrBa)Mn2O6−δ, showing oxygen intercalation during charging. DFT+U calculated density of states (DOS) for (c) cubic and (d) hexagonal PBM (reproduced from ref. 207 with permission from the Royal Society of Chemistry). 
Finally, defective oxide perovskites can also perform as electrodes for cation intercalation/deintercalation. In these oxygen-deficient perovskites, the positively charged oxygen vacancies serve to establish associated electric fields as shown in Fig. 50, which act as fast-ion transport channels for Li+ intercalation/deintercalation. Pan et al.737 fabricated oxygen-deficient BiFeO3−δ by electrochemical deposition on N-doped carbon fibres, immersion in melamine, and calcination at 800 °C under N2. This anode was incorporated in a battery–supercapacitor hybrid (BSH) and the electrochemical testing revealed an excellent capacity of 155 mA h g−1 at 1 A g−1 and a high cycling stability of 92.6% after 10000 cycles at an operating voltage of 1.9 V in a LiCl electrolyte. Such outstanding performance was ascribed to the rapid Li+ intercalation/deintercalation in the BiFeO3 structure accompanied by simultaneous reversible lattice distortion.
 Fig. 50  Refined crystal structures of BiFeO3−δ during charging (left) and discharging (right), showing electric fields around oxygen vacancies (δ) in red and the surrounding electric field for Li+ transport in green (reproduced from ref. 737 with permission from Elsevier). 
8. Defect engineering of perovskites in other applications
There have been many successful investigations to improve the activity and stability of oxide perovskites through defect engineering for applications such as chemical looping, solid oxide fuel cells (SOFC), protonic ceramic electrochemical cells (PCECs), and ceramic membrane reactors. The A-site and B-site cations in oxide perovskites play critical roles in determining the catalytic activity. Different A-site and B-site cations can induce different crystal structures, structural distortion, defect concentration changes, and variations in the ionic valences.
The present section reviews some experimental studies relevant to the preceding principal applications as well as complementary computational approaches, particularly the Nernst–Planck–Poisson (NPP) model, to characterise defect transport in electrolyte membranes in protonic ceramic fuel cells and electrolysers. This section also discusses the role of defects and how their tuning can be used to improve catalytic properties.
8.1. Chemical looping
The concept of chemical looping refers to the deconstruction of a chemical reaction into multiple subreactions. The chemical looping process involves the circulation of an oxygen carrier between a reduction reactor (also known as a fuel reactor) and an oxidation reactor (also known as an air reactor) to close the loop.743 A critical aspect of this technology is the reduction and regeneration of an oxygen carrier material, which usually is a metal oxide. Owing to their unique abilities to accommodate different cationic dopants at the A and/or B sites, to create resultant charge-compensating and cation and/or oxygen vacancies, and to cause redox charge compensation, oxide perovskites can be tailored to act as oxygen carriers and redox catalysts.3,4 Some perovskites used in the chemical looping process include Sr(Mn1−xNix)O3−δ,744 (La1−xSrx)FeO3,745 (La1−xCex)BO3−δ (B = Co, Mn),746 Ca(Ti0.125Mn0.875)O3−δ,747 Ca(Mg0.1Mn0.9)O3−δ,748 (Sr0.2La0.8)(Co0.2Fe0.8)O3,749 and Ca(MxMn1−x)O3−δ (M = Ti, Fe, Mg).750
Strontium lanthanum ferrite perovskites ((SrxLa1−x)FeO3−δ) have been investigated widely as oxygen carriers in chemical looping for the partial oxidation of CH4 to syngas.751–753 Syngas is a fuel gas mixture consisting primarily of hydrogen and carbon monoxide. In this process, CH4 adsorbs onto surface-Fe sites and dissociates stepwise into CH3, CH2, CH, and C. The dissociated hydrogen ions undergo reductive coupling at the Fe sites to form H2. At the same time, subsurface O ions diffuse to the vacancy sites (formed in the defective perovskite), and they form bonds with the adsorbed C radicals, ultimately releasing CO. In addition to syngas, methane also can be converted to other value-added chemicals. Oxidative coupling of methane (OCM) involves the direct conversion of natural gas (primarily methane) into the C2 hydrocarbons C2H6 and C2H4.754 In chemical looping for OCM, CH4 first dissociates into CH3 and H; two CH3 groups then form C2H6. Subsequently, C2H6 undergoes homogeneous or heterogeneous dehydrogenation to give C2H4. It has been reported that increasing the concentration of oxygen vacancies serves to lower the activation barriers for C–H and Fe–C bond cleavage and thus favours CH4 activation and partial oxidation.751,755,756
Zhang et al.757 recently investigated the role of A-site cations in modulating the oxygen capacity of iron-based perovskites in chemical looping for syngas production from methane. They reported that doping substitution of La by Sr to form (SrxLa1−x)(Al0.2Fe0.8)O3 significantly nearly tripled the oxygen capacity from 1.0 mmol g−1 (x = 0.1) to 2.7 mmol g−1 (x = 0.5). This also increased the oxygen vacancy concentration, resulting in enhanced CO selectivity to >94% and suppressed coke deposition over reduced oxygen carriers. Characterisation demonstrated that the dynamic crystal reconstruction of Ruddlesden–Popper (SrLan)(AlxFen−x)O3n+1 (n = 1 and 2) exhibited good methane activation and oxygen transport properties.
8.2. Solid oxide fuel cells (SOFCs)
The SOFC system, which consists of a cathode, an anode, and an electrolyte, is an alternative concept for off-grid, distributed, electric power generation.758 However, manufacturing cost-competitive SOFCs with appropriate power outputs, lifetimes, and reliability at minimal working temperatures, which currently require >1000 °C, remains a challenge.
Intermediate-temperature SOFCs are solid electrochemical cells that can convert the chemical energy contained in gaseous fuels into electrical energy and heat at operating temperatures of 700–1000 °C.759 Further, these devices have very low environmental emissions and exhibit high chemical-to-electrical energy conversion efficiencies.760 Recent research has focused on perovskite-based materials as alternatives to the favoured yttria-stabilised zirconia (YSZ) and gadolinium-doped ceria (GCO) as electrodes.761,762 In 1994, Ishihara et al.763 reported high oxide ion conductivity in (SrxLa1−x)(Mg1−yGay)O3−δ. The ionic conductivity was increased by co-doping the A-site with Sr and B-site with Mg as acceptor substitutionals, both of which would generate oxygen vacancies. Higher-order Ruddlesden–Popper phases (n > 1) have also been investigated as SOFC cathodes. Amow and Skinner152 prepared Ruddlesden–Popper Lan+1NinO3n+1 (n = 2 or 3) phases, which yielded anion deficient La3Ni2O7−δ or La4Ni3O10−δ, respectively. These materials exhibited improved electrical conductivity, thermal stability, and electrode performance.
Cobalt-based oxide perovskites have been studied widely as cathode materials in SOFCs, including (Sr0.4La0.6)CoO3764 and (Ba0.5Sr0.5)(Fe0.2Co0.8)O3−δ,765 owing to the high concentrations of oxygen vacancies. There is extensive interest in mixed ionic–electronic conducting cathode materials for SOFCs that operate below 600 °C, including (Sm0.5Sr0.5)CoO3,766 (Sr0.4La0.6)1−x(Co0.2Fe0.8)O3,767 (Ba0.5Sr0.5)(Fe0.2Co0.8)O3−δ,768 and (Sm0.5Sr0.5)CoO3.769
Xia et al.770 prepared anodic materials that combined defect design and catalyst impregnation. In this study, a porous A-site deficient perovskite (Sr0.15La0.57)TiO3 was synthesised by a modified polyacrylamide gel route, which was used to impregnate the pores with 3.4–8.3 wt% (Gd0.1Ce0.9)O2−δ and 2.0–6.3 wt% Ni/Cu, as an alternative anode for SOFCs. The electrodes were prereduced at 900 °C for 12 h in H2 before impregnation, thus facilitating oxygen vacancy formation. The results revealed that the lowest anodic polarisation resistances of 0.5 Ω cm2 and 0.73 Ω cm2 at 900 °C and 800 °C, respectively, were obtained for the (Sr0.15La0.57)TiO3 anode impregnated with 3.9 wt% Ni and 6.6 wt% Ce0.9Gd0.1O2−δ.770

Table 19 summarises the performances of various perovskite cathodes and anodes in fuel cells in terms of the peak power densities (PPDs).

8.3. Protonic ceramic electrochemical cells (PCECs)
Intermediate-temperature protonic ceramic electrochemical cells (PCECs) represent an emerging technology that can produce hydrogen and generate electricity in a single device in the temperature range (400–600 °C) lower than that required for SOFCs. PCECs also offer other advantages, such as high efficiencies, longer system durability, and less expensive materials.784 The principle is based on the application of a solid-state electrolyte with high proton conductivity that results from low activation energy for proton conduction.785,786
Tang et al.787 used a wet-chemistry self-combustion method to synthesise what was considered to be A-site cation ordered and deficient (Pr(Ca0.2Ba0.8))0.95Co2O6−δ layered perovskite, followed by calcining in air at 1000 °C for 4 h. The layered structure resulted in both high oxygen diffusivity and oxygen vacancy concentrations, which were facilitated by the A-site deficiency. A high electrolysis current density of −0.72 A cm−2 at 1.3 V and a peak power density of 0.540 W cm−2 were achieved at 600 °C. The long-term stability testing of the cell in fuel cell mode at 0.7 V and in electrolysis mode at 1.3 V at 500 °C for 160 h showed no observable degradation.
8.4. Ceramic membrane reactors

8.4.1. Background. 
The oxide perovskites used for membrane reactor applications are typically mixed ionic–electronic conducting (MIEC) materials. It is possible for perovskites to exhibit multiple simultaneous forms of conductivity, which can be electronic (electrons e−, electron holes h˙, delocalised small polarons ) and ionic (cations, anions, anion vacancies, protons in , oxygen vacancies ).785,788–790 It is well known that many semiconducting materials exhibit electrical conductivity at room temperature, while ionic conductivity requiring high temperatures at ≥500 °C under oxygen (O2), water vapour (H2O), or hydrogen (H2) partial pressures. The type of defects and their concentrations are the determining factors in the ionic conductivity and thus performance of the membrane. At high temperatures, both conductivity mechanisms operate, which may not be desirable, although one or the other tends to be predominant.791 This becomes more critical when using defective perovskites as protonic conducting fuel cells (PCFCs). The formation of protonic conductivity is contingent upon the simultaneous presence of an oxygen vacancy in the lattice and water vapour.792 The water dissociates upon incorporation in a perovskite to form a hydroxyl group and proton. The hydroxyl group then occupies the vacancy site, and the remaining hydrogen ion forms a covalent bond with a surface oxygen ion. The relevant reaction is (18)
As these hydrogen ions are mobile, they can contribute to the bulk conductivity of perovskites, as shown in Fig. 51. While these ionic defects give rise to conductivity, the defects are specifically protonic defects, and hence the conductivity is protonic.
 Fig. 51  Proton conductivity of various oxides calculated from data for proton concentrations and mobility according to Norby and Larring.798 
As discussed previously, B-site substitution with cations of lower oxidation states (viz., acceptors) results in the formation of oxygen vacancies from ionic charge compensation. Therefore, it is possible for perovskites to act as protonic conductors when water vapour is incorporated in the lattice at intermediate temperatures, typically 500°–600 °C.789Eqn (18) indicates that the proton conductivity is proportional, in principle, to the , which depends on both the amount and valence of the dopant.793 While the  is determined by the amount and valence of the dopant, the charge-carrier density is determined by the extent of lattice hydroxylation. Further, although A-site substitution generally has only a minimal effect on the proton transfer, it can assist the process indirectly by being used to minimise structural distortion.794 This has been confirmed through a study of B-site substitution in the perovskite Ba(Y0.2Zr0.8)O3−δ, which exhibited increasing protonic conductivity with increasing substitution up to a maximum at 20 at% Y, at which point it decreased owing to structural distortion, although excessive [H+] was offered as an alternative explanation.795 Chen et al.796 studied the lattice expansion from A-site substitution of La3+ (0.172 nm) by Sr2+ (1.32 nm) and consequent equilibrium oxygen vacancy formation in (SrxLa1−x)CoO3−δ, where x = 0.2, 0.4, or 0.7. The resultant nonlinear trend was attributed to relaxation of the lattice expansion from substitution by the introduction of lattice-contracting oxygen vacancies.
Although the more commonly applied B-site substitution is expected to enhance the performance of protonic conducting perovskites, very low proton conductivity at low dopant concentrations has been observed. Mather et al.797 reported computational calculations for lattice simulations using the Born model (based on the potential energy of the system in terms of atomic coordinates), energetics of defects, oxygen migration, and dopant incorporation. This study revealed that dilute substitution of trivalent cations (Nd3+, Gd3+) in the perovskite Sr(Yb0.1Ce0.9)O3−δ is more energetically favourable at the A site (Sr) rather than the B site (Ce). This would have the significant effect of causing the dopant to act as a donor rather than an acceptor, so it would annihilate oxygen vacancies and so inhibit proton uptake. This study reiterates the previously mentioned caution concerning the potentially incorrect assumption that the targeted substitution will take place.


8.4.2. Measurement of ionic conductivity. 
Perovskites of the general formula of A2+B4+O3, where the A site is occupied by Sr, Ba, or Ca, and the B site is occupied by Ce or Zr, have shown proton conductivity at high temperatures. As discussed previously, the introduction of an acceptor dopant at the B site yields greatly improved proton conductivity and chemical stability.
The contributions of charge carriers vary depending on the working temperature (T), oxygen partial pressure (PO2), and hydrogen partial pressure (PH2). Nomura and Kageyama799 used comprehensive impedance and conductivity measurements to investigate the predominant variations in the type of defect charge carriers, including proton, oxide ion, and hole conduction domains, for the Ba(Y0.2Zr0.8)O3−δ perovskite as a function of T, PO2, and PH2. For the data obtained under controlled atmospheres in the range 150–1000 °C, the results yielded an informative charge carrier map for PH2vs. PO2 at 400°, 500°, and 600 °C, indicating the predominant H+, O2−, and h˙ conduction domains. This was overlaid with lines for the ionic transference numbers tH+ = tO2− = 0.5 at three values of PH2O at 600 °C.
Below 600 °C in the presence of H2O and/or H2 gas, the predominant conduction mechanism was by protons, the conductivity of which at 600 °C was 4.2 × 10−3 S cm−1; the apparent activation energy at 500–600 °C in 40% H2 (PH2O = 0.042 atm) was 0.38 eV.799 In contrast, at 1000 °C under dry and low PO2 conditions, the predominant conduction mechanism was by O2− ions; the apparent activation energy at this temperature was 0.75 eV. This higher activation energy was proposed to originate from the local distortion of the BO6 octahedra owing to the ionic mismatch between larger Y3+ (0.090 nm) and smaller Zr4+ (0.072 nm) as well as due to the tendency of the Y3+ ions to form a C-type (cubic) rare earth structure.
Temperature programmed desorption (TPD) is an alternative method that can be used to evaluate the proton conductivity of defective oxide perovskites. This method is based on the estimation of the proton concentration from the amount of evolved water vapour, where the mobility of protons can be predicted from the temperature of the maximal peak.784 The TPD method is a diffusion-controlled technique, so the thickness of the sample affects these data. Hibino et al.800 reported an in-depth analysis of the relationship between the TPD peak temperature and the sample thickness, confirming that TPD data can be applied for the quantitative analysis of proton conductivity.


8.4.3. Computational calculations. 
Based on the conductivity values obtained through experimental studies, extensive computational calculations aiming to incorporate defect thermodynamics and defect diffusion coefficients under equilibrium and nonequilibrium conditions have been done. The Nernst–Planck–Poisson (NPP) approach initially was implemented computationally by Cohen and Cooley.801 The NPP equation generally is used to determine defect transport fluxes, including diffusion (concentration polarisation) and migration (electrostatic potential gradient). The defects are protons , oxygen vacancies , delocalised small polarons , electrons (e−), and/or holes (h˙). In order to capture fully the transport of these charged defects, the model formulates and solves coupled defect-conservation equations and reactions for all of the mobile charged species, with the defect fluxes being represented using the NPP equations.
In oxide perovskite proton conducting membranes, the computational approach involves the assumption that the defective perovskites at the membrane surfaces are in equilibrium with the gas phase, thus establishing boundary conditions for the transport equations.801 Internal electric potential gradients are formed within the membrane in order to maintain electroneutrality. Thus, the defect fluxes are affected by both concentration polarisation and local electric fields. The membrane performance depends on physical parameters, including defect mobility and equilibrium constants for the gas/surface defect reactions. The steady-state one-dimensional system of governing equations forms an ordinary-differential-equation boundary-value problem, which is solved computationally using finite-volume discretisation. The NPP process then is formulated in terms of mass and charge conservation equations.
Kee et al.790 simulated defect concentrations and conductivity of dense defective Ba(Y0.2Zr0.8)O3−δ electrolyte membranes using NPP models, both with and without trapping of O-site small polarons . Fig. 52 shows the flux variations of defects and gases in a tube configuration with a fixed gas composition of 98 mol% H2, 2 mol% H2O, and trace O2 (10−6 mol%) inside the tube, while the gas outside the tube was comprised of Ar, trace H2O (10−6 mol%), and O2 of variable concentrations of 1 × 10−5, 1 × 101, and 2 × 101 mol%, as shown by the respective blue, green, and red colour coding. Fig. 52a illustrates both positive (radially outward) and negative (radially inward) calculated fluxes of the potential defects and gases through the tube wall at 750 °C. These data indicate that the proton flux  is positive and increases significantly with increasing [O2] outside the tube, increasing from ∼0.7 to ∼1.4 mol S−1 m−2 for [O2] increasing from 1 × 10−5 to 1 × 101 mol%. Simultaneously, both h˙ and  fluxes were calculated to be negative. Also, the trend in the h˙ flux is proportional to the [O2]. The small  flux at 1 × 10−5 mol% O2 disappeared entirely at higher [O2]. Similar to , both H2 and H2O have positive fluxes and the former increases with increasing [O2].
 Fig. 52  Predictions of (a) defect and gas fluxes inside the tube, (b) defect concentration and electrostatic potential as a function of tube radius, and (c) defect and gas fluxes outside the tube (positive flux = radially outward; negative flux = radially inward; OH˙ concentrations for 1 and 2 × 101 mol% overlap) (reproduced from ref. 790 with permission from the Electrochemical Society). 
Further, the calculated radial concentration profiles and electrostatic potentials of the defects for gas equilibrium inside and outside the tube are shown in Fig. 52b. These data show that (with two exceptions), with increasing outward distance through the tube wall, the  decreases significantly, the [h˙] increases very significantly, and the  decreases slightly. The two exceptions are the  at the lowest [O2], which shows little change, and the [h˙], which shows a slight increase at the lowest [O2]. For proton conductivity by diffusion, the key parameters are the  and the . This study show that, while the [O2] has little effect on the , its contribution is small. In contrast, the [O2] has a significant effect on the . Further, when the  diffusion (via defect polarisation) is insignificant, the migration (via electrostatic potential) becomes the dominant mechanism in proton conductivity. Consequently, these data allow the system design to be engineered to leverage the optimal proton conductivity from both mechanisms. That is, the conditions to achieve a requisite high defect concentration and a low electrostatic potential are determined by the relative scales and slopes of the curves. So the approximately constant slope for the  curve under vacuum (1 × 10−5 mol% O2) indicates that diffusion is minimal, which means that the proton conductivity depends almost solely on the migration. Fig. 52a shows that the  flux is low, so vacuum conditions are undesirable. In contrast, going to positive-pressure conditions (1 and 2 × 101 mol% O2) has a significant effect on the , although there is little difference between the two [O2]. In this case, the greater slopes of the  curves relative to those of the electrostatic potential curves indicate that diffusion has the dominant effect. Since Fig. 52a shows that the flux is greatest for positive-pressure conditions, it can be concluded that diffusion is the desirable mechanism and, since this depends on the , optimising these represents a key engineering strategy. Additionally, Fig. 52b illustrates the potential to engineer a wall thickness that is commensurate with the maximum in the combined diffusional and migrational proton conductivity. A similar consideration is likely to be the case for the [O2], although the nature of the interpolation is unknown.
Concerning Fig. 52c (outside the tube), the similarity in trends to those in Fig. 52a (inside the tube) for all of the defect fluxes (, , h˙) reflects the relatively low thickness of the tube wall relative to its inner radius (i.e., 20 μm vs. 1 cm). These data allow the conclusion that the computational results for the tubular configuration should be essentially the same for a planar configuration.
Other researchers have applied experimental ionic and electronic conductivity in extensive computational calculations in order to generate defect thermodynamics and defect diffusion coefficients. Zhu et al.795 predicted the concentrations and conductivity of defects in dense defective Ba(Y0.2Zr0.8)O3−δ electrolyte membranes using NPP models, with and without the trapping of O-site small polarons. Fig. 53a and b show equilibrium defect concentrations as a function of temperature, and Fig. 53c shows the variations for defect conductivity relative to temperature.
 Fig. 53  Predicted equilibrium defect concentrations, electrical conductivity, and transference numbers as a function of temperature, with (solid lines) and without (dashed lines) polaron trapping (gas phase = 80.0 mol% O2 and 20.0 mol% H2O) (reproduced from ref. 795 with permission from the Electrochemical Society). 
The polaron trapping weakly affects the  and  but decreases the untrapped polaron concentration  by approximately four orders of magnitude. As the temperature increases, the proton concentration  decreases while the oxygen vacancy concentration  increases. Fig. 53b shows that increasing temperature decreases the trapped polaron concentration  and increases the untrapped O-site polaron concentration . Fig. 53c illustrates that the immobilisation of the polarons has a negligible effect on each defect's partial conductivity. These data further show that increasing temperature would enhance the defect conductivity; this enhancement is more significant for the polaron defects than the proton and oxygen vacancy defects.

Fig. 53d compares the defect transference numbers. At 500 °C, the polaron contribution to the total conductivity is ∼35.0% but it increases to 57.0% at 600 °C and to 72.8% at 700 °C. As discussed subsequently, the increasing contribution of the O-site polaron  conductivity acts to reduce significantly the faradaic efficiency of the proton transport in electrolysis cells.795
The concentration of untrapped O-site polarons, which is the electronic leakage, significantly influences potential membrane or fuel-cell applications. In another study by Zhu et al.,802 these authors incorporated the experimental conductivity into NPP models in order to calculate the faradaic efficiency. In protonic-ceramic fuel cells and electrolysers, the latter parameter represents the proton conducting effectiveness and is the ratio of the H2 production flux against the imposed electric current density. The results suggested that increasing electronic leakage caused by increasing working temperature significantly reduced the faradaic efficiencies of both fuel cell and electrolyser.
As mentioned previously, another approach to computation is the incorporation of experimental data on lattice strain and structural distortion resulting from defect formation. Taghikhani et al.792 developed an electro-chemo-mechanical computational model based on an extended NPP formulation to predict transient defect concentration profiles and stress states as a function of electrical current density in a thin (10 μm) dense Ba(Y0.1Zr0.9)O3−δ perovskite supported by a thick porous Ni/Ba(Y0.2Zr0.8)O3−δ composite electrode.792 In this work, all three charged defects (protons, O-site polarons, oxygen vacancies) were considered to be incorporated through equilibrated defect-incorporation reactions.
In contrast, another simulation approach to model defect transport through the protonic conducting membranes is the integral formulation method, where the defect fluxes are evaluated analytically based on experimental thermodynamic data and gas-phase compositions. In contrast to the NPP method, which requires the computational solution of boundary-value problems, the integral formulation method relies on the assumption of electroneutrality. Vøllestad et al.803 reported a comparative study of the two simulation methods to calculate defect fluxes in mixed-conduction materials. The results revealed that both models deliver quantitatively similar results in some conditions. However, in cases where there is exposure to large and/or multiple partial-pressure gradients, the predicted fluxes differ. It was concluded that the deviations originate from the different assumptions of the physical properties and mathematical simplifications of the two models.


8.4.4. Defective perovskites for protonic conducting fuel cells (PCFCs). 
Protonic conducting perovskites are suitable for applications where hydrogen transport is of high priority. Oxide perovskites with high proton conductivity and low electronic conductivity are advantageous as electrolytes in solid oxide fuel cells,804 while materials with mixed ionic and electronic conductivity are potential membrane materials for the separation of hydrogen from gas mixtures.
The perovskite Ba(Y1−xZrx)O3−δ possesses high, medium, and low values of proton, oxygen-ion, and electronic conductivity, respectively. However, multiple B-site substitutions with transition metals (e.g., Co and Fe) significantly enhance the electronic conductivity while maintaining the ionic conductivity. Duan et al.805 developed a protonic ceramic fuel cell (PCFC) cathode by multiple substitutions (Co, Fe, and Y) at the Zr sites of BaZrO3. The stoichiometry of Ba(Co0.4Fe0.4Y0.1Zr0.1)O3−δ exhibited an outstanding power density of 1.4 W cm−2 in CH4 at a very low temperature of 350 °C. This material offers a new generation of PCFC cathodes with triple conducting properties with the advantage of instantaneous proton, oxygen-ion, and electron–hole conductivity. The Ba(Co0.4Fe0.4Zr0.1Y0.1)O3−δ structure successfully eliminated the triple-phase boundary constraints associated with conventional composites, where either electron conductivity or mixed oxygen/proton ion conductivity was feasible for cathodes. While this operating temperature is significantly lower than the >700 °C reported for the best Ba(Y1−xZrx)O3−δ type materials, it was also reported subsequently to exhibit long-term durability (<1.5% reduction after >1000 h).806

9. Summary and outlook
9.1. Summary
The present work surveys five broad themes that are focussed on oxide perovskites. These materials are of considerable interest owing to their applications in many technological areas, particularly catalysis and energy storage. This review is distinguished by its multidisciplinary approach that integrates different technical fields with the aim of being more than the sum of its parts. In this sense, it attempts to blend both fundamental and applied information in order to elucidate a number of mechanisms that may not be immediately familiar to the intended readership. These five broad themes are
• Approach: integration of chemistry and materials science of defect-related concepts and strategies.
• Science: fundamental and applied aspects of defect equilibria in oxide perovskites.
• Mechanisms: strategies to engineer the generation and control of defects in oxide perovskites.
• Applications: thermocatalysis, electrocatalysis, photocatalysis, and energy storage using oxide perovskites.
• Background: survey of experimental and simulation studies of defective oxide perovskite synthesis and performance.
In regard to the mechanisms that may be less familiar to readers, extensive examination of the published literature has uncovered a number of key technical issues that appear to be subject to unverified assumption, misunderstanding, and/or gaps in knowledge. Some of these are related to preconceptions that may not be justified. Some are related to information that is well known to one field but much less so to another. Some are related to the scientific approach toward the technology itself. These problematic areas for which greater advantages can be leveraged are:
• Doping: advantage of the use of more informed approaches (size, valence, structural, and chemical) leading to a better grasp of the probabilities and outcomes of the mechanisms and extent of solid solubility.
• Lattice sites: advantage of the clarification and confirmation of the nature of site filling and attained stoichiometry (hypo, equilibrium, and hyper) associated with intended A-, B-, and oxygen-site substitution.
• Defects: advantage of the implementation of the rationale for the design, imposition, modification, and control of defect types, concentrations, and specific function, which may be contradictory for different catalytic pathways and applications.
• Charge: advantage of the utilisation of experimental data and defect equilibria in order to assess and interpret mechanisms of charge compensation and charge transfer.
9.2. Outlook
As in any field, the future is broad. This is particularly the case for oxide perovskites as there are several thousand of these from which to choose. The potential to modify these through defect engineering extrapolates this number literally to infinity. However, the design, fabrication, modification, and application of these materials present many challenges, and their resolution depends significantly on the potential to harness existing technologies for synthesis, characterisation, and simulation and to develop new ones. The present work suggests that the trajectory for perovskites is likely to focus on their continued development as catalysts and energy-storage devices that potentially may benefit from implementation of the following strategies:
• Defects: integration of experimental data, advanced analytical techniques, and simulations of defective oxide perovskites and associated defect equilibria.
• Synthesis: development of techniques that lead to thermodynamic equilibration and the achievement of steady-state performance.
• Characterisation: development of techniques for in situ characterisation and testing in service.
• Optimisation: control and maximisation of appropriate types and concentrations of defects as active sites
• Reliability: structural and microstructural stabilisation of surfaces and local defects.
• Surface vs. bulk: development of techniques for surface engineering that are decoupled from those used for bulk processing.

Table Content:

Hamidreza Arandiyan
	Hamidreza Arandiyan received his PhD from Tsinghua University in 2014. He was awarded a Vice-Chancellor's Research Fellowship in the School of Chemical Engineering from UNSW Sydney in 2015. He was awarded a University of Sydney Senior Fellowship in the School of Chemistry in 2018. Currently, he is a Senior Research Fellow in the Applied Chemistry & Environmental Science at RMIT University, Melbourne. His research interests lie in heterogeneous catalysis for environmental remediation and energy applications.

Sajjad S. Mofarah
	Sajjad S. Mofarah obtained his PhD in Materials Science and Engineering, UNSW Sydney, in 2020, with a focus on the defect structures of functional oxide materials for energy and environmental applications. He was appointed as a Research Associate at the same institution in 2019, expanding his work on advanced characterisation of 2D heterojunction materials based on ultrathin metal-based coordination polymers for catalysis and energy storage. Since April 2020, he has been a Research Associate studying the cyclic reprocessing of battery materials. His research interests include layered oxides, defect creation and characterisation, batteries and supercapacitors, and related thermodynamics.

Charles C. Sorrell
	Charles C. Sorrell has been the Professor of Ceramic Engineering at the University of New South Wales (UNSW) Sydney since 1987. He obtained his BSc degrees in Ceramic Engineering (1977) and Chemistry (1980) from the University of Missouri-Rolla, his MSc degree in Ceramic Science (1980) from the Pennsylvania State University, and his PhD degree in Ceramic Engineering (1987) from the University of New South Wales. His principal research foci are ceramic processing and phase equilibria, which have been applied across many areas. At present, his main work is on catalysis, biomaterials, geopolymers, and waste utilisation.

Esmail Doustkhah
	Esmail Doustkhah received his PhD in 2017. During his PhD studies, he was awarded several prizes, scholarships and internships, including visiting research position and an internship at the National Institute for Materials Science (NIMS), Japan. In 2018, he was accepted as a post-doctoral researcher at NIMS, working on mesoporous and 2D materials. In late 2019, he was awarded the JSPS fellowship in Japan. His current research interests are focused on the development of new porous and layered materials and studying their (photo)(bio)catalytic features.

Yuan Wang
	Yuan (Helena) Wang received her PhD degree from the School of Chemical Engineering, UNSW Sydney in 2018. She was appointed as a Research Assistant at the National Research Center for Geoanalysis, Chinese Academy of Geological Science in 2013. She was a Postdoctoral Research Associate in the School of Chemistry, UNSW Sydney. Currently, she is a Research Fellow in the Applied Chemistry & Environmental Science at RMIT University, Melbourne. Her research interests focus on the development and characterisation of nanoporous materials for energy-related applications.

Zongping Shao
	Zongping Shao is a Professor of Chemical Engineering at Curtin University, Australia, and Nanjing Tech University, China. He obtained his PhD from Dalian Institute of Chemical Physics, China, in 2000. He worked as a Visiting Scholar at the Institut de Researches sur la Catalyse, CNRS, France, and as a Postdoctoral Research Fellow at the California Institute of Technology, USA, from 2000 to 2005. His research interests include solid oxide fuel cells, lithium-ion batteries, supercapacitors, and low-temperature energy-conversion devices. He has been recognised as a Highly Cited Researcher in the Energy section by Elsevier since 2015. He has also been selected as a World Highly Cited Researcher by (Thomson Reuters) Clairvate Analytics for the year, 2014, 2017, 2018, and 2019.

Thomas Maschmeyer
	Thomas Maschmeyer is a Professor of Chemistry at the University of Sydney, co-founder of three companies Avantium (2000), Licella (2006) and Gelion (2015) and inventor of the Cat-HTRTM recycling and the ENDURE battery technology. He is Fellow of four academies, serves on the boards of six international journals and has received many awards, including most recently the David Craig Medal from Australian Academy of Science (2021), Prime Minister's Prize for Innovation (2020), the Federation of Asian Chemical Societies’ Contribution to Economic Development Award (2019) and the Eureka Prize for Leadership in Innovation and Science (2018).

	Neutral (Ba2+)	None	(Sr1−x2+Bax2+)TiO3

	Donor (V5+)	Ti vacancies	Sr(Ti1−y4+□¼(1−y)Vy5+)O3

	Donor (V5+)	O interstitials	Sr(Ti1−y4+Vy5+)O3+½y

	Donor (V5+)	Ti4+ → Ti3+ reduction	



	Neutral (Zr4+)	None	Sr(Ti1−y4+Zry4+)TiO3

	Donors (Al3+ & V5+)	Sr & Ti vacancies	(Sr1−x2+□½(1−x)Alx3+)(Ti1−y4+□¼(1−y)Vx5+)TiO3

 	Fig. 12  Imaging limitations of characterisation techniques applied to defects.	 

High-angle annular dark-field scanning transmission electron microscopy (HAADF-STEM)	Dopants, vacancies, surface defects, edge dislocations	Local composition, surface morphology, electronic structure	
277

Electron energy loss spectroscopy (EELS)	Vacancies, surface defects, grain boundaries, surface reconstruction, lattice disorder	Local composition, defect structure	
278

Energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS)	Dopants, voids, edge dislocations, planar defects	Direct correlation of image and spectroscopic data, composition and distribution of dopants	
278

Atomic force microscopy (AFM), scanning force microscopy (SFM)	Surface point defects, antiphase boundaries, grain boundaries, volume defects	Topographical imaging, surface atomic defects, mineralogical analysis	
233 and 279

Atomic electron tomography (AET)	Point defects, twin boundaries, edge dislocations, screw dislocations, surface reconstruction	Atomic displacements, 3D image reconstruction, 3D structural determination of crystal defects	
280

Atomic-resolution spherical-aberration-corrected transmission electron microscopy (ACTEM)	Point defects	Resolution of individual atoms (spatial resolution <0.05 nm)	
241 and 242

Scanning tunneling microscopy (STM)	Dopants, surface defects, Schottky pairs, Frenkel defects	Local defect structure, defect mobility, planar surfaces	
235 and 236

High-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM)	Dopants, vacancies, voids, linear defects, planar defects, lattice disorder	Surface morphology, defect types and concentrations	
169

X-Ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS), near ambient pressure XPS (NAPXPS)	Dopants, surface defects, other point defects, intrinsic defects, lattice disorder	Elemental composition of surface and near-surface region, valences	
246

X-Ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS), X-ray absorption near-edge structure (XANES), extended X-ray absorption fine structure (EXAFS)	Oxygen vacancies	Local composition, local arrangements of defects, coordination number, bond length and angle	
261–263

Dilatometry	Vacancies, other point defects, dislocations	Macroscopic temperature- and defect-induced size changes	
281

Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA)	Oxygen vacancies	Defect concentration from mass change upon heating	
282

Optical absorption spectroscopy	Point defects	Spatial distribution of point defects, electronic structure, defect states	
283

Secondary ion mass spectrometry (SIMS)	Oxygen vacancies	Determination of particle, isotope, and molecular compositions	
284

Positron annihilation lifetime spectroscopy (PALS)	Vacancies, vacancy clusters, other point defects, dislocations	Defect types and concentrations	
253, 254 and 285

Electron paramagnetic resonance spectroscopy (EPR) or electron spin resonance spectroscopy (ESR)	Oxygen vacancies, other point defects	Unpaired electrons, valences, defect types and concentrations	
250

Laser Raman microspectroscopy	Oxygen vacancies, disordered surfaces	Mineralogy, disordered structures, defect types and concentrations	
271

Inductively coupled plasma optical emission spectrometry (ICP-OES), optical absorption spectrometry (ICP-AES), and mass spectrometry (ICP-MS)	Dopants	Quantification analysis of dopants	
286

 	Fig. 43  Schematic of the principle of an Li–air battery, with perovskite as the OER catalyst.	 

Nonaqueous (aprotic) electrolyte	Oxygen reduction reaction, discharge at cathode (ORR)	Li+ + e− + O2 → LiO2 (E0 = 3.0 V vs. Li/Li+)	Reaction (1)
2LiO2 → Li2O2 + O2a (chemical reaction)	Reaction (2a)
LiO2 + Li+ + e− → Li2O2a (E0 = 2.96 V vs. Li/Li+)	Reaction (2b)
Oxygen evolution reaction, charge at cathode (OER)	Li2O2(s) → 2Li+ + 2e− + O2b (two-electron process)	Reaction (3)
Li2O2(s) → Li+ + e− + LiO2b (one-electron process)	Reaction (4)
Aqueous electrolyte	Oxygen reduction reaction, discharge at cathode (ORR)	4Li + O2 + 2H2O → 4LiOH (base electrolyte)	Reaction (5)
4Li + O2 + 4H+ → 4LiOH + 2H2O (acid electrolyte)	Reaction (6)
Oxygen evolution reaction, charge at cathode (OER)	4(LiOH + H2O) → 4Li + 6H2O + O2	Reaction (7)
